==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This master thesis addresses the required components for a multi-agent system with the
purpose of surveillance and mapping of critical infrastructure in marine environments.
First, a modular sensor fusion payload for an Unmanned Surface Vehicle was designed and
built. This payload was designed with the explicit purpose of acquiring real-world marine
datasets in collaboration with an ROV equipped with a UScanner, an inertial stereo-
camera data-capture device. Two major data acquisition campaigns were conducted, with
the second resulting in an applicable dataset for fostering further development towards a
multi-agent marine system, with the purpose of mapping and surveillance. With a stereo
SONAR configuration accompanied by a LiDAR, a seabed-to-sky map has been created.
A 3D object detection architecture, primarily evaluated on synthetic scenes, have been
proposed as well as an initial photogrammetry pipeline.
Additionally, using a visual-
inertial odometry algorithm, the ROV has been given localization capabilities, tested
successfully in a controlled environment. Finally combining the efforts, a theoretical multi-
agent system utilizing collaborative SLAM for autonomous mapping has been proposed.
Keywords: Robotics, Multi-agent, LiDAR, SONAR, VIO, C-SLAM, Object detection,
IEKF, GNSS-denied, Acoustic localization, Marine, Seabed-to-sky, Acoustic mapping
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

the implementation and theoretical foundations of methods used.

1.1
The two different C-SLAM paradigms. (a) shows the centralized approach
where one machine performs the global map and trajectory estimate. (b)
illustrates the decentralized approach, where the local map is globally con-
sistent between robots. The illustration is taken directly from [19]. . . . . .

The USV consist of 5 different sensors, two of which are SONARs, one
LiDAR, an IMU, and at last a GNSS receiver. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

The connectivity diagram of the sensor fusion platform. The colours indi-

2.4
The Sensor Fusion Payload on day of deployment. Coloring is only for visual
clarity. 1) SSD. 2) RPis for Data Logger and the two sonars. 3) NVIDIA
Jetson and the associated DC/DC adapter. 4) Physical switches for the
sensors. 5) Ouster Breakout Board. 6) Blueprint Oculus Connector. 7)
POE injector for Teledyne Blueview Sonar. 8) Otter Ethernet Connector.
9) 48V out DC/DC converter. 10) Unmanaged PoE+ switch. 11) 24V out
DC/DC converter. 12) Unmanaged Ethernet switch. 13) AC/DC converter.
14) Outgoing cables zip-tied to the wooden board in the payload. . . . . . .

2.6
The path of a single ping and how each element of the SONAR equation
comes to play.

Backscattering Strengt (BS), Target Area (TA), Noise Level (NL) and
Transmission Loss (TL). Inspired by [120].

3.1
The ROV has three different onboard sensor modalities; stereo camera, IMU
and a preassure sensor. For expreiments, an USBL transponder is present
for geolocalization. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
43
3.3
The full connectivity setup for the ROV at the time of deployment. The
BluROV laptop connects independently to the ROV and the UScanner,
despite the fact that they are deployed as one vehicle. This is to ensure
the modularity of the setup, making it possible to transfer the UScanner
to other ROV’s and types of vehicles. The BlueROV2 Heavy is controlled
with a Logitech Game controller. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
45

rial. The UScanner is mounted on the frame attached to the bottom, while
the buoyancy material is secured under the two holders at the sides with
screws. All sides are show in Appendix D. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .

3.6
The software setup implemented to save the data from the UScanner as well
as the BlueROV2 Hevy onto a database. On the UScanner, all data and
processes interface through ROS, and published in topics, which are logged
to a database via the bluerov_listener docker container. The ROV publishes
MavLink UDP messages to the laptop through the topside interface, which
is converted to ROS-topics using the mavros node and then saved.

5.6
a) shows a horizontal SONAR image without the open filter applied, where
the green scatter indicates the observed leading edge. Image b-d shows the
result of applying open filters of different kernel sizes. In b), it is clear that
less noise has been captured by the leading edge threshold. In c), the kernel
has been extended vertically, which doesn’t seem to affect the denoising. In
d), a taller kernel mitigates almost all noise, but it has heavily affected the
rest of the image, resulting in the leading edge ”bleeding” into the surface. .

==PAGE:19==
5.9
Conceptual diagram of the proposed method.
The input images are al-
ready pre-processed, following the steps presented in section 5.1.1. After
being transformed into Cartesian coordinates, a transformation to the hor-
izontal SONAR frame is performed. The images are then trimmed based
on intersecting areas, reducing processing time in the following steps. Fea-
tures are then extracted from each image and clustered. These clusters are
then matched based on the descriptors of the clusters. Features are then
matched between the two images, restricted by the assigned cluster pair.
The matched features are at last converted into 3D points in Cartesian space.102

SONAR (blue). Drawn in Fusion 360.

Nrc = 3 (blue and light blue). The figure is presented in steps to clarify
what is included in the individual average. Adapted from [35].

5.13 By comparing the position of two state nodes, the expected measurement
^zij. The error eij then depends on the displacement between this expected
measurement and the real measurement zij. The information matrix Ωij
accounts for the uncertainty of the measurement. Inspired by [164]. . . . . . 110

loop closure factor. Source: [107]. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 111

6.5
An illustration of how the dot product between a query qi and multiple
keys (k1, k2, k3, k4) produces alignment scores. By taking Softmax(⟨k2, qi⟩),
a distribution will be induced over the values, which effectively will select v2.138
6.6
Illustration of attention mechanisms. Left: Scaled Dot-Product Attention,
which computes attention scores using the dot product of queries (Q) and
keys (K), scales the result, applies a mask (optional), and normalizes with
a SoftMax function before weighting the values (V ). Right: Multi-Head
Attention, which extends the scaled dot-product attention by applying
multiple attention mechanisms in parallel (each with its own set of lin-
ear projections) and concatenating their outputs, followed by a final linear
transformation. Figure is from: [177].
. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 138
6.7
(Left) The model processes an input point cloud and produces a set of 3D
bounding boxes in an end-to-end manner.
A Transformer encoder com-
putes per-point features through multiple layers of self-attention. These
features, together with learnable query embeddings, are then passed to a
Transformer decoder that outputs bounding box predictions. A set-based
matching loss aligns the predicted boxes with their ground-truth counter-
parts. Color information is shown only for visualization and is not used by
the model. (Right) A random subset of points is sampled as “query” points,
which are embedded and subsequently decoded into the final bounding box
predictions. Figure is from: [87].
. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 140
6.8
The architecture of a single encoder and decoder layer in 3DETR. The en-
coder layer takes N′ × d input point features and produces N′ × d output
features by applying self-attention followed by a Feedforward Neural Net-
work (FNN). The decoder layer takes as input B × d query embeddings (or
outputs from a previous decoder layer) and N′ × d encoded features, pro-
ducing B × d outputs corresponding to predictions for B bounding boxes.
The decoder performs self-attention on the query embeddings, followed by
cross-attention between these embeddings and the encoder’s output fea-
tures. F indicates Fourier positional encodings used in the decoder, and
the feature dimension d is set to 256. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 141
6.9
Training loss curve over the course of 350 epochs. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 144
6.10 Example of baseline clustering for a scene of synthetic canal data. . . . . . . 145

6.12 Cross attention weights aggregated from all heads for an object query point
(red).

7.2
The employed image pyramid with a down-sampling factor of 3, for a patch
size of 8×8, giving the multilevel photometric patch (green). Here is shown
with an example image from the pool environment in ASTA. As the image
is downsampled, the 8 × 8 pixel-wide patch covers progressively more of
the image, capturing local information at a larger scale. This results in a
multilevel patch descriptor, where the information immediately around the
tracked landmark retains the highest level of detail. Figure inspired by [60]. 156
7.3
Calculating the photometric error for a tracked landmark for one level of the
multilevel patch. The blue arrow indicates that the visual-inertial system
moves, and the passage of one timestep. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 157
7.4
The calculation of the photometric error. The patch Pl in the image pyra-
mid at level l from the prior timestep is predicted at location p in the image
Il for the current timestep. The corresponding warped patch is extracted
from the image, where D maps pixel coordinates to image coordinates. pj
denotes a pixel coordinate of the patch, and sl is the scaling factor account-
ing for the downsampling. Figure inspired by [60].
. . . . . . . . . . . . . . 158
7.5
Demonstrating the FAST corner detector, with a circle of 16 pixels around
the selected pixel candidate. The candidate is classified as a corner if there
are n contiguous pixels among the 16, with an intensity outside the limits
given by threshold Ip ± t. Fulfilling this criterion gives a semicircle of pixel
values either all brighter or darker than the candidate, indicating a corner. . 159
7.6
The IMU-fixed frame I in relation to the inertial world frame W, at the
time of initialization for ROVIO. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 161
7.7
The computational loop for the ROVIO algorithm, based on the IEKF. The
prediction step, the per landmark update step, and the landmark manage-
ment step are all indicated by their designated colors. Each block of the
algorithm has a given number, marked with red, showing the order in which
it is executed in the loop. Note that due to the iterative nature of the IEKF,
the internal loop in the per landmark update step runs multiple times. Fig-
ure inspired by [60].

8.1
ROV SLAM block, where the Photogrammetry part will be covered in this
chapter. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 197
8.2
Depth estimation pipeline from stereo images, involving undistortion, rec-
tification, and stereo matching, or an alternative approach using Depth
Anything v2, resulting in an RGB-D image. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 197
8.3
Overview of RT‑DETR. Multi-scale features from the backbone’s last three
stages are fed into an eﬀicient hybrid encoder, which converts them into a
sequential representation using Attention‑based Intra‑scale Feature Inter-
action (AIFI) and CNN‑based Cross‑scale Feature Fusion (CCFF). Next,
the uncertainty‑minimal query selection mechanism chooses a fixed set of
encoder features as initial object queries. Finally, a transformer decoder
with auxiliary prediction heads iteratively refines these queries to produce
the final category labels and bounding boxes. Figure is from [203]. . . . . . 198
8.4
The fusion block in CCFF. Figure is from [203]. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 198
8.5
Histogram showing the number of training and test instances of each class.
199
8.6
SAM employs a heavyweight image encoder to generate an embedding that
is eﬀiciently queried with various prompts, yielding object masks in near
real-time. For ambiguous prompts involving multiple objects, SAM pro-
duces several valid masks with corresponding confidence scores. Figure is
from: [209]. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 200
8.7
Figure of a rectified stereo image setup. Figure is from: [210]. . . . . . . . . 203
8.8
Solid lines indicate the flow of labeled images, whereas dotted lines denote
the flow of unlabeled images. The figure highlights the significance of large-
scale unlabeled datasets.
The label S signifies the application of strong
perturbations. To enrich the depth estimation model with robust semantic
priors, an auxiliary constraint is introduced between the online student
model and a frozen encoder, thereby preserving semantic capability. Figure
is from: [211]. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 204

9.1
This chapter addresses the Collaborative SLAM integration of the Multi-
agent system. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 211
9.2
The proposed system that has been further expanded from Figure 1.3. The
decentralized approach adds a global optimization aspect to each robot,
marked as C-SLAM in the figure, which estimates the relative transforms
between the local SLAM graphs by sharing a common sparse message rep-
resentation through the shared message pool when communication is estab-
lished. The USV dictates the mission plan of the AUV by using automatic
object detection of the seabed map. The mission objective is then sent to
the AUV, which conducts path planning and performs an Inspection of a
given ROI. The full maps are shared with an external database. . . . . . . . 212
9.3
Illustration of the interaction between decentralized local SLAM processes
and the shared message pool. Reinterpreted and inspired by figure 3 from
[106]. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 215
9.4
A high-level overview of the method.
The local pose graph (gray area)
of each robot (different colored triangles) has intra robot loops. The pur-
ple area shows the global coordinate transformation graph, enabling map
transforms between the robots. Image from [106]. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 215
9.5
System diagram for DRACo-SLAM for a single AUV. The message pool
consists received scene descriptors. If regisition yields a inter-robot lopp
closure it is added to the pose graph and shared with team members. Image
from [116]. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 217

F.1
Opening and erosion filters with different kernels applied on the same image.
Using a simple erode operation tends to thin out the features of the surface,
risking that the edge ”bleeds” into the surface. However, it does not seem
to be a huge issue when the kernel is small (3 × 1). Using a too-large filter,
for instance, the 5 × 1 removes a lot of noise but is simply too aggressive
resulting in bad results for edge detection. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 295

Opening and erosion filters with different kernels applied on the same image.296

Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:29==
1
Introduction
The ocean has played a crucial role in the evolution of our species.
Indeed, ancient
civilizations such as Egypt, the Indus River Valley Civilization, China, and Mesopotamia
are all examples of hydraulic civilizations, where large-scale control over aquatic resources
was key, as it provided possibilities for irrigation and an important source of food [1].
During the Roman Empire, massive aqueducts were built to transport water from the
Alps to the cities and were some of the most impressive civil engineering projects of their
time[2]. Today; the ocean plays an important role as ever, being pivotal for global economic
and technological systems. About one-third of oil and gas that is extracted worldwide
comes from offshore sources, and there are currently around 900 large-scale offshore oil
and gas platforms around the world and tens of thousands of miles of submarine pipelines
in current operation[3].
Rising oil prices and surging demands for energy sustain the
economic viability of offshore extraction, keeping it a profitable business despite the high
inherent costs[4]. In addition to fossil fuels, investments in offshore energy are increasing
rapidly, with 2024 being the second-best year ever for the global offshore wind industry.
The current total global offshore wind capacity is more than 75 GW, and this number is
expected to grow to an impressive 487 GW by 2033[5], supporting the claim that offshore
wind farms will lead market growth due to their higher eﬀiciency and a larger number of
feasible installation sites than the land-based counterpart[6].
Energy, oil, and gas are not the only resources that are being transported across the
seafloor. In 1866, the SS Great Eastern laid the first permanent transatlantic telegraph
cable, and since then, the expanse of submarine communication cables has grown to more
than 850.000 miles[7]. These cables now transmit more than 95% of international internet
traﬀic, proving their invaluable role in keeping the world connected[8].
With the ocean continuing to support and drive human development, countries around the
world not only benefit from developing maritime infrastructure but are also increasingly
dependent on its protection and maintenance, as they are critical for the functioning
of society[9].
Emerging threats from state and non-state actors in the wake of rising
geopolitical tensions and the risks posed by extreme weather conditions caused by a rapidly
changing climate means that control and surveillance of these vital assets are increased
to a point short of war[10]. The vulnerability of maritime assets and the threats against
them have become starkly evident in very recent times, marked by a growing number
of incidents in the Baltic Sea. Within just the last three years, these have included the
explosions on the Nord Stream natural gas pipelines in September 2022, the damage to
the BCS East-West Interlink and C-Lion1 fiber-optic cables in November 2024, and most
recently, the disruption of the Sweden-Latvia submarine cable, also a fiber optic cable, in
January 2025.
In a 2023 article published in Marine Policy: The International Journal of Ocean Affairs,
Christian Bueger, Professor of International Relations at Copenhagen University and Di-
rector of SafeSeas — a network of academic institutions focused on maritime security
— and Tobias Liebetrau, a researcher at the Centre for Military Studies at Copenhagen
University, argue that current underwater surveillance in Europe is insuﬀicient. Current
systems, so-called ”Maritime Domain Awareness” or ”MDA” systems, are based on ship
routing and position from the Automatic Identification System (AIS), and augmented by
data from coastal radars, CCTV, and patrols, are mostly focused on the maritime surface
and the tracking of vessels from the fishing and transport/travel industries.
However,
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
1
==PAGE:30==
as the main threats might come from underwater vehicles, this approach to maritime
surveillance is problematic. Therefore, investments in underwater sensors and drones are
warranted to effectively extend surveillance to the subsea domain[11].
With autonomous marine technologies playing a critical role in safeguarding maritime
infrastructure, continued investment in their development is essential. However, the cur-
rent state of maritime robotics for surveillance and underwater mapping presents several
challenges due to the inherent constraints of Autonomous Surface Vehicle (ASV)s and
Autonomous Underwater Vehicle (AUV)s, as well as the limitations of available sensor
modalities.
Surface vehicles offer significant advantages in perception and navigation, such as LiDAR
and Global Navigation Satellite System (GNSS) for robust localization and mapping in
open waters [12] and GNSS-denied environments. Additionally, integrating acoustic sen-
sors enables bathymetric mapping, providing valuable topographical information about the
seafloor [13][14][15]. Despite these advantages, acoustic sensors have inherent limitations,
particularly in their ability to accurately classify objects due to resolution constraints and
noise interference.
For more detailed and close-range inspections of potential underwater threats, underwater
robots equipped with acoustic and visual sensors can be deployed, thereby eliminating
the need for human divers[16]. However, these Remotely Operated Vehicle (ROV)s and
AUVs face their own challenges, as underwater localization, where the absence of GNSS
requires alternative positioning methods such as acoustic or inertial navigation systems.
Additionally, surveying larger areas using underwater robots alone can be time-consuming
and resource-intensive.
To overcome these limitations, the deployment of both USVs and AUVs in a collabora-
tive setting presents a promising approach[17]. Leveraging the complementary capabilities
of both platforms to enhance the eﬀiciency and accuracy of seafloor mapping should be
explored further. Such efforts will require the development of advanced guidance and nav-
igation systems, and insights into collaborative Simultaneous Localization and Mapping
(SLAM) enable the level of reliability that is required in solving complex missions with
multiple agents [18].
The current state of collaborative autonomous vehicles for maritime surveillance is still in
development [19], and further advances are needed as this has become increasingly more
important in the current world situation. Therefore, we wish to address how collaboration
between two marine vehicles can be used to survey critical maritime infrastructures.
By working towards the following project objectives, this thesis aims to advance multi-
agent maritime systems with the purpose of mapping critical marine infrastructure.
• Develop a modular payload solution for a USV that allows for easily modifying the
onboard sensor suite.
• Acquisition of one or more real-world marine datasets, including localization data
along with acoustic and optical data from surveys using the USV and ROV.
• Generate ”seabed-to-sky” semantic maps that are cm-level accurate.
• Design a collaborative multi-agent system.
2
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:31==
1.1
Literature Review
A system involving both a USV and ROV is proposed to accommodate the project ob-
jectives. Before being able to map out the full system, a comprehensive literature review
covering different aspects of existing systems is conducted. First, the current approaches
in seabed-to-sky mapping are explored with a particular focus on USVs, and a more in-
depth review of acoustic mapping is presented. This is followed by a review of how Visual
Inertial Odometry (VIO) methods are used to enable robust navigation below the water
surface for ROVs. Subsequently, object detection is explored, as it is crucial for enabling
the automatic detection of potential threats and objects in the water. Lastly, the cur-
rent state-of-the-art in collaborative SLAM is investigated, as the collaboration of the two
robots is pivotal for the motivation of this project.
1.1.1
Seabed-to-Sky Mapping
The term ”seabed-to-sky” refers to a complete map representation of maritime environ-
ments. Utilizing features from both sub-environments has the potential to improve the
monitarization of maritime environments through the integration of various sensor tech-
nologies.
The utilization of Autonomous Surface Vehicle equipped with diverse sensor
suites has been pivotal in advancing this field, and the availability, advances, and price
reductions of LiDAR technology do not make a fusion of sensor modalities less relevant
[20][21].
One of the earlier publications on this topic, [22], proposed a novel seabed-to-sky map-
ping system using a compact ASV equipped with a 3D LiDAR and a Teledyne BlueView
Forward Looking Sonar (FLS). The paper proposes a method that can account for the
degradation of the GPS information when approaching bridges by leveraging the extero-
ceptive information captured by the LiDAR in combination with measured motion using
the IMU, through a Simultaneous Localization and Mapping (SLAM)-based approach.
Researchers from the University at Porto [23] highlight the importance of cyclical moni-
toring and inspection around offshore wind farm facilities, and proposes a similar solution
to the previous one, utilizing an ASV equipped with GPS, IMU, SONAR and LiDAR
to create a seabed-to-sky representation. Leveraging both GPS and IMU measurements
reduces the positional error of the USV down to 4 cm and can reconstruct a representation
of the environment in real time through simulation.
Two years later, the same group of researchers presented a modular, multi-domain aware
ASV designed for inspection tasks [14]. Their system is modular in the sense that their
hardware architecture is split into four different modules; energy, navigation, perception
and localization. The authors leverage Robot Operating System (ROS) to establish seam-
less communication between localization, perception, and navigation modules, enabling
motion control, obstacle avoidance, and way-point navigation in the navigation module,
motion and odometry estimation in the localization module, and multi-domain mapping
in the perception module.
In addition to these achievements, a major contribution is the publicly available multi-
domain maritime dataset captured on a single vehicle. This dataset supports applica-
tions in odometry estimation, navigation, and bathymetry survey [14]. Unfortunately, the
SONAR data provided in the dataset is only given as line scans, i.e., extracted 3D points,
which means other SONAR extraction methods cannot be tested on this data.
A newer publication, [12], focuses on the technical aspects of consistent mapping of marine
structures using ASVs, emphasizing the need for incorporating navigational sensors to
compensate for ocean disturbances, enabling inspection of an offshore power plant under
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
3
==PAGE:32==
harsh ocean disturbances. In contrast to the methods introduced above, this ROS based
system estimates its current state only using a GPS and an Attitude Heading Reference
System (AHRS)1 without integrating any information from the LiDAR and SONAR that
are solely used for mapping. For mapping, sub-map-based filtering techniques are applied
to enhance the accuracy and reliability of the mapping process [12].
[15] and [13] emphasize the importance of being able to localize the ASV when GNSS is
either partially or fully denied due to shadowing of buildings and obstacles or jamming.
Instead, a LiDAR based odometry is used for both mapping and localization through
SLAM based approaches. Both reports conduct an extensive data campaign in a Danish
canal, where tall buildings and bridges obstruct the GNSS signal. The two approaches
mainly differ in how SONAR and GNSS data is incorporated into the system. [15] solely
relies on the LiDAR data for localization, and adds the SONAR points to the map based
on these estimations. On the other hand, [13] includes the SONAR and GNSS data in the
pose estimation by treating the extracted SONAR points as LiDAR data. However, this
didn’t seem to improve localization compared to the very robust LiDAR localization.
A common tendency for seabed-to-sky mapping is the use of LiDAR and echosounder
technology or SONAR to perceive above and below the water surface, respectively. The
SONAR is mostly mounted in a downward-facing direction, performing bathymetry map-
ping. However, the field of seabed mapping has been explored beyond this technique,
which will be reviewed in the following section.
1.1.2
Acoustic Mapping
Acoustic sensing has emerged as a pivotal technology for mapping underwater environ-
ments.
It leverages sound waves to gather spatial information at impressive distances
that vision methods would not be able to achieve. Acoustic sensors have been in use for
decades, with some tracing the origins of the echo-sounding era back to the 1920s when
single-beam SONAR was employed in the search for the Titanic wreck [16]. However,
large-scale mapping of underwater environments such as marine habitats first started in
the late 1970s, and the technology has been evolving ever since, increasing the accuracy,
variety, and accessibility of SONAR systems [24].
In general, when classifying SONAR systems, there are two main categories: active and
passive SONAR, which signify whether or not the system is emitting sound pulses it-
self. Passive SONAR systems solely listen for sound pulses emitted by the environment,
thereby not emitting any signals themselves. These devices have been successfully used to
map seabed habitats by analyzing the spectral characteristics and time-domain evolution
[24]. However, passive SONARs cannot detect silent objects, making them unsuitable for
mapping quiet or very noisy environments or detecting stealth targets.
Active SONARs are widely used as their ability to emit and receive sound signals enables
them to accurately map the underwater environment by analyzing the echoes that are
bounced back from the surrounding environment by measuring the Time of Flight (ToF) of
the signals. Within active SONARs used for mapping, mainly 3 types appear: single-beam,
multibeam, and sidescan. The single-beam sonars are mostly mounted in a downward-
facing direction and are widely used on vessels. These types of sensors are simple to use
and are well suited for providing a good estimate of the depth and type of seabed that the
vessel is currently traversing. However, only utilizing a single beam limits their coverage
and their ability to accurately resolve vertical features and map irregular seafloor [24][16].
To increase the FOV of the scan, side-scan SONARs are used, which are composed of
1An IMU with integrated estimation of attitude and heading information.
4
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:33==
two transducer arrays deployed on each side towards the bottom of the ship, covering an
angle of up to 240◦and enabling mapping of irregular seafloor. The last type of active
SONARs that are covered in this literature review is the Multibeam Echosounder System
(MBES) (also referred to as multibeam imaging sonars or Forward Looking Sonar (FLS)
in the literature), which due to their manageable size and their ability to cover a wide
swath at high resolution [16] has become very attractive to use on smaller AUVs and USVs
[14][22][15][25][26][27].
As covered in Section 1.1.1, it is very common to use MBES for bathymetry mapping by
extracting the prominent features obtained in the scan. However, since the data obtained
in the 3-dimensional environment is squashed in the vertical axis due to the nature of
linear phase arrays, uncertainty will be introduced depending on the properties of the
SONAR and its mounting configuration [15]. Another key drawback of this utilization
of MBES is the loss of valuable information that could be extracted from the feature-
rich images. Two approaches have been adopted to address these limitations. The first
utilizes a single SONAR, positioned either facing forward or tilted downwards. The second
combines multiple SONARs, leveraging their different positional configurations for 3D
reconstruction.
The single SONAR solutions are mainly used for localization [28][29], object recognition,
object classification [30] and some approaches even extend into SLAM[31][29]. One ap-
proach to mapping is projecting the features extracted from the 2D SONAR image into a
bird-eye-view (BEV) 2D occupancy map. [32] explores underwater cluttered environments
using such map representation, as they leave enough features to perform SLAM.
[27] proposes a novel probabilistic method for 3D occupancy mapping, where an inverse
sensor model is designed to enable occupancy mapping by representing features, shadows,
and free space from an acoustic image into the occupied, unknown, and free categories of
an acoustic map. This approach has been further elaborated by placing it into a SLAM
context, creating 3D local maps [31]. However, introducing occupancy maps is expensive
for larger scenes, especially if finer resolution should be maintained. This shortcoming is
addressed in [33], where they present a neural network approach to perform 3D recon-
struction using a single SONAR.
Dual SONAR approaches aim to accurately reconstruct environments in 3D by leveraging
the introduction of a secondary SONAR to infer elevation information. This approach was
first presented in [34], where a FLS combined with a Profiling Sonar (PS)2 By having the
scan-line of the PS perpendicular to the FLS, geometrical constraints and the additional
sensor information are used to predict the elevation angle. This setup has been further
explored and validated in [25]. A similar approach uses two orthogonally aligned FLSs
[35]. In this approach, the 20◦of elevation that each SONAR provides in the intersecting
area is utilized to obtain a 3D reconstruction of the features captured by both SONARs.
This approach has been further expanded in [26], where a Bayesian approach is used to
infer the obtained 3D information onto objects outside of the intersecting FOV of the
dual setup under the assumption that classes of objects repeat within the environment.
Another group of researchers has employed a similar physical setup for target positioning
where their proposed filtering method, Threshold-Mask-Guided Filter, yields good results
on target estimation [36], further supporting the potential of a dual SONAR configuration.
2A mechanically rotating single beam SONAR.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
5
==PAGE:34==
1.1.3
Visual Inertial Odometry
To ensure robust navigation below the surface, the ROV must be equipped with a method
to accurately and reliably determine its position in the world coordinate frame over time
as it moves. A solution to this problem could be to equip the system with an IMU and
integrate over the linear accelerations and angular velocities. However, since all IMUs
exhibit some degree of noise and random walk in their measurements, the integration over
time means that the estimated position will eventually accumulate a large enough error
to render any such system insuﬀicient for a prolonged period of time.
Another method to determine the location is to outfit the object with an USBL-transponder,
which communicates acoustically with a vessel- or shore-mounted transceiver of which the
precise location in the world coordinate frame is known.
However, in addition to the
cost of purchasing and setting such a system, the USBL-system suffers from a number of
shortcomings that compromise its use as an accurate and robust localization device. First
of all, due to the short baseline between the transceivers, the system is sensitive to small
angular variations of the transceiver mounting[37]. Further primary factors influencing
the positional accuracy are errors in sound speed, the fact that acoustic signals degrade
with distance, and bending of the sound rays[38] due to temperature salinity and pressure.
In order to solve the problem of localization, another method is required: combining
the IMU with other exteroceptive sensors, such as a stereo camera setup, utilizing sensor-
fusion is advantageous. One such solution is called Visual Inertial Odometry (VIO), which
combines an IMU with one or more cameras to extract information from the environment.
To form a more complete understanding of VIO, it is relevant to first consider Visual
Odometry (VO).
A note on Odometry and SLAM
In the following part of the literature review, several odometry and SLAM algorithms
will be presented alongside each other as contributions to the field of VIO. This is due to
the fact that the SLAM algorithms presented rely on some implementation of VIO, and
are therefore still contributing to this field of research. The main advantage of SLAM
is the ability to enable loop closure information, allowing the error of the localization to
be bounded. For SLAM, this is achieved by maintaining some implementation of a map,
allowing for place recognition. Traditional VIO algorithms do not maintain such a map
and effectively dead reckon, as only the initial position is known with absolute certainty.
Visual Odometry
Visual odometry is a family of odometry algorithms that is exclusively vision-based and
relies only on the data input from one or more cameras. The concept of inferring the
traversed path from visual input was first proposed by Moravec in [39][40]. In this project,
a robotic rover, autonomously moving in steps of approximately one meter, was equipped
with a camera on a sliding rail that, for each step, took nine pictures of the obstacle
course. Using a primitive corner feature extractor in one image and then finding these
same features in the other frames, a ”camera solver” is able to determine the position of
the feature in 3D space. A path planning algorithm then plans a step in the course with
a high margin of safety to the features, and after this, the entire process is repeated[39].
This early implementation of a VO algorithm demonstrates one of the fundamental con-
cepts in VO and VIO, namely the identification and tracking of features in the image
plane as well as 3D space to infer the relative location of the robot over timesteps.
The next major developments in VO would come in the early 2000s, with several notable
papers being published. While Moravec had some success with a visual system 20 years
earlier, many odometry and SLAM systems relied mostly on laser, sonar, or a stereo cam-
6
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:35==
era setup used as a rangefinder[41]. Vision-based systems were, up until then, mostly
concerned with offline estimation of structure from motion, which included trajectory re-
covery, with real-time performance posing a much more diﬀicult problem at the time [42].
However, cameras becoming increasingly compact and affordable sparked an interest in
building vision-based odometry and SLAM systems. In 2003, the Oxford Robotics Re-
search Group presented a top-down Bayesian framework for localizing a single camera by
mapping a sparse set of natural features using motion modeling and an information-guided
active measurement strategy[42]. In this paper, using an extended Kalman filter archi-
tecture, leveraging active search for reliable image patches based on information content,
real-time performance was achieved, and they demonstrated robust localization within an
indoor scene. The group followed up on the work in 2004, where the single-camera SLAM
method in [42] was modified to use a wide-angle camera. This significantly improved per-
formance due to an increased movement range and a greater ability to track agile motion
[41]. Finally, in 2007, Davison et al. published the paper of one of the first well-known
visual SLAM algorithms; namely MonoSLAM by Davison et al[43][44]. This publication
built heavily upon the two earlier ones, i.e., using an extended Kalman filter framework
with a wide-angle camera, with the additional approximation that an extracted landmark,
an image patch, lies on a locally planar surface. This approximation allows the landmark
to change appearance from frame to frame[43].
Alongside these developments, many influential works were published in the same time
period. In 2004, Nister et al. published [45], which formally established the term ”Visual
Odometry” for that field of research, and presented an algorithm using a Harris Corner
detector for feature detection in image frames, and a geometric algorithm for detection the
pose of the camera after tracking detected features over a certain number of frames. One of
the most notable contributions of this algorithm is the ability to use a stereo camera setup,
greatly simplifying the estimation of the pose, as scale can be inferred from the baseline
between the cameras[45]. Additionally, Mouragnon and colleagues published two papers
in 2006, with the first implementing estimation of the camera position in real-time using
feature tracking, in addition to using selected keyframes and local bundle adjustment[46].
This algorithm was further refined in [47].
For the monocular camera setup, the main problem of using only visual input for retrieving
the trajectory is the lack of scale caused by the absence of reliable depth information. This
causes the error to accumulate over distance, and this led to the integration of the IMU
into VO systems, effectively giving rise to VIO.
Visual Inertial Odometry
One of the first publications to integrate visual inertial measurements was [48] in 2004.
Two algorithms for estimating motion from visual and inertial measurements are presented:
An offline batch method that estimates both the motion and structure of the scene using
the entirety of the measurements, and a recursive method employing an Iterated Ex-
tended Kalman Filter (IEKF) to estimate motion and scene structure sequentially[48].
Another early method, using simply the Extended Kalman Filter (EKF), was presented
by Anastasios I. Mourikis and Stergios I Roumeliotis in [49], where a measurement model
is introduced that can express geometric constraints when a feature is observed by multiple
cameras, thereby not requiring the 3D position of the feature in the state vector, greatly
improving computational complexity.
Indeed EKF based methods[50][51],[52] remain popular due to computational eﬀiciency[53].
While most approaches to EKF methods track corner features as these are easily identi-
fiable, some implementations use line-features [54][55][56], while others use plane-features
[57][58]. Another variation is to implement the EKF in a robocentric fashion, with re-
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
7
==PAGE:36==
spect to a moving local reference frame. In this manner, the robot can start in any initial
pose[59], and all landmarks are tracked in relation to the local reference frame[60][61].
In contrast to the filter-based approaches, batch optimization, or ”smoothing” methods
[62][63][64] solves the Bundle Adjustment (BA) problem, which is essentially a non-linear
least squares problem. Solving this problem over a set of captured measurements allows
for greater accuracy at the cost of computational complexity[53].
When it comes to fusing visual and inertial measurements, the approaches can be roughly
divided into ”loosely-coupled” and ”tightly-coupled”[53][44].
In loosely coupled sensor
fusion, [65][63], the visual and inertial measurements are processed separately, and then
the obtained motion constraints are fused. In the case of tightly-coupled sensor fusion,
[56][49][64][60], the visual and inertial measurements are fused directly, inducing a higher
computational cost but with increased accuracy in the estimation of the trajectory.
In extension of loosely and tightly coupled approaches to sensor fusion, the indirect and
direct approaches to implementing visual measurements should also be considered. Indi-
rect methods, [64][49][66], which are often considered the most classical, pre-process the
sensor measurements to generate an intermediate representation, often by the use of ex-
tracting and matching sparse image features such as keypoints, often corners, leading to
the formulation of a geometric error[53]. Direct methods, [67][61][60][68][69][70], consider
raw pixel intensities, either in terms of the entire image or selected patches[60].
This
allows for including much more information from the image and leads to the formulation
of a photometric error[53].
Implementing VIO in the undersea domain is challenging due to environmental constraints,
most notably poor visibility, low light, and a lack of features and texture[71][72]. In [73],
by Xu et.
al., an integrated VO system is proposed that implements optical flow to
track features between consecutive camera frames and tightly merges IMU andSONAR
measurements to track depth. This was found to work well in sparse feature spaces and
outperform state-of-the-art algorithms, as it was tested against OrbSLAM2, which failed
to initialize owing to the sparse nature of the environment. Continuing their work, Xu
et al. presented [74], where a monocular camera, once more using optical flow for feature
tracking, was tightly merged with the measurements of a dual IMU setup. ”ReAqROVIO”,
a method presented by Mohit Singh, Kostas Alexis and Mihir Dharmadhikari in [75][76],
integrates the estimation of the refractive index of the camera lens in water, as a state
variable of the VIO method Robust Visual Inertial Odometry (ROVIO) presented in [60]
by Bloesch et. al. With this, robust underwater odometry is achieved in conditions with
both adequate and low light. Indeed, the choice of ROVIO as VIO algorithm was due
to its robust performance in low light and low texture environments[76]. A method to
specifically combat the degradation in performance of VIO algorithms was presented by
Josi et al. in [71], where a dual estimator setup was utilized. When the performance of the
VIO algorithm degrades suﬀiciently, the position estimator switches to a model-based one,
integrating inertial information, water depth, flipper configuration, and velocity. Finally,
in [77], reprojection bundle adjustment with dynamic weight factors for the visual residuals
is utilized, while the IMU aids the depth measurements.
The ROVIO algorithm presented in [60] and [61] was chosen as the VIO algorithm for
the project, motivated by the success by Singh et al, in [75] and [76], attributing the
achieved robust underwater performance to ROVIO estimating well, also in low light
conditions. Another motivator was the fact that ROVIO not only tracks corner features
in consecutive images but uses a multi-level patch-based feature descriptor, which is able
to include local information around the tracked landmark. This allows for tracking of
8
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:37==
non-corner landmarks, greatly increasing the amount of trackable landmarks, improving
performance in feature sparse environments. Other methods such as ORB-SLAM3 and
DSO was considered, but these were either not tested underwater (or in the case of ORB-
SLAM2 on which ORB-SLAM3 is built shown to perform poorly[72]), or simply just not
publicly available, as was the case for [71].
1.1.4
Object Detection
The task of object detection is to find regions of interest ROI that contain an object’s
pose and/or category.
This approach is built on the hypothesis that different objects
have intrinsic attributes that can be captured via sensing techniques. Objects can thereby
be described as a combination of features, geometric positions, colours, etc. However,
due to the large variance in how the same object can be perceived, such as lighting,
orientation, different poses/scale, occlusion, etc., the problem has continuously posed a
diﬀicult question in the field of computer vision.
Point cloud 3D object detection has, in recent years, become an active research area
in the field of the 3D computer vision community.
These topics within research and
industry encompass mobile robots, autonomous driving, urban surveying, forestry, land
management, and many more.
Development in these areas is partly a result of more
readily accessible tools and techniques to extract 3D information of a given environment,
such as LiDAR or RBG-D methods[78].
Data Representations
A fundamental question in 3D perception is how best to represent raw geometric data for
eﬀicient analysis and interpretation. Over the years, multiple data representations have
been proposed, each capturing different aspects of the environment and offering distinct
advantages. The most intuitive is the point cloud representation, which is a collection
of individual points, each defined by a x, y, z coordinates that collectively describe a
3D scene in Cartesian space. Here, features like the structure, shape, and characteristics
are reflected by the distribution and density of the points. The challenges with working
directly with point clouds are potential noise in the data, which makes the extraction
of desirable features more diﬀicult. However, multiple filtering approaches[79] have been
developed, such as density outlier detection. Another challenge is the lack of connectivity
information inherent to a simple (x, y, z) representation.
This unordered and continuous representation can also impose computational issues. Here,
volumetric representations have been suggested that transform the 3D data into a regular
grid.
For this transformation, a point is encoded such that the spacial information is
reflecting a discrete volume in 3D space, resulting in the set {x, y, z}. Here, voxels and oc-
trees are two popular volumetric representations. Voxels add a binary value v to represent
if a given voxel is free space or occupied. In addition, occupied voxels can be categorized
as visible, occupied, or self-occluded. This encoding gives implicit information about the
viewpoint of the 3D scene. However, since voxels explicitly represent a scene’s occupied
and free space, a sparse representation is not possible, leading to a larger memory space
and computation needs.
An octree is a hierarchical data structure that recursively subdivides 3D space into eight
smaller regions, or octants. This adaptive approach allows for variable resolution, allo-
cating more detail where it’s needed while reducing complexity in less critical areas. By
storing only the non-empty nodes that contain relevant geometric information, octrees
significantly reduce memory requirements compared to dense voxel grids. Thereby, this
sparse representation enhances computational eﬀiciency. Octrees are particularly effective
at capturing the fine structural details of 3D shapes, providing richer local information
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
9
==PAGE:38==
than standard voxel methods.
Many other 3D representations have been proposed, such as 3D-to-2D projections, graph-
based models, meshes, multi-view approaches, and RGB-D data[79]. Each of these repre-
sentations presents its own unique advantages and disadvantages, which essentially boil
down to considerations like dense versus sparse data, local versus global features, and their
computational and spatial implications.
Traditional methods in 3D object detection often rely on handcrafted features. This refers
to features and descriptors that are manually engineered, such that it can capture vari-
ance in specific objects. These methods can be divided into global and local feature-based
methods; however, both rely on domain expertise and knowledge. Global methods encap-
sulate the entire object into a single feature vector, capturing all its characteristics. This
approach includes methods like spin images, Fourier descriptors, histograms of shape dis-
tributions, etc. [80]. Here, a widely adopted method was histogram-based methods that
generally either worked with calculating the distance between points or angle between vec-
tors. Through the comparison of these histograms, global shape structures can be isolated,
but much of the finer-grained details are lost through this method. This is especially a
problem when wanting to distinguish between different objects of similar global structure.
In addition, occluded areas or noise can skew the global features. The local feature ap-
proach instead focuses on distinctive features like corners, edges, and points. Doing this
makes the local approaches much more robust to changes in viewpoints, illumination, and
scale. However, similar features might coexist in different objects, introducing a degree of
ambiguity while deformation is a concern in general. Popular approaches include Speed
Up Robust Features (SURF)[81], Oriented FAST and Rotated BREIF (ORB)[82], and
Scale-Invariant Feature Transform (SIFT)[83]. Here, machine learning methods such as
k-nearest neighbours, decision trees, Random forest, and Support Vector Machines (SVM)
are used for classification and recognition tasks. However, these methods that rely on ex-
pert and domain knowledge have shown limitations in their scalability and generalization.
Something that more learning and data-driven approaches have tried to solve in more
recent years.
Broadly, most deep learning based methods can be placed in two camps: discriminative
(i.e., Convolutional Neural Networks) and generative (i.e., Variational Auto Encoders).
Here, the discriminative model learns a conditional probability distribution P(y|x) of an
output given an input, while the generative model creates a joint probability distribution
P(x, y) of an input-output pair. This thesis focuses mostly on supervised methods for
object detection. Supervised models learn a feature representation, given labeled data
that ideally generalizes to unseen input data. None of the models explored in this thesis
offer zero-shot capabilities, that is, the ability to classify or detect objects that have never
been seen during training. Consequently, the input environment must reflect the data
distribution seen during training.
Early learning-based methods working directly on raw, irregular point cloud data is Point-
Net[84]. The core idea behind PointNet is to process each point independently by applying
a shared FFN (feed forward neural network) to learn point-wise features, followed by a
max pooling to obtain permutation invariant global features. This architecture allows the
model to handle unstructured 3D data without the need for voxelization. The issue is that
since each point is treated largely in isolation before the pooling step, PointNet’s capa-
bilities to capture local features are lacking. To address this limitation, PointNet++[85]
introduced a hierarchical extension.
For object detection in 3D, the ability to model local context is especially important. While
10
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:39==
PointNet and PointNet++ were initially proposed for classification and segmentation,
subsequent works have adapted their core designs for detection tasks.
In many such
approaches, the learned point features provide a strong backbone, which can then be
combined with bounding-box regression heads to detect and localize objects in 3D space.
Another line of work in 3D deep learning includes continuous convolution-based ap-
proaches, which aim to adapt the concept of convolutions to unstructured point clouds
in a manner that does not depend on voxelization. For instance, SpiderCNN [86] defines
continuous convolutions by computing a convolutional kernel as a product of a learnable
function and a polynomial or sinusoidal kernel function in the local neighborhood around
each point. This allows the network to capture local geometric features without imposing
a rigid voxel grid.
While convolution-based methods adapt classical convolutional filters to continuous 3D
spaces, another promising direction has been the use of attention mechanisms via the
transformer architecture, particularly motivated by their success in 2D vision tasks and
natural language processing.
The inherent permutation invariance of the transformer
makes it suited for raw point cloud data, as it is unstructured and unordered.
Here,
attention-based mechanisms can aggregate information across all points without relying
on volatilization neighborhood-based methods. The 3D Detection Transformer (3DETR)
framework[87] utilizes the transformer in an end-to-end fashion for 3D object detection,
omitting any convolutional backbone but operating directly on raw point sets. By formu-
lating detection purely as a set-to-set prediction problem within a transformer, 3DETR
aligns conceptually with DETR (DEtection TRansformer)[88]. This approach is in con-
trast to other pipelines, such as two-stage detection frameworks like PointRCNN (Point
region-based convolutional neural network)[89] and PV-RCNN[90], or methods that com-
bine 3D feature extractors with 2D projections (e.g., MV3D[91]). VoteNet[92] uses a 3D
object detection pipeline that operates directly on sparse point clouds. Instead of relying
on region proposals or anchor-based mechanisms, VoteNet uses Hough voting to generate
object proposals. The method first learns point-wise features, often using a PointNet++
style backbone, and then aggregates votes from local regions to form bounding box pre-
dictions.
In this thesis, the 3DETR architecture will be further explored to investigate its capabilities
for 3D object detection in sparse underwater representations.
1.1.5
Collaborative SLAM
Collaborative SLAM or simply C-SLAM is the extension of ordinary SLAM to a multi-
agent system that enables the creation of a global shared map of the environment by
integrating the locally generated maps from each of the robots in the system. This col-
lective understanding of the environment gives rise to a shared situational awareness,
allowing each robot to localize reliably using information obtained across the system. One
of the main drawbacks and challenges of building C-SLAM systems is the increased com-
putational overhead. However, C-SLAM is a useful tool that has been proven to greatly
improve performance in mapping and localization applications, even when using only a
small team of robots[19].
One of the early theoretical formulations of C-SLAM was presented in the 2000 paper ”A
Probabilistic Approach to Collaborative Multi-Robot Localization”[93], wherein a sample-
based Markov localization is utilized to do on-demand localization of individual robots in
a team. Each robot maintains a belief function, which is updated whenever two robots
meet, allowing information to transfer between the robots. In their experiments, drastic
improvements in speed and accuracy for the mapping task were observed[93]. Using a
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
11
==PAGE:40==
probabilistic algorithm for the localization of multiple robots proved popular in early
research. Sebastian Thrun published the 2002 paper ”A Probabilistic Online Mapping
Algorithm for Teams of Mobile Robots”, in which a Monte Carlo localization algorithm was
used in conjunction with a maximum likelihood estimation of the map. In this algorithm,
each robot maintained a posterior over map, estimated locally on each robot[94]. Later
developments led to algorithms based on particle filters where [95][96] proposed methods
extending the Rao-Blackwellized particle filter localization method and extended it to two
or more robots.
An additional problem solved in these papers is the one of unknown
start positions: Relative poses were initialized for the robots that were updated during
rendezvous events, providing the base for the initialization of the filter[95][96]. Current
trends in the C-SLAM domain include methods for distributed pose graph optimization, as
presented in the 2021 paper ”Distributed Certifiably Correct Pose-Graph Optimization”
by Tian et al. Here, a sparse semidefinite relaxation is proved to provide optimal pose
graph solutions when moderate measurement noise is assumed[97].
Figure 1.1: The two different C-SLAM paradigms. (a) shows the centralized approach
where one machine performs the global map and trajectory estimate. (b) illustrates the
decentralized approach, where the local map is globally consistent between robots. The
illustration is taken directly from [19].
The creation of the shared map in C-SLAM can happen continuously by means of one
global algorithm running on a central processing unit, with the robots taking on the role as
essentially mobile sensor platforms. However, this problem can become infeasible to solve
due to both computational and communication constraints as the number of components
in the system grows[98]. Figure Figure 1.1(a) illustrates this paradigm. Alternatively,
it can happen in a decentralized fashion where each robot is responsible for providing a
local solution to the SLAM problem, which is then iteratively improved and added to
the shared map[19]. Furthermore, this has the added benefit of allowing for one or more
robots in the team of robots to be turned off at any given moment without disabling the
remaining robots in the team. This system is shown in Figure 1.1(b). This distinction
between centralized and decentralized approaches will serve as the framework to structure
the remainder of the literature review, presenting current methods in each paradigm. In
Section 1.1.5, an overview describing the two paradigms is shown. Both of these can be
implemented in a distributed system where the computational load is shared between all
robots instead of on a central processing node. In general, a decentralized system is often
distributed[19].
Centralized C-SLAM
For the centralized paradigm of C-SLAM algorithms, the problem is solved from a global
perspective, utilizing one single algorithm, which can be deployed at one or more compu-
12
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:41==
tational units, with the latter being the case for a centralized distributed system.
Table 1.1: Overview of the two collaborative SLAM paradigms.
Centralized
Decentralized
Global view of all robots
Local view from own data and partial
data from neighbors
Perfect knowledge of all measurements
Scales well to many robots
Measurements shared on demand
Iteratively find local solutions mutually
consisten across the team of robots.
Communication constraints make a large
number of robots intractable
Common understanding of
map upon convergence
Early notable adaptations of the centralized paradigms include the 2008 paper ”C-SAM:
Multi-Robot SLAM using square root information smoothing” by Lars A. A. Andersson
and Jonas Nygards. In this paper, a team of robots builds a shared map, with or with-
out access to initial poses, using square root information smoothing. Using this optimal
smoothing algorithm for merging maps gathered from multiple robots, not only a shared
map is created, but the trajectory for each of the robots can be recovered[99]. In the
2010 paper ”Multiple relative pose graphs for robust cooperative mapping” by Kim et al.,
the incremental smoothing and mapping method is extended to enable online multi-robot
mapping based on multiple pose graphs[100]. More recent publications within the field
include ”CCM‐SLAM: Robust and eﬀicient centralized collaborative monocular simulta-
neous localization and mapping for robotic teams” by Patrik Schmuck and Margarita Chli
in 2019, in which each robot in the team is able to run visual odometry onboard, ensuring
individual autonomy. A central server enables collaboration by collecting all captured data
and merging and updating the maps[101]. In the 2024 paper ”Collaborative Autonomous
Navigation of Quadrotors in Unknown Outdoor Environments: An Active Visual SLAM
Approach” by Elahian et al., a centralized distributed architecture is used to control a
group of three quadrotor drones, with one being the leader. As for the previous paper,
a central node fuses all information from the drones and determines an optimal path for
the lead drone[102].
For considerations regarding the implementation of these systems, Deutsch et al. propose
in the 2016 paper a framework designed to enable collaborative mapping using existing
single robot SLAM systems. This is achieved by combining local pose graphs obtained
from the robots into a global pose graph, which is, in return, communicated back to the
robots[103].
Bailey et al.
proposed a centralized distributed system in which egocen-
tric data such as IMU, odometry, and GPS data is fused locally on each robot. State
estimates and inter-robot measurements are forwarded to a central node computing an
optimal estimate for the poses of all the robots. To ensure eﬀicient communication, spar-
sity in the messages between the robots and the central node is obtained by employing
the information-form representation of a Gaussian[104].
Decentralized C-SLAM
One of the main issues of using the centralized C-SLAM approach is the fact that unless
measures are taken to optimize communication, the scalability of such a system is sub-
optimal. A solution to this is to consider the local frame, solving the C-SLAM problem in
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
13
==PAGE:42==
a decentralized manner and spreading the computational load across multiple robots[19].
One early example of such a system was defined in the 2007 paper ”A Scalable Hybrid
Multi-Robot SLAM Method for Highly Detailed Maps” by Pfingsthorn et al.
In this
paper, a method maintains a graph with observations by the sensors stored in the nodes
and pose differences stored in the edges.
The updates to the graph are local by the
robots and are able to be communicated effectively across the team.
In this manner,
detailed maps can be produced incrementally, maintaining scalability to larger teams of
robots[105].
A more recent example of decentralized multi-robot collaborative SLAM
frameworks is the ”DiSCo-SLAM: Distributed Scan Context-Enabled Multi-Robot LiDAR
SLAM with Two-Stage Global-Local Graph Optimization”, where a distributed approach
for mapping and localization using LiDAR technology is taken yielding impressive results.
The system utilizes a compact LiDAR scan map representation called Scan Context as
shared feature descriptors between the collaborating robots [106]. The implementation
adopts LiDAR Inertial Odometry Smoothing and Mapping (LIO-SAM) [107] (LiDAR-
SLAM algorithm also used in [13]) as the local SLAM framework on each robot. Each robot
performs a two-step global-local optimization, which ultimately will align the individual
local representations into a common global frame.
C-SLAM for Marine Applications
C-SLAM has been employed in marine applications for such purposes as inspecting under-
water structures such as shipwrecks[108], or more extensively mapping a complete area of
the seabed[109][110]. As previously covered, communication constraints are a challenge to
be solved, and that is especially true in the underwater domain, where opportunities for
inter-robot communication are either rare due to the robots requiring a rendezvous event or
due to the fact that communication of data wirelessly underwater is diﬀicult and expensive.
In the 2020 paper ”Towards Multi-Robot Visual Graph-SLAM for Autonomous Marine
Vehicles” by Francisco Bonin-Font and Antoni Burguera, an approach to create a joined
topological map that evolves continuously in the directions according to the trajectories
of the robots. Herein, the data to be exchanged between the robots was reduced to hashes
of small dimensions to accommodate the communication constraints[111]. Likewise, in the
2014 paper ”Decentralized Cooperative Trajectory Estimation for Autonomous Underwa-
ter Vehicles”, by Paull et al., the communications constraints of underwater robots are
likewise considered, presenting an algorithm specifically designed for maximum aposteriori
estimation for AUV’s, ensuring that the broadcast packet sizes increase only linearly with
the number of agents[110]. In the 2016 paper ”Real-time adaptive multi-robot exploration
with application to underwater map construction” by Kapoutsis et al., and the 2017 paper
”Probabilistic cooperative mobile robot area coverage and its application to autonomous
seabed mapping” by Paull et al., the entire seabed is the focus of mapping, employing
cognitive adaptive optimization and a sliding window filter pose estimation algorithm,
respectively.[109][112]. Furthermore, for Paull et al., an adaptive planning strategy is pre-
sented that improves the eﬀiciency of the mapping by considering the conditions of low
vehicle uncertainty[112].
1.2
Methods & Tools
Concluding upon the literature review, this section will account for the methods and
tools used during the project. This will include the considerations regarding the physical
setup of the system that will both simulate the actual implementation of a collaborative
autonomous system, as well as allow for the collection of the necessary data to facilitate
further development. Next, the specific methods implemented as part of the multi-agent
system are presented.
14
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:43==
Figure 1.2: Illustration of the setting and the key element and sensors included. Only for
conceptual understanding. Nothing is to scale.
Due to the numerous buildings, machinery, containers, and other structures occupying
the mainland, GNSS is often either unavailable or too inaccurate when applied in the
context of creating accurate, detailed environmental maps. Consequently, [13] explored
the feasibility of employing Light Detection And Ranging (LiDAR) technology for this
purpose in combination with a single FLS SONAR. This study demonstrated that the
current state of the art in LiDAR technology and SLAM development enables highly
accurate localization and mapping in densely populated maritime environments above sea
level. Although mapping below sea level was also examined, the findings indicated no
significant advantage in regards to localization in integrating 3D-extracted SONAR points
into the SLAM algorithm.

Inspired by this work, a multi-agent system composed of an USV mounted with two
sonars for high-resolution point cloud mapping and a stereo camera mounted on a ROV
for the inspection of objects of interest is proposed. By utilizing the advantages of the
acoustic sensor technology to sweep the seabed in the harbor area, while maintaining
robust localization of the USV using LiDAR based SLAM, automatic Region of Interst
(ROI) detection is performed to create a set of potential missions for the ROV that can
perform close-up inspections using stereo vision on the Object of Interest (OOI), and
is equipped with a VIO algorithm for robust navigation. To complete the multi-agent
setup, a collaborative SLAM approach is proposed, outlining its intended functionality
beyond the components developed in the project. An illustration of this setup is given
in Figure 1.2. The setup is not required to operate the two vehicles simultaneously but
will support it and potentially leverage it. The overall pipeline is described below and is
supported by Figure 1.3.
USV The Unmanned Surface Vehicle is mounted with a payload consisting of two SONARs,
LiDAR, IMU and a GNSS-receiver, proposing a setup that can benefit from several
modalities when localizing and mapping. The USV setup is extending the setup by
[13].
USV SLAM LiDAR data is used to perform Simultaneous Localization and Mapping to
construct a ground map and perform localization based on both IMU and LiDAR
(potentially GNSS as well). The choice of SLAM algorithm is LIO-SAM [107], which
will be modified to support below water-surface mapping. For acoustic mapping, the
dual SONAR 3D mapping that is suggested in [35] will be used in combination with
leading edge detection for bathymetry mapping. The main purpose of the ground
map is localization, while the seabed map is used for object detection.
Object Detection The mapping of the seabed will be used as input to the 3DETR[87]
16
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:45==
object detection algorithm, which is able to create bounding boxes around objects
that need further investigation, ie, OOIs. As the map grows rapidly, several ROI’s
will potentially exist.
Selection of ROI It has to be decided, based on some heuristic, which Region of Interst
should be investigated by the ROV. This will not be covered in the project.
Path Planning to ROI Having decided on a ROI, path planning is performed. This will
not be covered in the project.
ROV The Remotely Operated Vehicle is equipped with an IMU, pressure sensor, USBL
transponder, and a stereo camera provided by the company UVision. The USBL
system is used for echo localization of the ROV and will be used as a reference for
evaluation.
VIO Ideally, the system should be independent of external echo-localization systems such
as USBL. Therefore, IMU, pressure sensor, and stereo images will be used for local-
ization using the ROVIO VIO algorithm proposed by [60].
ROV Position The position will be estimated using the VIO in combination with location
updates from the Collaborative SLAM based on shared information between the two
robots.
3D Photogrammetry Here initially an semantic masking is created by utilizing the RT-
DETRv2[113] and SAM2 models[114].
This allows the 3D color points obtained
from the images to be populated in the seabed mapping. These points are used for
correcting the position of the robot using the shared information between the robots.
Here the Depth Anything[115] is used for depth estimation.
Collaborative SLAM and database The information that the two individual systems gen-
erate will be combined into a common representation, including a full seabed to sky
map utilizing LiDAR, SONAR, and image data. Additionally, a collaborative SLAM
system is proposed, utilizing perceptual information from each vehicle to enhance
localization and obtain a common representation of the reference frame. For this,
mainly two papers, [116] and [106], are used.

chapter, indicating which part is being addressed. Chapter 9 presents a more elaborate
system pipeline. The reader is advised to revisit Figure 1.3 when needed to grasp how the
different blocks are tied together. The more elaborated system can be found in Figure 9.2.

1.3
Contribution
This thesis builds on the foundational work as presented in [13] and [117], extending the
captured sensor modalities to a stereo SONAR setup, and adding an ROV equipped with
an inertial stereo camera device. In this manner, the overall approach to mapping, has
been extended to that of a collaborative setup.
The first contribution is that of the sensor fusion payload box, a modular system both
in hardware and software, capable of recording data from multiple sensor modalities to a
central location. In addition to designing and building this sensor fusion payload, interface
software for the SONARS had to be migrated from ROS 1 to ROS 2.
Containerized
applications have been utilized to handle interfacing with the individual sensor modalities,
and an Ansible pipeline has been created to automate the deployment of each of the
containers.
An additional contribution within the hardware domain is a calibrated inertial stereo
camera system, knowing the extrinsic transforms between the IMU-fixed frame and the
cameras of the UScanner.
This information is vital in implementing VIO and visual-
inertial SLAM solutions.
Both the USV and the ROV, with the attached UScanner,
have been deployed through the collection of marine datasets in a partially GNSS-denied
environment.
Building on the datasets obtained, and the work by [13], seabed-to-sky mapping capabili-
ties have been employed by extending LIO-SAM to include features from the SONARs as
part of the mapping process. A more feature-rich mapping is achieved through interpola-
tion between keyframes while maintaining real-time capabilities.
A synthetic 3D data curation pipeline has been suggested for creating simple environments.
This enabled evaluation for a deep learning, transformer-based architecture, 3DETR, re-
flecting a later observed canal environment.
Moving towards a multi-agent system, the ROVIO visual-inertial odometry algorithm has
been implemented and evaluated on the UScanner in a controlled environment, by com-
paring estimated trajectories with a recorded baseline. Additionally, the implementation
has been tested on the real-world data obtained during the data acquisition campaign,
where the UScanner is attached to the ROV.
Utilizing the underwater visual inspection capabilities the ROV offered, a pipeline build-
ing on the RT-DETRv2 and SAM2 models allows for creating accurate image masks of
detected objects in 2D. Additionally, the foundation model Depth Anything was explored
for its ability to create accurate depth maps.
Finally, a proposal for a multi-agent system based on the completed efforts is given. The
proposed system builds on the results obtained in the project, utilizing collaborative SLAM
to facilitate the creation of a shared map.

sentences. ChatGPT and GitHub Copilot have been used to assist with programming,

1.4
Thesis Overview
Chapter 2: The Unmanned Surface Vehicle Sensor Platform presents the design and im-
plementation of the modular sensor fusion payload. This includes both hardware
and software design choices and descriptions of the included sensor modalities. The
platform is evaluated and suggested improvements are discussed.
Chapter 3: The Remotely Operated Vehicle Setup outlines the integration of the UVi-
sion stereo camera system with the BlueROV2 Heavy ROV. This covers both phys-
ical mounting solutions and software integration, as well as extensive calibration of
the system.
Chapter 4: Data Acquisition covers the two Sydhavn Belvedere Canal data campaigns
and additional experiments conducted in the Autonomous Systems Test Arena (ASTA).
The acquired data is presented and analyzed, highlighting potential issues.
Chapter 5: USV Simultaneous Localization and Mapping describes the implementation of
seabed-to-sky mapping through a LiDAR-IMU-based SLAM method and presents
the resulting maps, followed by a discussion on the strengths and weaknesses of the
tested implementation.
Chapter 6: Object Detection poses a supervised transformer-based approach for using the
seabed maps for semantic object detection in 3D.
Chapter 7: ROV Visual Inertial Odometry addresses the use of VIO for vehicle localiza-
tion, evaluated in both controlled and real-world environments.
Chapter 8: Photogrammetry presents a pipeline for creating semantic masks in images.
Also explores both the classic stereo-amtching method and a deep learning based
monocular based depth estimation method.
Chapter 9: Multi-agent Marine System presents a Collaborative SLAM framework for
aligning the representation of the environment between multiple agents and leverag-
ing from shared observations. The framework is yet to be implemented. The chapter
will end with a discussion that reflects upon the current state of the system.
Chapter 10 and 11: Conclusion and Future Work highlights key insights and results ob-
tained through the thesis, and presents how these influence the development of future
systems.

Building on the research by Galeazzi et al. [117], Lund et
al. [13], and Thompson et al. [15], a modular sensor fusion
payload is designed, built, and implemented with the aim of

data collection,which will be described later in this project.
This chapter will detail the employed modalities and their
implementation, discuss key design decisions, and provide an
overview of the data types generated by the system.
The payload extends the existing onboard capabilities of the
”Otter” USV from Maritimes Robotics. The vessel available
for our project is pre-equipped with GNSS, a forward-facing
camera, and a radio frequency remote control. A vital feature
of the Otter is that it can easily be disassembled for transport.
