2.1
The Sensor Fusion Payload
The main purpose of the sensor fusion payload is to combine multiple sensor modalities into
one centralized system for the USV, enabling seamless operation of the sensors mounted
on the vehicle. The sensors used in this project are listed in Table 2.2. The different
modalities are described in later sections of this chapter.
Designing a system to be used for research prompts a solution that prioritizes modularity
and effective system integration during concept development. Table 2.3 presents the sensor
fusion platform’s complete Product Design Specification (PDS).
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

2.1.1
The Case
Before this project, a Mitsutomo Waterproof Case (dimensions: 215mm×425mm×569mm)
has been modified so that it contains a hole for cable outtakes from the box. A tube extends
the hole by 120 mm, making the payload more splash-resistant. For prototyping, a 10mm
wooden baseplate has been placed in the bottom of the case, making it suitable for moving
the mounts during development. Additionally, two 35mm DIN rails are mounted on the
board, making it easy to mount and dismount the different components, given that they
have a DIN mounting point. The case can be easily opened without any tools, has a handle,
is lightweight, and can be carried by a single person. The case fits into a custom-made
mount on the USV in a tight fit.
2.1.2
Modular setup
Several key considerations have been made to create a fully modular setup. Each sensor,
when feasible, is equipped with its own Raspberry Pi. This dedicated single-board com-
puter runs the necessary software for communicating with the sensor, including converting
measured data into the appropriate format. To streamline communication between the
single-board computers, it has been decided that each device must support Robot Oper-
ating System (ROS) 2. Implementing this requirement will simplify sensor information
management and facilitate future system extensions. For example, the data formats of
the sonars vary significantly; however, they must be unified into a single format for effec-
tive downstream processing. Therefore, each SONAR RPi is responsible for reading the
messages from the SONAR and converting them to this format before the messages are
recorded. Furthermore, services are called to communicate messages with the SONAR
to set the settings and start data collection. Due to the variance among sensors, it is
essential to manage each one individually within its system. This system should be de-
signed to be dismountable and utilized separately if necessary. Additionally, running the
sensor individually can prevent failure in data collection from other sensors if one sensor
malfunctions, as it won’t necessarily affect different parts of the system.
Utilizing ROS2
Some of the main reasons to use the newer ROS 2 instead of the well-established ROS
version 1 are:
• The latest release of ROS 1, Noetic Ninjemys, reaches its End of Life (EOL) on May
2025. This means no further updates will be provided, including security patches or
bug fixes. This will likely cause a decline in support for ROS 1 packages and drivers.
• It is more secure due to the switch to DDS.
• The MCAP storage format is natively supported in ROS2. MCAP performs better
than SQLite3 for large datasets [118].
22
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:51==
• Communication between nodes is more seamless as it doesn’t go through a ROS
Master.
DDS has removed the need for the ROS ”master” node, which ultimately simplifies a
modular setup, as all nodes can discover and communicate with each other without a
single point of failure [119].

To collect the data into one single file, one RPi has the sole purpose of recording the data
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
23
==PAGE:52==
from all the other RPi’s into a single MCAP file. This Raspberry Pi is referred to as the
Listener Pi or Datalogger Pi and is only connected to the other RPi’s through Ethernet
and has an external Solid State Drive (SSD) storage for extended data storage.
Communication between RPi’s
While different setups of powering the RPi’s and establishing communication between
them were considered, the final solution ended up using Power over Ethernet (PoE)+
combined with oﬀicial RPi PoE+ Hardware Attached on Top (HAT)s, which allows the
RPi to receive power through PoE+ while being connected at the same time. Therefore,
all RPi’s are connected to an unmanaged PoE+ network switch through their onboard
Ethernet port.
Different Voltage Inputs
Different sensors have varying voltage input requirements, which must be accommodated
in the setup. In this specific sensor configuration, all components are powered using a
24V power supply during development and a 30V battery during deployment. However,
the chosen Ethernet PoE+ switch needs a 48V input. Therefore, a 24V out and 48V out
DC/DC converter is integrated into the setup. The 48V version might become useful for
other configurations in the future.

supports DIN-rail mounting and room for

which can be seen in Section 2.1.3.
2.1.4
Connectivity Diagram
Having established the practical and technical requirements of our system along with the
different components that must be integrated, a connectivity diagram has been drawn
in order to establish an overview of the complete sensor fusion system of the USV. The
diagram can be found in figure 2.3.
A few comments to clarify potential misunderstandings of the diagram. First of all, two
sources of power are drawn on the diagram. The 24V out AC/DC converter is practical for
setups that are not being used on the USV or when developing. However, when deployed
on the USV, the payload is powered by the 30V batteries on the Otter. Secondly, the
wireless connection between the Otter and the Otter Laptop is established using a 5 GHz
access point for radio communication, which is used to control the Otter. Due to internal
restrictions on the use of this laptop, another laptop is connected to the HP Laptop, which
works as a communication unit for the payload. This laptop is equipped with ROS 2 as
well, and the program Foxglove for data inspection during the campaign.
While the original idea was that the Teledyne BlueView could be powered directly using
the 50-57V PoE+ ethernet switch, this was not possible due to incompatibility between
the PoE powered sensor and PoE+ switch. Therefore, a standard PoE injector is used
instead.

time synchronization and starting procedures were still conducted. Further testing should
be conducted to check.

2.2
Sonar Imaging
To achieve a dense 3D reconstruction of sub-sea environments, a dual sonar setup is uti-
lized, based on the hypothesis that two Forward Looking Sonar (FLS) sonars arranged
orthogonally can effectively generate 3D spatial points [35]. How this method is imple-
mented is further described in section Section 5.1.3.
Building on this motivation, the
subsequent section provides a detailed review of the principles underlying active sonars,
with a specific focus on FLS technology. Finally, the section concludes with an overview
of the specific sensors employed in this study and their implementation within the system.
2.2.1
Active Sonars
1
Ping is transmitted
2
Ping arrives at
bottom
3
Echo event
4
Echo arrives at receiver
Figure 2.6: The path of a single ping and
how each element of the SONAR equation
comes to play.
The terms are Signal Ex-
cess (SE), Signal Level (SL), Backscattering
Strengt (BS), Target Area (TA), Noise Level
(NL) and Transmission Loss (TL). Inspired
by [120].
This
study
focuses
exclusively
on
ac-
tive sonars, specifically Multibeam Echosounder
System (MBES) or also mentioned For-
ward Looking Sonar (FLS). These types
of echosounders employ beamforming tech-
niques to determine the direction of incom-
ing sound waves that are reflected from the
surrounding environment.
By combining
this directional information with distance
estimation based on the Time of Flight
(ToF), multidimensional measurements of
the environment can be obtained.
To keep track of the involved factors when
using a SONAR, literature refers to the
SONAR Equation, whose purpose is to de-
scribe the different factor that comes into
play when a signal ping is transmitted. The
equation has been posed in many different
versions as the detail level varies. Here, the
SONAR equation is defined as
SE = SL −2TL + BS −NL + TA
(2.1)
where the Signal Excess (SE) is received is captured by the transducer. Figure 2.6 follows
the path of the ping where the ping is transmitted at Signal Level (SL) and then atten-
uated by Transmission Loss (TL) until it hits the bottom. This event causes backscatter
which is denoted Backscattering Strengt (BS). The Target Area (TA) is the effective area
contributing to the reflected signal strength, while the Noise Level (NL) accounts for noise
that may occur through the whole process.
The transducer in a SONAR is an isotropic source, meaning that it emits a signal that
spreads energy equally in all directions, also called isotropic expansion.
This physical
behavior is not ideal as it strikes the seabed in all directions, and the direction of the
return echo is not identifiable. This can be resolved by using an array of transducers and
performing beamforming [120]. The following will present a case of beamforming using a
uniform linear array and the delay-and-sum method.
Given a uniform linear phase array as depicted in Figure 2.7, a signal i with angle θi
arrives at sensor r1 before the subsequent array element, which means that the signal
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
27
==PAGE:56==
Figure 2.7: A Uniform Linear Array. The diagonal dotted line denotes the wavefront of
the signal. Source: [121].
travels d sin θi further before impinging r2 after r1 has been impinged, where d is the
distance between each array element [121].
If the incoming wavefront strikes the array of transducers at a 0-degree angle, summing
all the measured signals results in a single high-gain peak. However, introducing delays
between the signals reduces their correlation, leading to a wider main lobe and lower
peak gain due to phase differences that introduce destructive interference and reduce
coherence. By artificially delaying the signals received by each transducer, the gain from
different directions can be controlled and measured. This principle forms the foundation
of Delay-Sum beamforming [122].
2.2.2
Representing the sonar images
Figure 2.8: Packing of sonar data into the
SonarImageData. Figure from [123].
To standardize the setup of sonars, a choice
was made regarding how the output format
of the sonars should be in order to stream-
line the pipeline.
Optimally, using the
sensor_msgs type would be preferable1.
However, none of the existing message
types are suitable for sonar data. Fortu-
nately, researchers at Washington Applied
Physics Laboratory Ocean Engineering de-
partment (APL OE) have implemented a
set of ROS messages, which are already
used widely in the maritime ROS com-
munity, called marine_msgs.
For multi-
beam sonars like the Oculus and Blueview, image data can be converted into the
ProjectedSonarImage message format, which is represented in a range/angle space. A full
understanding of this message is critical, as it will be used throughout the implementation
[123].
The ProjectedSonarImage message consists of 4 topics besides the header;
ping_info
contains a submessage of type PingInfo which has the center fre-
1sensor_msgs ROS2 Message Documentation
28
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:57==
quency of the sonar in Hz, the speed of sound used in m/s and the
tx_beamwidths and rx_beamwidths which are directions in the tem-
poral domain. Tx stands for transmit, Rx for receive.
beam_directions which is list of vectors in the geometry_msgs/Vector3[] message
type. The 3D unit vectors give the directions of each beam in the
spatial domain.
ranges
which is a list of each range bin in meters. It is given in a float32[]
format.
SonarImageData
is the sonar data packed into a row-major order [123].
Figure 2.8 shows how the raw sonar image pixel data is packed into the SonarImageData
message in a row-major order as an array of bytes, fx. in uint8 or uint16, specified explicitly
in the dtype flag.
This message type contains all the necessary information needed for further processing, as
long as it is populated correctly from each individual sonar ROS node.

One of the two SONARs used in
this project is the Oculus M750d
from the company Blueprint Sub-

operating frequencies, 750 kHz and

will be used in this project.
ROS 2 Implementation
In order to streamline the framework, a great effort has been put into converting an existing
ROS implementation into ROS 2. The main driver of the sonar is the liboculus driver
from the University of APL OE. The main driver has been used almost ”as is”, apart
from changing some compile files in order to comply with colcon compilation instead of
catkin. However, the driver needs to be controlled, and the messages returned from the
sonar need to be converted into the correct format. Therefore, a ROS wrapper is needed.
Fortunately, APL OE has provided a wrapper for the Oculus sonar that provides these
features but developed in ROS 1.
An initial attempt was made to utilize an alternative implementation developed by ENSTA
Bretagne Robotics, which provides both a driver and a wrapper compatible with ROS 2.
However, this implementation could not be successfully integrated with the Oculus M750d,
necessitating the abandonment of this approach. Consequently, it was decided to rewrite
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
29
==PAGE:58==
the ROS 1 wrapper for the liboculus implementation to ROS 2 as this had been successfully
tested on the SONAR with a simple command line interface. It should be stressed that
the resulting implementation is heavily based on the existing aforementioned wrapper, but
in ROS 2.
For the wrapper, two ROS 2 nodes have been implemented for the sensor: A main client
node, oculus_client_node, which is responsible for initializing the sensor and setting
the initial settings such as ping-rate and gain. Here, several changes were made to the
original code in order to comply with ROS 2’s params paradigm instead of using ROS
DynamicReconfiguration.
The node publishes the raw data received from each ping
into the /oculus/raw_data topic, which the second node subscribes to.
The second
node, reprocess_oculus_raw_data, is responsible for reordering the raw data into the
message type ProjectedSonarImage, which is described earlier in Section 2.2.2.
This
process is slower than simply using the raw data, and it therefore makes sense to have this
implemented as two different nodes. In addition to this, an implementation for publishing
the actual images was included as well. However, due to a decrease in performance, slowing
down the update frequency, this was taken out of the code and handled elsewhere in the
system.
2.2.4
Teledyne BlueView
Table 2.5: System specification of the Teledyne Blue-

The second SONAR used in the
project
is

sis.
The sonar has two different
operating frequencies: 900kHz and
2250kHz, as given in the name, and
features three different options of
horizontal FOV in both frequency
modes, giving a total of six modes
of operation.
As for the Oculus,
the specifications depend on which
operating frequency is used. These
specifications are listed in table 2.5.
Given the specifications and the
setup of the project, it becomes
clear that the BlueView should operate in the high-frequency (2250 kHz) mode as we
are operating in shallow waters, and this mode has the largest vertical beam width (ver-
tical aperture) of 20◦.
ROS 2 Implementation
As for the Oculus M750d, a requirement was that the SONAR should publish its read-
ings through a ROS node, with the selected ProjectedSonarImage message type. Such a
framework was readily available through an earlier project at DTU. However, it was im-
plemented in ROS 1 Noetic. A considerable amount of work was therefore spent to convert
this framework into a usable ROS 2 implementation. The Blueview Software Development
Kit itself was provided by Teledyne and was not changed. However, it was only compatible
with 32-bit arm processors, and therefore, for the containerized implementation, ROS 2
had to be built from source.
The blooview_interface node, initially written by Fletcher Francis Thompson as a ROS
interface utilizing the BlueView SDK package, was the module requiring re-implementation
30
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:59==
in ROS 2 Humble.
As for the wrapper for the Oculus M750d, changes were made
such that the interface could be controlled with ROS 2 service calls in place of the
DynamicReconfiguration paradigm. These service calls were facilitated by the re-implementation
of the blooview_msgs package, likewise merging this to ROS 2.
Performance Issues
Prior to the first preliminary data acquisition campaign, described in Section 4.1, the main
focus was ensuring that the blooview_interface node was operational and publishing
data to log. Following this data campaign, it was determined that the implementation
should be optimized, as the frequency of the recorded SONAR was approximately 2.3Hz,
even though the ping rate had been set to 10. In the campaign, the range of the SONAR
was set to 10m, and the high-frequency head with a FOV of 130◦was used.
The first modification after profiling the operations in the blooview_interface node in-
volved removing the standard image publishing capabilities, such that only the ProjectedSonarImage
topic was published. As this operation included a computationally heavy conversion of
the SONAR data from polar coordinates to a cartesian image coordinate system for each
ping, a considerable amount of time was saved. However, this optimization alone did not
bring the rate of the recordings close to the desired ping rate of 10Hz.
To further identify computational bottlenecks, the interface was run on bare metal by
installing a server version of Ubuntu 22.04 onto a RPi, building ROS-2 Humble from
source, and finally building the interface.
While this did further improve the rate of
recordings, no more than 5-7Hz could be obtained.
Ultimately, it was decided to switch to the high-frequency SONAR head with a 45◦FOV,
as the BlueView SONAR would be used in a vertical configuration with the Oculus that
itself has a 20◦vertical aperture. This reduction in FOV from 130◦to 45◦meant that
the amount of data to process was reduced with 65.4%, and consequently a recording rate
of the desired 10Hz was achieved, both when running the node on bare metal, and in a
containerized application.

Figure 2.9: All designed by [13]. The image
is a screenshot of the design, visualized On-

tions of both singular or multiple SONARs
mounted as it is highly configurable.
A
screenshot of the design can be found in
Figure 2.9.
The designed mount is 3D
printed in PLA.
2.3
LiDAR
Light Detection And Ranging (LiDAR) is
a Time of Flight measurement sensor that
utilizes laser light for distance estimates.
These range estimates are based on the
time needed for a light beam to reach the target and return. Applying multiple indi-
vidual laser transceivers at different altitude levels placed on a spinning actuator allows
for a 360° 3D FOV in azimuth and in elevation depending on the specific LiDAR’s limita-
tions[127]. This gives a dense point representation of the environment. In addition to the
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
31
==PAGE:60==
relative positional information, LiDAR also provides the reflected amplitude. This inten-
sity depends on the surface of the reflected objects, often revealing intrinsic properties of
the material in question.
When light hits a point p, depending on the surface, the wavelength of the incident light
will be reflected almost isotropically due to diffuse reflection. This gives the property
that regardless of the angle between p and the transceiver, as long as the emitted light
falls within the receiving aperture, the light will return almost parallel to the transmitted
beam.

Typically, the range estimates are based on the phase shift between the transmitted and
received light at a known frequency f. Here, the modulating wavelength λ follows the
relationship of c = f · λ, with c being the speed of light and f being the modulating
frequency. Here, the total distance traveled by the emitted light is given by:
r′ = l + 2r = l + θ
2πλ.
(2.2)
Here θ is the electronic measured phase change between the transmitted and received light
beam. r is the distance between the rotating mirror and p while l is the distance between
the mirror and detector. Thereby, the relative distance can be expressed as:
r = (l + θ
2πλ) −l
2
=
θ
2πλ
2
= λ
4πθ.
(2.3)
Shortcomings of this approach include scenarios with a coherent reflection of the target
energy. Using light, this occurs when striking a highly polished surface, imposing com-
plications estimating the relative position, due to the lack of diffuse reflections instead
being dominated by a specular reflection. Using conventional LiDAR systems will fail
at measuring the water surface since the wavelengths are refracted, scattered, and partly
absorbed by the water, resulting in hulls in the data[128]. The bathymetric LiDAR fixes
this by using a short and strong laser pulse, typically using a green wavelength of 532 nm.
Given parameters such as water transparency, turbidity, and water depth, some light will
successfully be reflected from the seabed. The bathymetric LiDAR deduces the time of
light hitting the water surface and seabed based on the waveform of the received signal.

Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:61==
Due to the high density of points and their high precision, this technology has been
increasingly used within the domain of high-resolution 3D mapping and localization. Here,
the field of mobile robots and autonomous driving have especially utilized LiDAR to assist
in navigating both static and dynamic environments. Potential issues with this use case is
if suﬀiciently high either linear or angular velocities are applied to the LiDAR the relative
position will be distorted.
2.3.1
Ouster OS1
In this work, the Ouster OS1 LiDAR is being utilized due to it being designed for all-
weather environments in an industrial setting. Ouster OS1 is a 360° horizontal FoV LiDAR
with 128 vertical channels. The range mainly depends on diffuse reflectance, albedo for a
Lambertian surface, but is reported as 90 meters at 10% albedo and 170 meters at 80%
with a detection probability of 90%.

2.4
Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU)
The Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU) is a passive proprioceptive sensor system that allows
one to perceive the kinematics of a given object. A traditional IMU uses the combination of
three axes of linear acceleration from the inbuilt accelerometer with three axes of angular
velocity from a gyroscope[127]. Together, this forms an IMU of six axes that allows for an
estimation of the position (x, y, z) and orientation (roll, pitch, yaw). The issue herein arises
due to bias and drift over time, since then integration acceleration to get velocity/position,
or integrating angular rate to get orientation, noise and these small biases accumulate over
time, causing drift. To make this more robust, a magnetometer can be added to measure
the local magnetic field vector. This allows for a fixed external reference, as it provides a
reference to Earth’s magnetic field, reducing long-term drift in orientation. Adding this
modality gives a 9-axis IMU.
Only using an IMU for pose estimation would rely on dead reckoning, namely to calculate
the current pose based on the previously determined pose.
This again introduces the
issue that the IMU estimate will inevitably drift. Here, different filtering methods, like
the Kalman filter or correction methods, can be used to improve the reliability of the
IMU[127]. A correction method could be the active heading stabilization (AHS) that uses
the magnetic field to stabilize the drift in heading.
Due to the choice of methods described in Section 1.2, it is most suitable to use a nine-axis
IMU with a minimum update frequency of 200Hz. For this, the XSens IMU MTi-10 9-axis
IMU has been chosen. The specifications for this can be found in Table 2.7. For system
redundancy, in case of faults or errors with the XSens IMU, the built-in InvenSense IAM-
20680HT 6-axis IMU of the Ouster LiDAR is considered as well, specifications for this can
be found in Table 2.8
2.5
Software
To achieve true modularity in the proposed system, it is advantageous to package individ-
ual software modules into containers. A container encapsulates Operating System (OS),
applications, libraries, and configurations required by a module’s needs, ensuring isolation
and portability. One popular choice for managing containerization is Docker, which is
a Platform as a Service (PaaS). Docker as a service can be categorized into three main
components: software, objects, and registries [133].
• Software: This component manages Docker containers and their associated objects.
It can be accessed through the Docker client program or the command-line interface
(CLI).

• Objects: These are the fundamental building blocks used to construct an application
in Docker. The primary objects are containers, images, and services:
– Containers: Standardized, encapsulated environments that execute applica-
tions. They are managed via the Docker API or CLI.
– Images: Read-only templates used to create containers.
They serve as the
foundation for application deployment. In order to create images, a sort of
recipe is defined in a file called a Dockerfile. The Dockerfile contains in-
structions specifying the base image, system dependencies, application source
code, environment configurations, and commands necessary to build and run
the containerized application.
– Services: Enable container distribution across multiple Docker daemons, form-
ing swarms to facilitate scalability and load balancing.
• Registries: Docker registries serve as repositories for Docker images.
The latest
images are stored and can be pushed to Docker Hub, a widely used public registry
[133].

development for SONAR drivers has been required to ensure compatibility with ROS.

which enables live visualization of SONAR images, which is very useful when inspecting
and calibrating SONARs in the field. Figure 2.12 shows how the bridge enables commu-
nication of specific topics.

37
==PAGE:66==
An oﬀicial bridge is maintained by the ROS community. However, as stated in the repos-
itory, enabling this bridge is necessarily straightforward. First of all, both ROS 1 and
ROS 2 have to be installed in the same system, requiring that one of them is built from
source. Furthermore, all message and service types that have to be shared between the
two systems have to be available in both installations at compile time of the bridge. Ad-
ditionally, careful configuration of which topics and services are shared is recommended
for larger projects, as dynamically bridging all the topics can add additional overhead to
the system [134][135].
It is worth mentioning that the bridge can enable live collaboration between the ROV
setup and the USV setup, which is currently operating in ROS 1 and ROS 2, respectively.
2.5.2
Ansible
The open-source automation framework Ansible is utilized to streamline and automate the
deployment of the containerized applications on the RPi’s. In practice, this is achieved
through ”playbooks”, which use YAML syntax to declare a desired state of a local or
remote system. Running these playbooks through Ansible ensures that the given system,
in this case, a RPi, remains in this state[].
For each of the docker containers depicted in Figure 2.11 (except the otter_usv_ouster_lidar_ros2
container, which is handled separately on the Nvidia Jetson), a number of playbooks are
created to facilitate easy control over the distributed system.
There are two types of
playbooks for each container: ”Launch” and ”Close”.
Launch playbooks are responsible for spinning up the associated docker container. First,
a check is made to see whether the container is already running. If this is the case,
a message will be returned informing the user of this. If not, the docker container is
launched on the RPi using the correct docker-compose file.
Close Playbooks are responsible for shutting down the running docker containers safely,
also using the docker-compose file. However, first, a check is made to see whether
the container is running at all. If not, a message will be sent with this information
to the user.
For each type of playbook, there is a main playbook that is responsible for calling all
launch or close playbooks. In this manner, the entire distributed system can be started
and stopped simply by running the corresponding main playbook. In addition to this,
the time_sync_playbook is responsible for connecting to all RPis and forwarding the
time from the main computer, and the main_shutdown_playbook shuts down all RPis
securely. Finally, an inventory.yml file is responsible for storing all information related
to the RPi’s, including their IP-address, as well as username and password.
2.6
Reference Systems and GNSS
Knowing the fundamentals of the different reference systems and how to transform between
them is essential as it is vital for determining the most appropriate system for navigation,
mapping, and, at last, evaluation of the overall system performance.
2.6.1
Latitude-Longitude-Altitude
The latitude states the north-south position relative to the equator at 0◦up to 90◦at
the north pole and −90◦at the south pole. Longitude on the other hand, defines the
angle from the Prime Meridian at 0◦to a ±180◦degrees, where eastward is positive and
westward is negative.
38
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:67==
Figure 2.13: Geodetic (µ and geocentric (µ′) latitude (eccentricity magnified). Figure and
figure text from [136].
Now, the approximation of the shape of the Earth and, hence, the coordinates will depend
on the chosen system. A common system to use is the World Geodetic System (WGS)
84, as this is what is used in the American Global Positioning System (GPS) systems.
This system is geodetic, which means that the latitude is determined by the tangent plane
and equator as shown in figure 2.13. The figure also shows how a geocentric latitude is
computed with respect to the center of the Earth, which will yield a very different latitude.
It is, therefore, important to state which system coordinates are addressed in [136].
2.6.2
Reference Frames
In the following, four different reference frames are described, three of which are used
actively. In the implementation, more reference frames are used, such as the frame of the
AUV or a specific SONAR. However, ultimately, the output of these module-specific frames
will always be transformed into one or several of the main reference frames described in
this subsection.
The first frame is the Earth-Centered-Initial Frame (ECI), which has its origin in the
center of the Earth, and its x−y axis coincides with the equatorial plane of the Earth and
the z points to the North pole. It is assumed to be inertial, which means that it is not
subject to accelerations. This reference frame is mostly useful for describing the motion
of celestial bodies such as satellites and not so much of objects on the surface of the earth
[136][137].
The Earth-Centered-Inertial Frame (ECEF) has the same center, and the x −y axis lies
on the equator while z points to the North pole. The difference is that the frame rotates
together with the earth, meaning that the x axis always points toward the prime meridian.
Due to the low angular velocity of the earth, the ECEF frame can be considered as inertial.
The ECEF frame is shown in figure Figure 2.14a.
While it can be practical for many reasons to have your coordinates in an Earth-centered
reference frame, it quickly becomes impractical when working with 3D mapping of the real
world compared to working with local Cartesian coordinates. This is due to the complexity
of the ECEF Coordinates, floating-point precision, computational eﬀiciency and general
integration of the coordinate frames used. It is, therefore, useful to introduce another
frame called the North-East-Down (NED) frame, which can be obtained by defining a
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
39
==PAGE:68==
local plane tangent to the area of interest. Since the plane is tangent to the surface of
the Earth, z is not necessarily pointing directly at the center of the Earth. This is, of
course, an approximation as we will neglect the curvature of the Earth locally. The x-axis
is pointing strictly north and the y-axis to the east [136]. Figure 2.14b shows the NED
frame.
(a) Earth-Centered-Inertial Frame (eccentricity mag-
nified). The x-axis rotates rigidly with the earth and
thus it always points the prime meridian.
(b) North-East-Down frame (eccentricity
magnified.
Figure 2.14: The ECEF and NED frames of reference. Figures and figure text from [136].
It is common that maritime and aerospace vehicles use the NED-system. However, some-
times the East-North-Up (ENU)-frame is preferred as it aligns well with map-based co-
ordinate conventions and can seem more intuitive is used in context of 3D pointcloud
mapping[13].
Fortunately, transforming the NED-frame to the East-North-Up (ENU)
frame is simply switching the x- and y-axis and flip the direction of the z-axis[138], hence
the rotation matrix for going either way is;
RNED↔ENU =

2.6.3
NMEA Messages
In practice, the GNSS receiver usually handles the estimation of position internally and
outputs the result in a specific protocol. One of the commonly used protocols NMEA
0183 protocol which outputs the information as American Standard Code for Information
Interchange (ASCII) stings. In this study, two different sentence types are used explicitly:
Global Positioning System Fix Data (GGA) (see Listing 2.1) and Geographic Position,
Latitude / Longitude (GLL) (see Listing 2.2)[139][140].
1
<Talker>GGA,<Timestamp UTC>,<Lat>,<N/S>,<Long>,<E/W>,<GPSQuality >,<#
Satellites >,<HDOP>,<Alt>,<Alt Unit>,<Geoidal Seperation >,<Geoidal Units>,<
DGPS Age>,<DGPS Ref>*<checksum ><CR><LF>
Listing 2.1: Global Positioning System Fix Data (GGA) message.
1
<Talker>GLL,<Lat>,<N/S>,<Long>,<E/W>,<Timestamp UTC>,<Status Indicator A/V Ref
><Mode Indicator >*<checksum ><CR><LF>
Listing 2.2: Geographic Position, Latitude / Longitude (GLL) message.
40
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:69==
The two messages are differ as GGA contains more information regard satellites and the
quality of the information compared to GLL. As an example, for this project GLL messages
are used to send a known fixed position to the USBL transceiver (see Section 3.5.1), and
therefore not directly obtained by the GNSS system at that given time, making this
sentence type more appropriate.
2.7
Discussion
This chapter has mainly covered the final design of the sensor fusion payload, with key
insights into the developmental process. However, as is customary for any engineering
project, the design underwent many iterations and was a work in progress up until the point
of deployment. Though a structured approach to the development phase was prioritized,
a considerable amount of time was spent on reconfiguring and redesigning otherwise well-
planned solutions, as bugs, errors, and issues were inevitably encountered. As these issues
were solved, the connectivity diagrams, distributed container architecture, bill of materials,
and standard operating procedures all had to be updated and, in some cases, rewritten
from scratch, adding to the time spent in addition to actually implementing the changes.
Especially the networking part posed troublesome, with suspected errors in the network
configurations of the RPi’s, leading to the inclusion of an additional unmanaged switch,
as the planned Ethernet dongles would cause the network manager on the OS’ to mal-
function, affecting the whole system. Additionally, the inclusion of an updated version
of the Ouster LiDAR increased the data load significantly from previous setups, requir-
ing additional debugging in network and DDS configurations. In the end, the solution
was to run the LiDAR on a separate NVIDIA Jetson, logging the measurements to its
internal database. Finally, rewriting and debugging already established codebases for the
SONARs, migrating from ROS 1 Noetic to ROS 2 Humble, was another time-consuming
aspect of the development phase.
Considering the PDS for the sensor fusion platform written in the initial stages of the
project, the proposed design can be evaluated in terms of how well the requirements are
fulfilled. For system requirements, a major shortcoming is that the Nvidea Jetson added to
handle the LiDAR recordings does not communicate with the rest of the system. For the
modularity aspect, depending on the specific type, the system is generally able to handle
multiple sensors, running with two SONARs, IMU, and GPS simultaneously with shared
communication. Additional testing is required to confirm the limit of the sensors and the
inclusion of LiDAR into the shared system. Furthermore, the requirement of each sensor to
have a reserved operational device is met, and new sensors can be added to the system as
long as an additional device with correct network configuration and ROS compatibility is
included in the system. However, a consideration should be made regarding the bandwidth
of the system, as the addition of a data-heavy sensor can put strain on the entire system, as
observed with the LiDAR. Finally, for safety, when all requirements are met, the criterion
of resilience against cyber attacks has not been addressed. Since the Otter, which the
payload is connected to, uses wireless communication for control and communication,
hijacking is a risk.
Generally, the goals and requirements for the sensor fusion platform for this project have
been met. One thing to consider going forward is that there is currently no capability
of implementing any online SLAM techniques as the RPi’s are unsuitable for this due to
their lack of computational power. Therefore, a proposed change could be to still manage
the individual sensors over RPi’s, but add a central computing node with more memory,
processing power and GPU acceleration, to handle the ROS-bridge, the onboard SLAM
system and possibly demanding sensors such as a LiDAR.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
41
==PAGE:70==
2.8
Chapter Summary
In conclusion, the following contributions have been presented in this chapter:
• The design and creation of a modular sensor fusion payload to record data from all
required sensor modalities for the project. Due to modular design, additional sensors
can be added.
• The design and implementation of a containerized distributed software system, in-
terfacing with the sensors locally on the designated operational device, and recording
data to a central storage location.
• ROS 2 wrappers / interfaces for the Oculus and BlueView sonars.
• ROS Bridge that allows for visualizing sonar images in real-time, with the purpose
of inspection.
• The creation of an Ansible pipeline for automated deployment of the entire system.

To enhance data acquisition in the underwater domain, a
BlueROV2 Heavy Remotely Operated Vehicle, is used along-
side an Otter USV. The ROV is equipped with the UVision
UScanner, which features a 3D underwater camera-based sys-
tem equipped with an inertial stereo-camera system and a
barometer. While the ROV is itself equipped with a camera,
IMU, and barometer, the addition of the UScanner is from
a perspective of modularity: Implementing VIO and pho-
togrammetry solutions on a self-contained, calibrated, mod-
ule allows for switching between multiple ROVs and even
different vessel types, without the need to re-calibrate equip-
ment and implement and fine-tune algorithms for the specific
vessel.
This chapter will cover the ROV block of the system shown in
Figure 3.1 by providing a description of the BlueROV2 Heavy
itself used for the project, the UScanner, and the USBL sys-
tem, which is used to obtain a baseline in Sydhavnen. In
addition, the calibration procedures and results for the in-
ertial stereo-camera system will be presented. Finally, the
software framework for operating the BlueROV2 Heavy and
collecting data from it will be described.
3.1
The BlueROV2 Heavy Setup
The BlueROV2 Heavy from Blue Robotics is an ROV configured with a ”Heavy” expansion
kit. In its base configuration, the BlueROV2 features six Blue Robotics T200 thrusters (4
vectored, 2 vertical), a 1080p HD camera, and several sensors, including an accelerometer
and gyroscope.
The BlueROV2 is controlled with the open-source ArduSub software,
with the GUI ”QgroundControl”.[141]. The ”Heavy” configuration retrofit kit from Blue
Robotics equips the ROV with two additional vertical thrusters, giving it a total of four.
This provides the ROV with six degrees of freedom control and active stabilization in
roll and pitch.
In the kit is also included additional buoyancy material for improved
stability and increased lifting potential, allowing for heavier payloads. In Table 3.1 below,
a complete overview of the ROV can be found.

3.2
The Uvision Uscanner
The UScanner is a 3D underwater scanner from the company UVision, equipped with a
global shutter high definition stereo camera setup, an IMU, and a barometer. This suite of
sensors allows the built-in processer to perform point cloud generation and photogramme-
try[144], as well as estimate the location and pose of the UScanner using an onboard VIO
algorithm that runs as soon as the device is powered on. In this project, it is desired to
implement the ROVIO VIO algorithm, running it exclusively with the data obtained from
the UScanner hardware. A modular approach is desired, as this gives the opportunity
to attach the UScanner to other underwater systems, giving out-of-the-box VIO, visual
SLAM, and photogrammetry capabilities, as well as enabling high-resolution underwater
data collection.
The version of the UScanner used for the project is a hardware prototype and features an
aluminum box construction with mounting holes at the baseplate. A schematic showing
the UScanner from four angles with key measurements is provided in Figure 3.5

”ROS_MASTER_URI” environment parameter to match the address of the UScanner.
This is shown in Figure 3.6. The data is logged on a database location which is mounted
to the container.

3.4
IMU Calibration, Multi Camera Calibration and
Camera-IMU Calibration
A requirement to ROVIO and many other VIO-algorithms is that the camera intrinsic and
the camera-IMU extrinsics are known, as this allows to convert visual measurements to the
IMU fixed frame. In the case of ROVIO, the specific requirements are the intrinsic camera
matrices and distortion matrices and the extrinsic parameters between the IMU and the
two cameras. The camera-IMU extrinsic must be given as a quaternion representing the
orientation and a 3D-translation vector for each camera in the IMU-fixed frame.
Two open-source tools have been used to obtain these parameters: The Allan Variance
ROS tool[145] by Russell Buchanan, openly available on GitHub, and the Kalibr multi-
camera, camera-IMU calibration toolbox by the Autonomous Systems Lab at ETH Zurich,
also available on GitHub. The Kalibr toolbox requires the white noise and random walk
parameters for both the accelerometer and the gyroscope from the Allan Variance ROS
tool as an input to the camera-IMU calibration procedure.
The final result is the estimated noise and random walk of the IMU and the desired
extrinsic parameters of the inertial stereo camera system.
3.4.1
IMU Calibration
The presentation of the IMU noise model is taken from the online GitHub wiki page for
the tool. Kalibr assumes an IMU-noise model with a rapidly fluctuating additive white
noise term n(t) and a slowly varying bias term b(t) representing the random walk of the
sensor. Using a single axis of the gyroscope as an example, the angular rate measurement
˜ω(t) at a given timestep can be presented as
˜ω(t) = ω(t) + b(t) + n(t).
(3.1)
This model is used to independently model the IMU noise for all three axes of the gyro.
The same model, but with different values for the bias and the white noise parameters, is
used to model the IMU noise for the accelerometer.
White Noise Parameter:
The white noise represented by n(t) for the IMU-noise model
is represented by a continuous-time, white noise Gaussian process, with zero mean and
independence between random variables:
E [n(t1)n(t2)] = σ2δ(t1 −t2),
(3.2)
where σ is the strength of the process, meaning that the higher this parameter, the noisier
the measurements, and δ(t1−t2) is the Dirac delta function, which is 0 unless t1 = t2. This
reflects the “independent” nature of the noise, as there is no correlation between noise at
different times. For the gyroscope and the accelerometer noise, the strength parameter is
designated σg and σa, respectively. Given that the actual data used for the calibration is
given in discrete timesteps, this Gaussian process must be simulated in discrete time as
well, using a zero mean unit Gaussian distribution for the noise:
nd[k] = σdw[k],
(3.3)
48
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:77==
where
w[k] ∼N(0, 1),
(3.4)
σd = σ
1
√
∆t
,
(3.5)
where σd represents either σgd or σad for the gyroscope or the accelerometer, and ∆t is
the sampling time.
Bias:
Likewise, the bias term b(t) is modeled using a Brownian motion process, cor-
responding to a ”Random Walk process in discrete time.
This process is obtained by
integrating white noise
b(t) = σbw(t),
(3.6)
where σb is the strength for either the gyroscope or the accelerometer scaling w(t), which
is white noise with unit strength. As before, this process is simulated in discrete time
bd[k] = bd[k −1] + σbdw[k],
(3.7)
where
w[k] ∼N(0, 1),
(3.8)
σbd = σb
√
∆t,
(3.9)
where once again σbd represents either σbgd or σbad for the gyroscope or the accelerometer.
Recall that both the white noise and random walk are modeled independently on each
axis, meaning that, for instance, σg is the same for all axes of the gyroscope.
Obtaining the parameters
To obtain the parameters, σg, σa, σbg and σba, a standard
Allan Deviation (AD) plot is generated for the gyroscope and the accelerometer trough
the Allan Variance ROS repository. The Allan Variance method, AVARis a time domain
analysis designed for characterizing noise and stability in clock systems and is an accepted
IEEE standard for gyroscope specifications[146]. The method is based upon the principle
of cluster analysis of time series data, using increasingly large sizes of clusters by extending
the period, τ, for each cluster. By plotting the calculated AD as a function of the sam-
pling period σ(τ), it is possible to evaluate short-term noise and long-term drift. These
properties make the AVAR method suitable for characterizing the white noise and bias of
an IMU.
The following explanation of the Allan Variance Method is based on the thesis by Leslie
Barreda Pupo[146]. It has been rewritten to fit the implementation in the Allan Variance
ROS tool, which operates on data in discrete time with no cluster overlap.

parameters were obtained by considering the value of the white noise fit-line at a period
of τ = 1s, and the random rate fit-line at a period of τ = 3s, for noise and random walk
parameters respectively.

3.4.2
Camera Calibration
In addition to the IMU parameters obtained by the AVAR analysis above, Kalibr also
requires the output of a multi-camera calibration step. However, only the intrinsic camera
parameters from this calibration are used in the camera-IMU calibration. While the com-
plete calibration pipeline from Kalibr has been carried out to obtain the desired camera-
IMU extrinsics, the intrinsic camera parameters given and used with ROVIO are the ones
supplied by UVision. Consequently, the multi-camera calibration step of Kalibr will not
be discussed in detail.
52

3.4.3
Camera-IMU Calibration
Kalibr has the built-in functionality to perform camera-IMU calibration and obtain the
rotation and displacement parameters between the optical frame of the camera and the
IMU-fixed frame as needed for ROVIO. The transformation between the frames must be
given in relation to the IMU-fixed frame.
World frame (inertial
reference frame), 
IMU coordinate
frame, 
Camera coordinate
frame, 
x
z
y
x
y
z
y
x
z
Figure 3.9: The rotational relation between the three reference frames; World W, Camera
C and IMU I frames. The camera frame has origo in the optic center of the camera and
z-axis along the camera axis, with the xy-plane spanning the image plane.
In Figure 3.9, the coordinate frames for the system are shown in relation to each other.
The frames in question are the inertial world frame W, the camera coordinate frame C,
and the IMU-fixed frame I. For the camera frame, the optic center is along the z-axis,
where the xy-plane that spans the image plane has the orientation of x-axis to the right
and y-axis down. The orientation of the IMU-fixed frame in the figure is shown as it is
implemented in the UScanner with the y-axis pointing down, the x-axis pointing left, and
the z-axis pointing backward.
The camera-IMU calibration method employed by Kalibr is taken from [147], which
presents a novel framework for jointly estimating the temporal and spatial offsets of dif-
ferent sensors, using continuous time batch estimation and maximum likelihood estima-
tion[147]. For a camera-IMU system, the estimated parameters are:
1. The gravity direction Wg in the inertial world frame.
2. The transformation between the camera and the IMU, TC,I,
3. The time offset, d, between the camera and the IMU.
4. The pose of the IMU,TW,I(t), at time t.
5. The accelerometer and gyroscope biases, bα(t),bω(t), at time t.
For the case of the camera-IMU calibration, the accelerometer and gyroscope biases are
not of interest and are, therefore, ignored for the remainder of the explanation.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
53
==PAGE:82==
The representation TW,I(t), which is a time-varying state, is achieved through B-splines,
which produce simple analytical functions of time with good representational power[147].
These B-splines take on the role of basis functions that can be used to represent any
time-varying state. Consider the general case of the D-dimensional state x(t), that can be
written as
x(t) = Φ(t)c,
(3.14)
Φ(t) =

ϕ1(t)
. . .
ϕB(t)

,
(3.15)
where Equation (3.15) contain B known basis functions ϕb(t), each a D × 1 analytical
function of time. In this manner, Φ(t) is a D × B stacked matrix of basis functions. To
estimate a state x(t), all that is required is to estimate the B×1 coeﬀicient vector c. Using
the notion of B-splines as basis functions, the pose of the IMU can be written as
TW,I(t) =
C (φ(t))
t(t)
0⊤
1

,
(3.16)
where φ(t) = Φφ(t)cφ encodes rotation parameters, and C is a function that creates a
rotation matrix from the encoded parameters.
Likewise, t(t) = Φt(t)ct represents the
translation. For a total of six degrees of freedom, using three for orientation and three for
translation, the IMU pose is thus parameterized by a 6 × 1 spline.
Consider now the pixel location ymj of a given landmark (a tracked point in the image,
i.e. the corner of an AprilTag) Wpm within the set of M landmarks, {Wpm|m = 1...M},
at time index tj + d, where j is the index of the image and d is an unknown time offset.
Then, a measurement equation can be expressed:
ymj = h
 TC,ITW,I(tj + d)−1Wpm

+ nymj,
(3.17)
where nymj is statistically independent added measurement noise, n ∼N(0, R), and h is the
nonlinear camera model. Note that Equation (3.17) is written with the assumption that
the image measurements are delayed with respect to the IMU measurements. However,
this notation is purely out of convenience as it is easier to write out and implement delayed
image terms[147]. With the measurement equation formulated, the desired quantities can
be estimated by writing the error term associated with the measurement, expressed as
the difference between the obtained measurement and predicted measurement, given the
currently estimated state, omitting the measurement noise:
eymj = ymj −h
 TC,ITW,I(tj + d)−1Wpm

(3.18)
Summing over all J images and all M landmarks and weighing the residuals with the
inverse covariance matrix to ensure high confidence in the measurements (leading to a
small noise variance) increases the impact of the residuals and vice versa, the objective
function can be expressed as
Jy = 1

As mentioned, the above explanation is concerned entirely on obtaining the relationship
between the camera and the IMU, and thus Jy is only part of the overall objective function,
which in the paper is expressed as:
J = Jy + Jα + Jω + Jba + Jbw,
(3.20)
where Jα and Jω is the objective functions for the accelerometer and gyroscope measure-
ments and Jbα and Jbω and are objective functions for their respective biases. For more
details, see [147]. The Levenberg-Marquard algorithm is then used to minimize Equa-
tion (3.20), obtaining the MLE estimate of all the parameters at once.

Obtaining the Parameters
The UScanner was mounted on a pole1 and placed in the pool in ASTA, with a board
featuring April tags kept stationary at the opposite side of the pool. Then, as required by
the Kalibr toolbox, all axes of the IMU were excited by moving the UScanner. A ROS-bag
of duration 54.3 seconds was recorded with a rate of 9.98 Hz and 9.8 Hz for the left and
right camera respectively, and an IMU rate of 402.58 Hz. In Figure 3.10 below, a left-right

3.5
Ultra-short Baseline System
Electromagnetic energy is known to have limited propagation in water, particularly at
higher frequencies. As a result, localization methods like GNSS are not suitable for ob-
taining the absolute position of an underwater vehicle. Instead, various acoustic ranging
techniques such as long baseline (LBL) and Ultra-short baseline (USBL) have been pri-
marily employed for tracking underwater vehicles [148].
1Full setup will be described in Section 4.4 and can be seen in Figure 4.33.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
55
==PAGE:84==
Figure 3.11: Different beacon-based under-
water localization techniques.
(a) Conven-
tional (two-way) long baseline (LBL) naviga-
tion; (b) time-synchronized (one-way) LBL;
(c) GPS-buoy navigation;
(d) Ultra-short
baseline (USBL) navigation. Figure and fig-
ure text from [148].
Both LBL and USBL can be categorized as
beacon-based underwater localization tech-
niques and are both very similar in the
way they operate.
A classic setup of a
LBL-configuration as the one shown in Fig-
ure 3.11 has two or more beacons (acous-
tic transponders) deployed in the area of
operation. These beacons listen to acous-
tic query pings emitted by a transceiver,
which when received, waits for a unique
turn around time (TAT) and replies on
its individual transmit channel. Using dif-
ferent TATs for each beacon do not in-
terfere with each other.
Different trans-
mit frequencies are also used to identify
which beacon the signal came from. Hav-
ing this setup of multiple transponders and
one transceiver enables positioning of the
AUV using trilateration. This is done by using the time difference between emission of
query ping and receiving a reply ∆ti to determine the one-way travel-time (OWTT) which
can be calculated by:
tOWTT
i
= ∆ti–tTAT
i
2
,
Which can be used to calculate the distance di between beacon i and the transceiver by
utilizing the speed of sound c in water:
di =
c
tOWTT
i
.
The accuracy of such system depends on the frequency band used for the ping. Low fre-
quency pings can travel great distances but have larger absolute position error compared
to using high frequency. A USBL system like the one illustrated in Figure 3.11d is very
similar to the LBL system as it still consists of the interaction between transponder (bea-
con) and transceiver. However, the transceiver is different as it contains several receiving
elements that are very close to each other, hence the “Ultra-short baseline”. This allows
the system to work with a single transponder, given that we know the position of either
the transponder or the transceiver. This position can be achieved by knowing the relative
position to a nearby GNSS receiver or if it is placed statically in a known position [148].
3.5.1
The Micro-Ranger 2 USBL System
For this study, a Sonardyne Micro-Ranger 2 USBL system has been provided for tracking
the absolute position of the AUV below water. The system consists of one transceiver, two
wireless transponders, and a Sonardyne Ethernet Serial Hub (ESH). As illustrated in the
proposed USBL connection diagram in Figure 3.12, the ESH will be used as a link between
the 3 different PC’s used in the setup. The additional ”Micro-Ranger Laptop” is needed
because the proprietary software requires a Microsoft Windows OS to run. Furthermore,
a GNSS receiver is connected to the Micro-Ranger Laptop to set the position of the Micro-
Ranger (MR) Transceiver2.
Sonardyne claims that the system has a system accuracy of < 5% slant range, where slant
range is the distance between the transceiver and transponder. If excluding the error from
2Note that for the second data collection campaign, an Arduino was programmed to act as a GNSS
receiver using a fixed predefined coordinate. See Section 3.5.1.
56
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

COM 4
Sonardyne ESH Switch

Microranger Laptop
IP: 192.168.179.100
GNSS Receiver
or Arduino with
static message
Address: 2209
Address: 2513

IP: 192.168.179.120

Figure 3.12: The proposed setup for the USBL system.
An external power supply is
needed for the Sonardyne ESH switch, which connects the transceiver and the operating
laptop. A GNSS receiver must be connected to the system for the software to operate.
GNSS, heading, pitch, and roll, the accuracy should be < 3.5% of the slant range. The
range of the system is, at maximum, 995 meters and operates in frequencies between 19-
34 kHz [149]. The full specifications of the Micro-Ranger 2 can be found in Appendix A
where the features, performance assessment, and inventory list are included.
The Micro-Ranger software can report the position of the transponder(s) in NMEA 0183
(see Section 2.6.3) messages through UDP interfaces, which can be recorded through either
one of their proprietary tools or other UDP-compatible services.
Using a fixed coordinate for GNSS location
Figure
3.13:
”Point
2”
from [13].
One issue with using a GNSS antenna, especially in a GNSS-
denied area like Sydhavnen, is that it adds additional un-
certainty to the system as signal strength and accuracy will
likely vary over time. Therefore, using static known coordi-
nates as the location of the USBL instead is preferable. At
Sydhavnen, [13] collected several well-placed reference points
where the location has been measured using Real-time kine-
matic positioning (RTK)-measurement equipment down to
centimetre accuracy. Therefore, placing the USBL at one or
relatively close to one is preferred in order to ensure quality
of the measurements.
Due to the system delivered by Sonardyne not allowing one
to input static GNSS coordinates into the system through
the MR-software, a workaround is needed.
The employed
solution is to have an Arduino Nano with a Real-time Clock
(RTC) module connect to the PC using USB. The Arduino
has, prior to deployment, been programmed to publish serial
messages in the NMEA 0183 GLL format described earlier
in Section 2.6.3. The message contains, most importantly, the pre-defined location along
with a timestamp. The RTC module should ensure that time is kept when disconnected.
The code written for the Arduino Nano can be found in Appendix B.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
57
==PAGE:86==
System Issues
It is important to note that issues with the transceiver communication were encountered.
Specifically, communication with the transponders stops periodically, which appears to be
related to the device realigning its internal compass and/or gyroscope. Unfortunately, this
issue could not be resolved, and it is suspected that the device requires servicing.
The transponder with address 2513 seemed to be lees consistent compared to the other
transponder provided. Therefore, this one was not used during experiment.
Device Mounts
For mounting the transceiver and transponders, PLA 3D-printed mounting solutions were
designed together with Marin technician Rune Garmund.

are in Appendix J.
3.6
Discussion
As most VIO and visual SLAM algorithms require the IMU-camera system to be well
calibrated, a substation part of the efforts regarding the ROV was centered on obtaining
the calibration parameters for the UVision UScanner. A first attempt was made with
the UScanner out of the water; however, due to internal settings that were not possible
to change, the above-water recordings made by the UScanner are blurred by the internal
denoising algorithm. This led to quite poor calibrations, as Kalibr could only extract a few
usable measurements of the board from each recording. This, therefore, necessitated that
the recordings of the AprilTag board were done below the waterline. However, while no
longer blurry, the underwater recordings of the AprilTag board were not as comprehensive
as might be desired for good calibrations. This was due to the fact that the board had
to be placed on the wall at one side of the pool, and the UScanner moved in front of it
while held by a pole at the other side. Therefore, complete coverage of the camera FOV
as recommended by Kalibr was not possible, and the amount of skewing views and varied
distances to the board was limited. Therefore, while usable calibration parameters were
found for ROVIO, there is still work to be done in improving these calibrations, which could
lead to better performance and minimize projection errors between the camera frames.
An additional limitation of the UScanner is that magnetometer data is not available due to
its metal construction. Therefore, to align estimated trajectories with the world coordinate
frame, the magnetometer data from the BlueROV is used. This somewhat goes against
58
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:87==
the notion of having a completely self-contained ”out of the box” VIO / visual SLAM
system. However, this alignment could just as well be made by knowing the initial position,
including orientation, of the ROV/UScanner at the time of deployment.
Working with the UScanner, which is a closed third-party system, did not come without its
implications. Most notably, there is no control over the update rate for the built-in sensors,
and there is no exact knowledge of the placement of the cameras in relation to the IMU,
which could have aided in the calibrations. The closed system of the UScanner furthermore
posed a problem when building the containerized, ROS-based recording application, as
some internal network settings of the device had to be altered by UVision themselves to
allow for access to the published ROS-topics.
Using the Sonardyne USBL system came with a few initial complications. First, a dedi-
cated machine needed to be set up due to it only supporting Microsoft Windows. Secondly,
reliability issues with both the transceiver and one of the transponders were encountered
and not resolved. However, the system has been reckoned to work consistently enough for
collecting reference data. For future deployment, a service of the system is needed, and
calibration of the internal sensors is needed.
3.7
Chapter Summary
Conclusively, the following has been addressed, which enables operation of the BlueROV2
Heavy with the UScanner system as part of the sensor payload:
• A modified payload skid that enables secure and adjustable mounting of the UVision
sensor in addition to buoyancy calibration.
• UScanner calibrated with IMU and IMU to stereo camera calibration pipeline.
• Docker container with data recording capabilities in ROS Noetic for the ROV and
UScanner.
• Setup and operation of the USBL system have been addressed.

==PAGE:88==
4
Data Acquisition
The following chapter will describe the data acquisition campaigns conducted during the
project period. Each section will conclude with a discussion section, which will provide
an analysis of the collected data, discuss important observations, and address, if required,
additional processing for the experiment.
All experiments have occurred in a controlled environment in the Autonomous Systems
Test Arena (ASTA) pool or at Belvedere Canal in Sydhavnen.
The main reason for
choosing this canal is that it allows comparison with previous DTU projects [15][13] where
the latter has conveniently provided RTK reference points that allow for point to point
evaluation.
4.1
Preliminary Data Acquisition

A preliminary campaign was conducted before the main data ac-
quisition campaign. The initial aim of this first campaign was to
acquire sensor data from both the USV and ROV in the Belvedere
Canal, Sydhavn. However, due to unforeseen practical issues, a
simpler initial campaign was conducted to obtain initial data,
gain experience with data collection experiments, and discover
potential flaws in the system configuration at that time.
The practical problems that were encountered just days before
the date of the first campaign were;
• Failure on the starboard rotor of the USV.
• Issues acquiring GNSS data from the USV.
As such, adjustments to the experiment plan had to be made,
mainly concerning the configuration of the two FLSs, the IMU,
and the area of data collection. The complete experiment plan
can be found in Appendix E.5.
4.1.1
Preparation
Most of the preparations for this setup involved adapting the en-
tire experiment to the limitations introduced by the unavailability
of the USV. The following had to be reconsidered;
• How the SONARs are mounted and operated without an
USV.
• Which parts of the location are feasible to survey with the
new setup.
• What sensors can still be included in the setup.
To move the SONARs around safely, an extension of the original solution was added,
giving the operator additional reach when scanning the seabed. As seen in Figure 4.1,
this results in a total length of 4.02 meters, making it possible to submerge the sonar and
the mounted transponder below water level without risking submerging the IMU. It also
makes the system safer to operate as the operator can walk and stand straight while doing
60
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:89==
so. A new mount was created for the IMU, printed in PLA using a 3D printer. One view
of the CAD is shown in Figure 4.2.
Figure 4.2: Mount for attaching the
IMU on the pole.
As the setup was designed to be operated on the
USV with the capacity for carrying the payload and
batteries on board, additional personnel now had
to carry the payload and a portable power bank
around close to the pole, as the USB cable for the
IMU limited the distance between payload and pole
to 2.9 meters. As a result, the survey area had to
be confined to a smaller region, around point B in
Figure 4.3. Finally, it was decided that the LiDAR
could not be included in this setup as no solution on
how to mount it safely on the pole could be made
within the given time frame.
One part of the experiment was also to deploy both
the USBL system and the ROV. Before this cam-
paign, an initial test using both ROV and the US-
canner was conducted in the pool in ASTA where it
was possible to retrieve data from the ROV using the MavLink logger, through QGround-
Control, and from the scanner using the UController software.
At this time of devel-
opment, obtaining the data through ROS was not accomplished. Finally, the route was
re-planned to be more feasible with the limited setup, and the points further down into
the canal were removed from the plan. The resulting map can be viewed in Figure 4.3.

When arriving, it was quickly realized that there was no reason to unload the car at the
originally intended parking spot. Instead, the car was parked closer to deployment and
afterwards driven to another parking lot nearby. Furthermore, both OOIs were deployed
from the bridge, isolating them from surrounding objects and placing them closer to where
the pole could be operated. The new positions along with updated checkpoints are found
in Figure 4.4. It was decided to remove the most eastern checkpoint, as there is no direct
path between that and the USBL transceiver. The pole was operated between checkpoints
1 and 2, while the ROV was operated between all three checkpoints shown in Figure 4.4.
When performing the experiment, numerous complications occurred that affected the data
collection. The noted complications were:
IMU Cable Length The IMU cable was too short, forcing the
payload box to be carried close to the pole and waterfront.
If future sonar-pole experiments are conducted, an USB
extender cord will be used for the Xsens IMU.
Improvised Pole Setup Assembling the improvised pole setup
took longer than expected.
Payload Cable Security Payload
cables
weren’t
secured
against sudden pulls, requiring someone to manually
hold them in place.
For future deployments, a more
robust securing mechanism should be implemented.
Tool Availability An M3 key was not available on-site; however
a colleague was able to provide one.
Angle Measurement An angle-measurement tool was missing.
Instead, the dual FLS mount angle was estimated using
a measuring tape and simple trigonometry.
FLS Mount Distance Distance between the FLS mount and
IMU might be off by a few centimeters, as the IMU was
removed before measuring, but markings helped minimize
the uncertainty.
FLS Mount Rotation The 3D-printed threads in the pole
didn’t prevent rotation of the FLS mount, adding un-
certainty to its position relative to the IMU.
UVision Camera Fogging The Uvision camera lenses fogged up
at the start of the ROV deployment; allowing them to
remain in the water for a warm-up period before the ex-
periment may help mitigate this issue in the future.
Figure
4.5:

the

on,

Power Supply An extra power socket would have simplified the setup.
Data Storage Data was saved on multiple computers; ideally, everything should be stored
in one rosbag.
USBL Calibration We didn’t define Center Reference Point and reference direction for the
USBL, causing inaccuracies in relative distances between the transceiver and GNSS
receiver.
USBL Mount The USBL mount was unstable and improvised, as seen in Figure 4.5.
USBL Data Logging USBL data logging through CommsViewer was unreliable, possibly
stopping before the end of the BlueROV deployment.
Sensor Auto-Recalibration Compass and gyroscope auto-recalibrations caused data col-
lection interruptions.

==PAGE:92==
Checklists Checklists and guides were not followed to a satisfactory degree.
IMU Disconnection The IMU lost connection early in the mission, possibly due to the
short cable being pulled.
Despite these complications, the experiment also had several successes. First of all, the
pole setup was successfully assembled and deployed using the sensor fusion payload to
control the sensors and log the data. The ROV was also successfully deployed and data
was recorded using the UController. Both OOIs were captured by the UScanner as well as
the two SONARs. The USBL system was operational and used to collect positional data.

ROV and UScanner
The data obtained from the BlueROV2 Heavy and the UScanner is not in a ROS-bag
format, but rather downloaded from the QGroundControl software and the UScanner in
proprietary formats. For the ROV itself, QGroundControl will log the telemetry data for
the session, as soon as a connection to the vehicle is established. This data is saved in a
log file, with file extension .tlog, and contains information about the timestamp, depth,
vibration, IMU, attitude, pressure and groundspeed captured from the internal sensors
of the ROV. This .tlog file was converted to a /texttt.csv file by a modified parser
66
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:95==
python script. The data from the UScanner is downloaded as an archive directly from the
UScanner, by the means of UVisions internal UController software. The file structure of
the archive is depicted in Appendix E.1, Figure E.1.
Furthermore, the baro.csv file does not contain any data.
The images contained in
the images are named after the set title for an experiment in the UController software,
followed by the the timestamp for when the experiment was started, and finally with
an index and a letter designating whether the image was captured by the left (L) or
right (R) camera. The remaining files of interest are the imu.csv and the odometry.txt
that respectively contain the captured output from the internal IMU and the estimated
trajectory from a VIO algorithm running on the UScanner. It should be noted that all
observations within the imu.csv and odometry.txt are not specified with a timestamp
either but rather associated with a filename of a specific image. In this manner, for this
method of collecting data from the UScanner, the rate of the IMU is tied to the rate of
the camera. Specific information regarding the content of the obtained files can be found
in Table 4.5. Furthermore, the availability of the telemetry data from QGroundControl
can be found in Figure 4.11.
Table 4.5: The table shows the content of the files obtained from the ROV and the UScan-
ner at the experiment, in terms of a description, frequency and number of observations.
The frequencies are obtained by averaging over observations.

Inspecting the images obtained from the underwater survey reveals that fog on the lenses
of the UScanner blurred the image slightly. This fog is caused by the hot internals of
the UScanner, heating the air inside the casing. When the lenses of the casing come into
contact with cold air or water, a level of condensation forms on the clear plastic lenses. This
fog will dissipate over time but can still pose an issue in terms of the quality of the images.
In Figure 4.12, a set of images from the left and right camera can be seen. The image
from the left camera in Figure 4.12a has been chosen to illustrate the somewhat blurry
condition of the image caused by the aforementioned condensation just as the UScanner
is submerged in the cold water, while the image from the right camera in Figure 4.12b
has been chosen to show a situation in which there are no discernable features, which are
suspected to pose a challenging case for VIO.
