scenario without any discernable features in the image.
4.1.4
Discussion
This first data campaign has given several key insights into what had to be improved
in the setup before moving on to a second data campaign. In general, it was noted that
assembling takes longer than expected, and would take possibly even longer when using the
USV. On that same subject, it is important to specify the exact tools to bring and follow
the prepared plans and guides thoroughly. Secondly, it was found that the USBL system
takes time to set up as well and that a more robust solution for mounting the transceiver
should be constructed.
Using a GNSS receiver for a static setup induces unnecessary
uncertainty in the system.
When inspecting the data posted to the campaign and trying to apply it to the developed
methods, several problems arose.
The BlueView SONARS’s ROS node was too slow,
leading to a low and inconsistent update rate and potential latencies. Furthermore, the
missing IMU data means that the rotation of the SONARs cannot be estimated, making
mapping diﬀicult.
The missing LiDAR data is also an issue, making additional data
collection with this modality necessary.
Having retrieved the collected ROV data and its structure, it has become clear that the
output from the UController recording software is completely unsuitable for use with a
ROS based VIO algorithm. Most critical is the low sampling rate for the images and by
extension the IMU, and the lack of accurate timestamps. Efforts to extract timestamps
from the image to a second-level accuracy have not been successful. Finally, the imu.csv
file is not annotated with headers, and therefore, attempting to extract the values from the
file would require some level of guesswork and assumption in regards to exactly what the
values in the data represent. One suggestion could be to use the IMU on the BlueROV2
Heavy itself, which has an update frequency of almost 10 Hz; however, the UScanner
was not accurately time-synced with the ROV, posing a considerable uncertainty when
attempting to combine the images with the IMU data, and this approach still does not
solve the issue with the images missing accurate timestamps. In addition to these issues
with the structuring of the data itself, the foggy lenses of the UScanner degrade the quality
of the images.
68
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:97==
4.2
Primary Data Acquisition
Less than two months after the first data campaign, a second data campaign was con-
ducted. Meanwhile, the USV thruster has been repaired, and numerous improvements
have been made on the software side and in the setup of the USBL system.
The aim of the primary data acquisition campaign was to obtain data in a well-known
maritime environment, enabling validation of the proposed system architecture. During
this campaign, the authors were accompanied by Nicholas Hansen, postdoc, who was of
great assistance during the whole campaign.
4.2.1
Preparation
In preparation for the experiment, both an experiment plan (Appendix E.8) and a checklist
of everything needed for deployment (Appendix E.9) were created. As part of the experi-
ment plan, a map of the most important waypoints for the mission was created, which can
be found in Figure 4.13. As the USV structure is large and heavy, assembling had to be
done near the water-surface with nearby car-parking area. Previous campaigns conducted
by DTU showed that using the canoe dock at location A, as shown in Figure 4.13 has been
suitable. The USV can be remotely steered to location (B). The experiment checkpoints
are placed inside the canal as there are known reference points in this area. The transform
configuration for the experiment utilizes the transform tree structure and node presented
in [13]. A plot of the transform can be found in Appendix E.3.
Figure 4.13: Overview of the area and important spots for the mission. The image in
the top right corner is taken by Google user Jens Peter Hansen for Google Streetview.
Background map is from [150].
Mounting the USBL Transceiver
The preliminary campaign revealed the need to rethink the USBL system’s implementa-
tion. The mount consisted of two wooden pieces, a 3D-printed pole lock (Figure 3.14b),
wooden blocks for seperation adjustment, and clamps for securing it to the bridge rail.
Images of the setup are found in Figure 4.14. As described in Section 3.5.1, a known fixed
point was used as GNSS reference point, removing the varying error induced from using
a GNSS receiver.

As the USV traveled from point A to point
B (see Figure 4.13), one of its rotors caught

experiment. The setup at point B was fur-

made it necessary to cover the ”control cen-

were hanged from the bridge as it made
it easier to capture them with the sonar
patch.

the opposing wall from the bathing pier,

point C (see Figure 4.16), due to a limited
tether length for the UScanner that would
prohibit surveys along the wall.

71
==PAGE:100==
Prior to object deployment, an initial clockwise survey was conducted along the canal
walls using the USV, beginning and ending at point B. Subsequently, an Arla milk crate
was deployed at point a, while a cylindrical object was positioned at point b. The new
positions have been marked in Figure 4.16. Following an additional USV survey run, a
lawnmower pattern was executed to cover the area in front of the bathing pier, extending
from the bridge to the open waters.
As the deployment of the ROV relied on the completion of the USV experiment, in the
meanwhile the equipment was moved from point B to point C. Due to wiring constraints,
the setup could only be moved in short increments from point C to the area between
the two OOIs.
Hereafter, a preliminary survey was conducted until it was discovered
that there was insuﬀicient storage space on the computer. An external hard-drive was
connected and the survey began again from 15.32 CET. Here multiple runs with both the
ROV facing the wall perpendicular and forward to the wall was conducted between the
two OOIs. However, the setting sun at 15:39 CET limited the visual maneuverability of
the ROV. Subsequently, the ROV was guided to the bathing pier, where all equipment
was disassembled and packed into the van.

USBL System
For this experiment, USBL was used to localize both the USV and the ROV. When de-
ploying, it was known that the heading was not correctly initialized as the automatic
heading was disabled to remove uncertainty introduced from using a magnetometer. In-
stead, a heading has been imposed post to the experiment as the direction of the USBL
was observed to be parallel to the bridge in the eastern direction, which means a direction
of 108.94 degrees. During data analysis, it became clear that this approximation was not
suﬀicient enough, as the observed data simply didn’t match the channel. Having adjusted
the angle to 127.4 degrees by manually inspecting the data, meaningful observations are
obtained and are visualized in Figure 4.23. It was investigated whether the GNSS data
could be used to adjust the USBL measurements by minimizing the distance between
recordings at corresponding timestamps. This did not yield better result.
(a) USV USBL locations.
(b) ROV USBL data.
Figure 4.23: Obtained USBL data, which rotation has been corrected manually.
Inspecting the availability of the USBL trough Figure 4.24 and Figure 4.23 shows larger
sections of missing data on the USV. This is possibly due to the mounting position as
the signal could be obstructed by the platoons and the aluminum pole on which it was
mounted. In Figure 4.23a, some observation are placed further into and outside of the
canal than expected. As illustrated in Figure 4.16, the USV never passes the second bridge
in the main canal. The distance overshoot could be caused by reflections on the walls and
poor calibration of VoS in water, which was left at the default value of 1500 m/s.

ROV and UScanner
During this acquisition campaign, data was collected from the BlueROV2 Heavy and the
UVision UScaner through ROS in a shared rosbag, with much higher update frequencies
for the images and IMU-messages from the UScanner.
Running the data acquisition
of the UScanner in ROS allowed for syncronized timestamps between the images and
IMU messages enabling the use of recorded runs to implement VIO. This furthermore
eliminated the need for using the proprietary UController tool, and the extraction of
telemetry data from QGroundControl. The data was recorded over multiple rosbags of a
duration of one minute per recording. This was done deliberately to split the recording
into smaller bags for easy handling, as the recorded data had a size of approximately 5.6
gigabytes per minute. A total of 30 rosbags were recorded, giving a total recording time
of exactly 30 minutes. Figure 4.25 shows a timeline of the data availability for each ROS-
topic. Figure 4.25 also depicts the timestamps where the survey of useful data started and
stopped. In total, 12 min. and 8 sec. of valid data was extracted from the overall data
acquisition campaign as a survey.
Figure 4.25: The availability of the data throughout the total duration of the data acqui-
sition campaign. The green lines indicate the start and stop times for the actual survey,
ie. where the data has been deemed usable.
Though many more topics were recorded through the MAVROS interface for the ROV, and
also from the UScanner itself publishing data to a lot of internal ROS-nodes and processes,
the Figure 4.25 only depicts the topics relevant for further work. More specifically, it is the
images, IMU and raw barometer reading from the UScanner, and the magnetometer data

4.2.4
Discussion
Despite the efforts of SONAR calibration at the initial USV deployment, several potential
problems are observed when reviewing the data post-experiment. Here, Primarily a high
gain setting that led to over-saturated images, necessitating downstream image correction.
The use of ROS for the ROV survey proved to be effective in the field, enabling data
inspection in real time and with syncronized timestamps between ROV and UScanner.
Recording all topics from both devices without compression generates large amounts of
unused data. Future experiments can avoid this by being selective about which topics to
record. In this case, due to uncertainty about the essential data for the VIO algorithm,
all available information as a precautionary measure.
However, the main lesson from the preliminary experiment that things take longer than
78
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:107==
expected, also proved true here as well, and unfortunately resulted in a subpar collection
of underwater ROV data. A multitude of factors contributed to these circumstances: The
data acquisition campaign was conducted on one of the shortest days of the years, giving
limited amounts of sunlight, and shortly before the end of the USV survey it was decided
that the ROV should be deployed from point C instead of B as originally planned to provide
recordings in an environment richer in features. Moving ROV and associated equipment
and requiring setting up once more added more work to an already tight schedule, and
it is estimated that this decision postponed the ROV survey with about 30-45 minutes.
This, unfortunately, renders the raw images less useful due to darkness limiting visibility.
A key issue arising from the relocation of the OOIs is the depth limitation caused by rope
constraints. This stems from the height difference between the bridge and the bulwark.
In the new deployment position the OOIs were of insuﬀicient depth to be detected by the
sonar patch. However, even if adequate rope was available from this new location there
would have been a trade-off between the depth of the OOIs and the visibility due to the
lack of lights on the ROV.
An investigation of the USBL system raised concerns about validity of the acquired data as
ground truth for localization. The internal heading estimation of the transceiver was not
used as a static heading was prefered. However, the estimation proved to be insuﬀicient
requiring adjustment through data inspection which is suboptimal. In future deployment,
a procedure for heading estimation should be used. A proposed procedure could be to
place transponders at known reference points and perform a series of measurements. This
would also provide a way of addressing the accuracy of the system.
Although the USV operated within the general vicinity of the canal, it registered points
from outside the designated survey area, north from checkpoint 4 and 5 in Figure 4.13 and
a general displacement in the area around checkpoint 3 towards the center of the canal.
Additionally, the ROV data indicated a systematic drift away from the wall in the bridge
area.
4.3
Dual SONARs in the ASTA Pool
Data collected in a controlled environment can be used to calibrate, verify, and fine-tune
the proposed setup and methods. Therefore, two experiments, one for the SONARs and
one for the UScanner were conducted in the pool facilities of the ASTA. In this section,
the SONAR experiment will be described in detail. The full experiment plan can be found
in Appendix E.6. The experiment was conducted on the basis of questions that had arisen
when using the collected data in the primary data campaign. The aim of the experiment
was to address the following;
1. The positional alignment between the two SONARs.
2. Collect data that enables to calibrate the alignment between the two SONARs.
3. Clarify what is/were causing artifacts on the SONAR images.
4. Obtain SONAR data in a known environment, enabling reconstruction of well-known
geometries.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
79
==PAGE:108==
4.3.1
Preparation

All relevant measurements are noted.
Unit of measurements is meters.

Origo
Figure 4.27: Setup of the pool experiment with all measurements given in meters.
To simulate similar environments in this experiment and the stereo vision experiment
Section 4.4, six objects were deployed in the pool, two of which are cylinders that are 3
meters tall and therefore, stretch from the bottom of the pool to the surface of the water.
These two objects are vital for this experiment, as they can be used as references when
evaluating the performance of the methods. A top-view illustration of the pool setup and
a side view of how the sensors are mounted is shown in Figure 4.27.
The OptiTrack motion capture system was used to ensure accurate tracking of the pole’s
position throughout the experiment. This system was configured to monitor both the
rotation and position of the pole by tracking a marker structure mounted on top, initialized
as a virtual rigid body in the motion capture software.
Several SONAR configurations were planned to meet the experiment objectives.
The
SONARs were first mounted in two different orientations: one at 0◦(facing straight for-
ward) and another titled 40◦downwards, replicating the setup used in the primary data
campaign. For each configuration, preparations were made to conduct steady-state record-
ings as well as recordings during rotational movement around the pole’s center. These
recordings were expected to facilitate positional alignment verification and potential cali-
bration of the setup.
80

while the SONARs were in motion. In this setup,
the SONARs would be oriented forward, and a col-

mount in between the two pieces of wood can be
loosened in place, making it possible to rotate and
move the pole in the vertical direction. This was
utilized when rotating the pole from side to side.
4.3.2
Experiment
The experiment proceeded as planned without major issues. Precise measurements of the
pool and cylinder positions took time, and attention was given to calibration and real-time
data analysis.
One issue encountered was that initially, it was not possible to mount the sonars at the
0◦configuration, as a fastening lever was mounted on the wrong side. Therefore, parts
of the mount had to be disassembled and reattached in the right configuration. This was
after recordings of the 40-degree configuration were done, meaning that the position of
the setup might have altered slightly between the two phases of the experiment.

4.3.4
Discussion
This experiment has provided useful data for further validation of the setup, and immediate
data exploration has provided meaningful insights on calibration and artifacts. Apart from
the already observed artifacts, new issues appeared due to the confined space of the pool,
causing reflecting.
The Optitrack data needs additional postprocessing, which will be addressed at the end
of this chapter.

4.4
UScanner in the ASTA Pool
Figure 4.32:
The BlueROV2 pay-
load skid with attached metal frame
with an adjustable gimbal mount
Like the SONARs, the UScanner was deployed in
the controlled environment of the ASTA pool, to
collect data in a suﬀiciently lit, known underwa-
ter environment.
Conducting a survey in a less
challenging environment enables verification of the
calibration and direct observation of how chang-
ing parameter values for the VIO algorithm affects
tracking and localization performance.
Addition-
ally, when combined with the OptiTrack motion
capture system, such a survey allows for accurately
quantifying errors and evaluating the overall perfor-
mance of the algorithm over time and distance and
furthermore allows for firsthand detection of any limitations when having to rely exclu-
sively on inertial navigation in, even brief, periods of no available visual features.
The experiment consists of attaching the UScanner to a metal pole, as can be seen in
Figure 4.33 and moving it along a predetermined trajectory in the pool. The aim of this
experiment was to obtain the following:
1. A survey of underwater data where the UScanner is moved along a trajectory that
has periods of both sparse and abundant amounts of features.
2. A baseline of the placement of the UScanner in the pool by using the Optitrack
System for both trajectories.
4.4.1
Preparation
The setup for the experiment in the pool is identical to that of the SONAR experiment,
with the exception of the cylinder with the black base in the middle of the pool, which in
Figure 4.27 is directly in front of the sonars. The UScanner was kept in the BlueROV2
payload skid that had been used in Sydhavnen, and a frame with an adjustable gimbal
mount that could be screwed into a pole was constructed. The payload skid and frame
construction can be seen in Figure 4.32.
This allowed for easy mounting of a pole with OptiTrack motion capture markers. The
entire portable setup can be seen in Figure 4.33, and was specifically designed to make it
possible to submerge the UScanner in the pool and walk with it along the edge, simulating
a ROV survey.
The trajectory that the UScanner should traverse can be seen in Figure 4.34 and was
created to simulate the realistic movement of a ROV with an attached UScanner payload,
pointing it forward in the direction of travel at all times.
Going around corners and
objects, realistic turning was emphasized, meaning that sharp 90-degree turns and quick,
jagged rotations in place around the pole were avoided, with the only exception being
at the upper left corner where the UScanner turns 180 degrees, and even here, slow and
fluid movements of consistent speed were performed. The full trajectory can be seen in
Figure 4.34, with the outgoing part of the trajectory in green and the returning part in
red.
The actual placement of the objects in the water was done with great consideration to
simulate a structured underwater environment with clear and distinct visual features,
giving the VIO algorithm the best possible opportunity for detection and tracking. It
is for this reason that many of the objects are placed in the corners of the pool, as this
ensures suﬀicient visual features when the UScanner makes 90-degree turns near the walls.
84
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:113==

Stairs
Origo
X

Without these, the algorithm would experience extended periods without visual input,
leading to dead reckoning, relying exclusively on inertial measurements.
However, observing how well the algorithm performs dead reckoning on inertial measure-
ments remains an interesting case to investigate. Therefore, during one 90◦turn on the
return part of the trajectory, marked by the black circle in Figure 4.34, the UScanner faces
the white wall at close range while completing the turn. An example of the captured data
by the UScanner in this specific scenario is shown in Figure 4.35.
Figure 4.35:
An example of cap-

during the 90◦counter-
clockwise turn with no distinguish-
able visual features.
As with the SONAR experiment, the OptiTrack sys-
tem was used to create a baseline for the UScanner.
In the system software, a virtual rigid body was
created, consisting of the motion capture markers
placed on the top of the pole. The actual position
of the UScanner at the edge of the pole was obtained
by making the transformation seen in Figure 4.33,
ie. applying the currently tracked orientation to the
transformation vector and then using the vector to
translate the tracked position of the rigid body to a
position below the pool surface.
As mentioned in the previous experiment with a
dual sonar configuration, the tracked orientation is
noise at the edges of the OptiTrack system. This small noise is greatly amplified by this
transformation from the top to the bottom of the pole. In Section 4.5, this issue will be
addressed.
4.4.2
Experiments
Prior to the date of the experiment in ASTA that provided the data used in the project,
two other attempts were made. These two attempts are outlined briefly, describing their
outcomes and how the learnings from one influenced the subsequent before the final exper-
iment is presented in greater detail. The experiment plan can be found in Appendix E.7.
Experiment 1: December 19, 2024
On December 19, 2024, the first experiment with the UScanner on a pole was commenced
in ASTA. Unfortunately, due to inexperience with the OptiTrack system, the virtual rigid
body on the end of the pole was not initialized with the correct orientation, making obtain-
ing the baseline with the correct orientation of the UScanner complicated. Furthermore,
the recordings by the UScanner were quite poor in quality, with many jerky movements of
the UScanner in the pool leading to a lot of movement of the water’s surface. As the ob-
jects in the pool are reflected on the surface, this introduces a lot of noise into the image,
making successful landmark detection and tracking diﬀicult. It was, therefore, decided
that the experiment should be repeated.
Experiment 2: January 5, 2025
The second experiment was performed on January 5, 2025, to accommodate what was
learned from the first experiment. First, the initialization of the virtual rigid body was
done correctly, and this time, the UScanner was moved more smoothly along the trajectory,
ensuring minimal amounts of rippling on the surface of the water. In addition to this, the
ROS-bag recording was started with the UScanner placed stationary at the edge of the
pool. This was found to be beneficial for later aligning the coordinate systems of the VIO
algorithm with that of the OptiTrack system. Furthermore, after placing the UScanner
in the water, it was held as still as possible for five seconds to let the ripples in the water
settle, avoiding distorted reflections in the surface. The recordings from this experiment,
86
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:115==
while done correctly, were ultimately useless as the glass lenses of the UScanner fogged in
the cold conditions of the ASTA, making the captured images unusable.
Experiment 3: January 10, 2025
The final experiment was done on January 10, 2025. As for the second experiment, the rigid
body was initialized correctly in the OptiTrack system, and the ROS-bag recording was
started with the UScanner stationary at the edge of the pool. The UScanner was moved
smoothly through the water, ensuring minimal noise from the reflections on the surface
of the water. This time, the lenses did not fog, giving usable recordings. Corresponding
baseline data from the OptiTrack system was collected.
4.4.3
Data
A ROS-bag with a total duration of 283.17 seconds containing the data from the UScanner
was recorded with corresponding OptiTrack baseline data as a CSV-file. For the ROS-
bag, the topics of interest are the same as shown in Table 4.10, with the same recording
frequencies. The OptiTrack system outputs the recorded trajectory at a frequency of 60
Hz.
Figure 4.36: Image taken by the left
camera of the UScanner at the start
of the relevant trajectory, 38.0 sec-
onds into the recorded data.
As described above, from prior experiments, it was
found that the VIO algorithm benefits from keep-
ing the UScanner still for a short while in the water
while the rippling on the surface succeeds. Further-
more, it is not desired to estimate the trajectory of
placing the UScanner or removing it from the pool.
Therefore, the relevant part of the trajectory that
the VIO algorithm should estimate begins 38.0 sec-
onds after the recording starts and stops 26.5 sec-
onds before the recording stops. This is the part of
the recording where the UScanner travels smoothly
along the edges of the pool, submerged. The image
captured from the left camera of the UScanner at
the start of the of the relevant trajectory can be seen in Figure 4.36. The obtained trajec-
tory in the pool from the OptiTrack system can be seen in Figure 4.37, with the trajectory
plotted in the xy-plane and 3D in Figure 4.38.
4.4.4
Discussion
Considering all three attempts from December 19 to January 10, this experiment was
not without complications; some were caused by limited experience with the OptiTrack
system, and others were caused by the tendency of the UScanner lenses to fog. However,
adjusting subsequent experiments based on the learnings from the prior proved valuable
in the end, obtaining high-quality underwater data and corresponding baseline data.
The obtained baseline and recorded visual and inertial data from the UScanner will be
used in the implementation and evaluation of the selected VIO algorithm. As the data
obtained from the primary data acquisition campaign in Sydhavnen is deemed unfit for
this due to the poor lighting conditions and the lack of a centimeter-precise baseline, these
recordings, are therefore particularly valuable.

4.5
Refining the OptiTrack Data
Figure 4.39: Image of the ASTA motion capture system. Source [151]
In both of the ASTA experiments, data was collected from the OptiTrack motion capture
system, which offers real-time tracking of objects within the confines of the system. The
system as installed in ASTA is shown in Figure 4.39 and consists of 16 cameras mounted
on an aluminum truss frame structure with dimensions 16 × 12 × 8 meters, length, width,
and height, respectively[151]. All of the cameras are mounted in the top frame, with one
camera in each corner and three cameras per beam, placed with a quarter-length distance
of the beam between them. The configuration of the cameras can be seen in Figure 4.40
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.40: Schematic of camera placements. Source [151].
The cameras track small retroreflective ball markers, which can be attached to objects. At
least three of these motion tracking markers must be attached to the object, as this allows
the Motive:Tracker 3 software to infer both the location and the orientation of the object:
Within the software, the three or more markers are selected, and a rigid body is created
from the selection. At the point of initialization, this rigid body will have an attached
coordinate frame that is aligned with the coordinate frame of the OptiTrack system. As
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
89
==PAGE:118==
the object is moved, the rigid body and the attached coordinate frame moves with it,
allowing for accurate tracking of both location and orientation.
Inside the area of the OptiTrack system is the pool used for the sonar and UScanner
experiments, which measures 6.5×3.5×3 meters in length, width, and depth, respectively.
This pool is at the far end of the confines of the OptiTrack system, as seen in Figure 4.39,
meaning that when moving either the sonar or the UScanner along the side of the pool
closest to the border of the system, the retroreflective markers are out of range of the
cameras directly above, meaning that they get tracked only by the cameras in the opposite
end of the system. To get an intuition for this flaw in the system, see Figure 4.40b. The
problematic side of the pool is depicted in Figure 4.41
Figure 4.41: The pool in ASTA with the problematic area/side at the edge of the OptiTrack
system highlighted in red.
As the platform with the mounted reflective markers on top of the pole is rather small, the
OptiTrack system will lose the tracked rigid body, creating gaps in the data. To accom-
modate this problem, the tolerances for the accuracy in the tracking of the markers had
to be adjusted, which was done by increasing the deflection setting in the Motive:Tracker
software. This unfortunately introduced noise into the tracking, especially in terms of the
orientation, as the coordinate system attached to the rigid body would exhibit ”shaking”
or ”jittering”, as the reflective markers were tracked with less accuracy. This inaccuracy
was amplified by the transformation from the top of the pole to the SONARs or the US-
canner at the bottom, as a small disturbance in orientation at the top will lead to large
and rapid displacements at the end of the pole. This results in an excessively noisy tra-
jectory for the object below the surface in the pool. To illustrate this problem Figure 4.44
and Figure 4.42 show the path of the UScanner along the problematic edge, as well as the
plotted velocities in the pool.
90
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:119==

Figure 4.42: The tracked velocities of the UScanner at the edge of the pole, in many cases
exceeding more than 30 m
s
To address this issue, a smoothing process was applied to refine the estimated trajec-
tory within the pool. As the source of the problem originates from the noisily tracked
orientation, a low-pass Butterworth filter was applied to each value of the tracked quater-
nion, representing the orientation. The Butterworth filter is initialized with an order of
2 and a cutoff frequency of 0.5 Hz. To ensure that the filtered signal has zero phase,
the scipy.signal.filtfilt is used, which applies the Butterworth filter forwards and
backward on the signal.
The original and filtered values of the tracked quaternions for a selected period are shown in
Figure 4.43, and likewise, resulting positions for a selected period are given in Figure 4.44

This section provides a detailed explanation of how SONAR,
LiDAR, and IMU data are fused to create a ”seabed-to-sky”
map.
The chapter begins by introducing two approaches for ex-
tracting 3D points from SONAR images; leading-edge detec-
tion and Forward Looking Sonar (FLS) stereo vision. Next,
a description LIO-SAM algorithm that provides the sky part
of the mapping and localization functionality will be given.
Subsequently, a solution for mapping the extracted SONAR
points into the seabed-to-sky map is proposed. This will be
followed by a description of how experiments in controlled
environments have been used to refine the mapping. Finally,
both ground and sea-map will be assessed.
5.1
3D Acoustic Point Cloud
Every image received from a SONAR can be represented as rectangular beam-range sonar
image I(R, θ) ∈R+, where there is a beam direction or bearing θ ∈Θ ⊆[−π, π] for each
column and a range R ∈R+ for each row. Each element in the image has an intensity,
which results in these images being polar coordinates on a 2D plane with intensities for
each coordinate. A 3D polar coordinates can be converted to Cartesian coordinates using
simple trigonometry:
Sp =

(5.1)
where ϕ corresponds to the elevation of the point. Figure 5.2 illustrates this projection.
As described in section Section 2.2.1, the FLS sonars in this project use linear phased
arrays, meaning that all points are projected to a single plane. Assuming 0◦elevation will
Figure 5.2: The SONAR projection geometric model. The figure is taken directly from
[36], figure 7, with minor modifications.

result in a simpler expression:

Therefore, assuming that a point is on the plane induces an error depending on the angle,
range, and vertical aperture of the setup, resulting in inaccurate mapping. Although it
is proposed to induce elevation using a stereo SONAR setup, this is limited by narrow
vertical apertures of the two SONARs of 20◦, reducing the FOV significantly. To overcome
this small FOV, both the FLS stereo vision and the leading edge extraction are considered
for 3D point cloud extraction.
5.1.1
Image Preprocessing
The images obtained in the main data campaign have a significant amount of noise and
several artifacts, which require additional care to enable robust feature extraction. More
specifically, Figure 4.19 showed how the Oculus SONAR mainly suffers from noise issues,
while the problems of the Teledyne Blueview illustrated in Figure 4.20 highlight the issues
of artifacts. The following three pre-processing steps have been applied for the horizon-
Figure 5.3: The outcome of the different pre-processing steps, applied on top the the
previous steps. a) shows the original image I. b) show the outcome of subtracting the
10% quantile value from the original image. c) remove noise by applying a mask based
on Otsu’s adaptive thresholding. d) shows the result of applying normalization on the
outcome of steps a-c. The images are given in polar coordinates, and the range has been
down-adjusted to 5 meters for visualization purposes. The images only have one channel,
but a color map is used for clearer visualization.
tal SONAR images.
First, the 10% quantile of each row in the image, Q0.1(I(R)), is
subtracted, for denoising purposes;
If(R) = max {0, I(R) −Q0.1(I(R))} ,
(5.3)
94
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:123==
This row-wise adjustment is to account for intensity variations observed across row group-
ings as shown in Figure 5.3a, which appear on some images. Additionally, this reduces
the amount of low-intensity values, improving the result of the following steps.
Subsequently, Otsu’s method [152] for automatic image threshold is applied to create
a mask that keeps the relevant high-intensity values of the image. Say that the image
intensities can be split into two classes: C0 and C1, where one is below an intensity
threshold k and the other above. Otsu’s method determines the threshold that minimizes
the intra-class variance of the image, defined as;
σ2
w(k) = ω0(k)σ2
0(k) + ω1(k)σ2
1(k)
(5.4)
where σ2
0 and σ2
1 are the variance of each class respectively, ω0 and ω1 are the associated
probabilities of class occurrence. These are determined by
ω0 =

(5.5)

(5.6)
where L is the number of intensity levels, ni is the number of pixels at the i intensity level,
and N is the total number of pixels. In practice, instead of minimizing the intra-class
variance, the inter-class variance σ2
B given by
σ2
B = ω0ω1(µ0 −µ1)2,
(5.7)
is maximized, as this is equivalent [153][154]. µ0 and µ1 describe the mean intensity values
of each class, respectively [152]. The complete derivation and explanations of why this is
the case can be found in Appendix F.8. Having determined the optimal threshold k∗, a
binary mask M(θ, R) is created to identify pixels with intensities above the threshold:
M(θ, R) =
®
1,
if I(θ, R) ≥k∗,
0,
if I(θ, R) < k∗,
(5.8)
The filtered image If(θ, R) is then obtained by element-wise multiplication of the input
image I(θ, R) with the mask M(θ, R):
If(θ, R) = I(θ, R) · M(θ, R).
(5.9)
However, this method has limitations, one being that it does not perform well under heavy
noise, making the prior de-noising step necessary [154].
Finally, the image is normalized1 to ensure a consistent intensity scale, which is essential
for feature matching between the two SONARs later in the pipeline.
1Converted to an 8-bit format where 255 is the maximum value.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
95
==PAGE:124==
Figure 5.4: This sequence of images shows the result of applying the four pre-processing
steps of the vertical SONAR output. (a) shows the original vertical image. (b) shows
the result of subtracting the row-wise mean from the corresponding rows. (c) visualizes
how removing the 98th percentile of pixel intensities reduces artifacts. The green marking
in the center image highlights the area where this operation is performed. (d) shows the
outcome of normalization, and (e) shows the image after a median filter has been applied.
For the vertical SONAR, artifacts are induced by too high gain settings2, requiring a
different sequence of pre-processing step. Ideally, these artifacts should be avoided by
proper calibration of the SONAR gain parameters.
Similar to the prior approach, row-wise subtraction is performed to reduce artifacts that
are induced across whole rows. Defining the mean of an image row as µ(I(R)), the math-
ematical operation can be expressed as
If(R) = max {0, I(R) −µ(I(R))} ,
(5.10)
This approach exploits domain knowledge about what the SONAR is scanning, which is
mainly seabed, and the orientation of the SONAR, creating a narrow set of features in
each row. Figure 5.4b shows the outcome of this process on an image where artifacts
appear moderately.
This operation is followed by statistical removal of pixels with low-level intensities in the
center of the image. It is observed that in the area between −4.25◦and 6.85◦from the
image center-line, artifacts tend to be most prominent. The area is marked in Figure 5.4c,
which also shows the result of the operation. A threshold is determined by:
k = Q0.98(I(θ)), for θ ∈[−10◦, 10◦],

Applying the mask using Equation (5.9) with θ ∈[−10◦, 10◦], only the upper 2nd percentile

5.1.2
Leading Edge Detection
Observed 
Figure 5.5: Example of irregularities in the
seabed can break leading edge assumptions.
The error is denoted ϵ, and the elevation is
denoted ϕ.
For 3D point cloud extraction, one method
used in this study is the leading edge pro-
posed in [15] and utilized in [13]. Here, it
is assumed that all points on the SONAR
image are on a single plane, and, therefore,
the method ignores the uncertainty that is
introduced in the elevating dimension, us-
ing a linear phase array. This is, of course,
an uncertainty that needs to be taken into
account when evaluating the method and
will depend on the range and angle of the
SONAR.
Given a rectangular SONAR image I(R, θ),
the leading edge can be determined by
identifying, for each beam direction in the
image, the smallest range R at which the image intensity exceeds a given threshold. This
can be expressed as:
r(θ) = min{R | I(θ, R) > τ},
(5.13)
Where:
• r(θ) is the range corresponding to the leading edge in the beam direction θ.
• I(θ, R) represents the magnitude of the sonar image at range R and beam direction
θ.
• τ is either a predefined or adaptive threshold value.
The set of leading-edge points is represented by the function r, which associates each
beam direction θ with its corresponding range r(θ). These points can be projected onto
the (x, y)-plane using Equation (5.2), where R = r(θ). Depending on the angle of the
SONAR and knowledge of the domain that is being scanned, an elevation of the leading
edge can be inferred to increase accuracy. For instance, in the setup for the main data
campaign, one sonar is pitched -40 degrees downwards, making a horizontal scan-line of
the seabed. Given the vertical aperture of 20 degrees and the fairly flat seabed, it can
be assumed that the leading edge, i.e., the shortest range, will be observed at the lowest
angle of the vertical aperture. Given a flat surface, the error range ϵr can be determined
by:
ϵr = 1 −
cos α
cos (α −β), where α = π
2 + γ,
(5.14)
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
97
==PAGE:126==
and β is the FOV and γ is the pitch of −40◦. If this assumption is broken, e.g. if an object
appears as shown in Figure 5.5, then ϵr = 0.26, i.e. 26%.
This formulation assumes the SONAR’s coordinate system is aligned such that the ori-
gin represents the sonar sensor and all angles are defined concerning its horizontal plane.
Expressing the data as tuples of range and beam directions simplifies the process of trans-
forming 2D sonar image data into 3D spatial coordinates, enabling applications in visu-
alization, mapping, and integration into robotic systems[15][13]. Some of the main issues
with using leading edge detection is the relatively large inaccuracy due to sonar image
projecting 3D spatial information onto a single plane. As addressed in [15], the method
is also potentially ”[...] challenged by structures with abrupt changes in direction” and
”shadows cast by ridges and objects peaking out of the main surface [...]”, page 7. Another
downside to this approach is that the remaining image is discarded, which potentially
holds valuable information for feature extraction. The next section concerns a method
that can utilize a larger portion of the image.
Image Processing for Leading Edge Detection
When identifying the leading edge in SONAR images, noise is an issue, as this computa-
tionally effective approach will detect noisy points if their intensity is above the predeter-
mined threshold.
Figure 5.6: a) shows a horizontal SONAR image without the open filter applied, where the
green scatter indicates the observed leading edge. Image b-d shows the result of applying
open filters of different kernel sizes. In b), it is clear that less noise has been captured by
the leading edge threshold. In c), the kernel has been extended vertically, which doesn’t
seem to affect the denoising. In d), a taller kernel mitigates almost all noise, but it has
heavily affected the rest of the image, resulting in the leading edge ”bleeding” into the
surface.
Finding a generalizable solution can be diﬀicult, and maintaining computational eﬀiciency
is necessary, as a high frame rate, i.e., low latency, is preferable when using leading edge for
98
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:127==
mapping. The following approaches will be considered: image filtering, Median Absolute
Diviation (MAD), and line-fitting.
Noise removal on the horizontal sonar
Even after applying the earlier described filtering on the horizontal SONAR image (Sec-
tion 5.1.1), undesired high-intensity areas are still present.
The images have a large FOV and capture a wide range of elements, such as the seabed,
walls, and objects, making simple line-fitting for outlier detection unsuitable. Instead, both
image filtering and Median Absolute Diviation (MAD), a method for outlier detection, are
investigated as possible filtering methods.
Figure 5.6a is an example of the noise captured after the pre-filtering. An approach to
minimize the amount of noise is to use morphological operations such as erosion, which
computes the local minimum over the area of the kernel. Another approach that extends
this is called opening, which is an erosion followed by dilation, closing some of the holes that
have been created due to erosion. Like erosion, dilation uses a kernel across the image but
finds the maximum value instead when convolving. The package OpenCV provides a wide
variety of morphological methods, including the above-mentioned [155]. Figure 5.6b shows
how open filtering is used to lower the amount of noise using a 3 × 1 open filter without
altering the clearly defined leading edge. The dimensions of the kernel are designed based
on the attributes of the noise, which spans over multiple columns (bearings) rather than
rows, making a tall kernel suitable. Increasing the kernel size results in even more noise
being filtered out. However, the filter has heavily affected the rest of the image, resulting
in leading-edge ”bleeding” into the surface. Other kernels were explored as well using only
erosion, of which examples can be found in Appendix F.1. Applying these operations is
time-wise effective, averaging at 0.058 ms for a horizontal image over 900 iterations on an
AMD Ryzen Threadripper 3960X 24-Core Processor.
Algorithm 1 Rolling MAD on Profile (Loop Version)
Require: x is a 1D array
Require: W integer, size of the window. Must be uneven.
Require: threshold. Default value is 2.5
Require: b, distribution constant. Default 1.4826
Ensure: v for valid indices, u for upper bound, l for lower bound.
1: n ←length of x
2: Whalf ←⌊W/2⌋
3: ”Edge” pad x with Whalf values on each side of x
4: Initialize ˜xrolling and madrolling as arrays of zeros of length n
5: for i = Whalf to n −Whalf do
6:
xwindow ←x[i −Whalf : i + Whalf]
7:
˜xwindow ←median(xwindow)
8:
madrolling[i] ←b · median(|xwindow −˜xwindow|)
9:
˜xrolling[i] ←˜xwindow
10: end for
11: u ←˜xrolling + (madrolling × threshold)
12: l ←˜xrolling −(madrolling × threshold)
13: v ←(x ≥l) ∧(x ≤u)
14: return v, u, l
Another approach is to use outlier detections like rolling Median Absolute Diviation3. The
3not to be confused with mean average deviation, which is more prone to outliers
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
99
==PAGE:128==
MAD is a measure for the variability of the data and is calculated by;
MAD(xi) = b · median(|xi −˜x|)
(5.15)
where ˜x = median(x), xi denotes the ith observation in x ∈Rn, are the x cartesian
coordinates. b is a constant linked to the distribution of the data. Typically, b = 1/Q.75
where Q.75 is the 0.75 quartile of the underlying distribution.
Assuming a Gaussian
distribution, this value is b = 1.4826 [156]. The MAD statistic is known to be very robust
and resilient to outliers compared to using the standard deviation [156][157]. One way to
reject outliers is to calculate this statistic and use a threshold such as ±3 · MAD when
considering outliers for standard deviation. However, due to how the data changes across
the observed line, better results can be achieved by only considering the outlier locally by
applying the rolling (or moving) MAD instead. A pseudocode of how this is implemented
can be found in Algorithm 1.
Figure 5.7: MAD used on the leading edge. The image is pre-filtered and is the same as
the one presented in Figure 5.6. The threshold changes depending on how the data varies.
Both images are plotted in Cartesian space in grayscale.
In Figure 5.7, rolling MAD has been applied, assuming normal distribution and a moder-
ately conservative threshold of 2.5. For comparison, two window sizes are tested on the
same image: 17 and 89. Here, the window size has a large effect on which points are
treated as outliers.
Before concluding on the image processing for the horizontal image, processing time should
be considered. While the ”loop version” of the algorithm presented is conceptually easy to
understand, it is not very eﬀicient computationally. By utilizing NumPy, strided windows
can be computed more effectively by using vectorized NumPy operations. The NumPy eﬀi-
cient stride version can be found in Appendix F.2. Experimental tests have been conducted
to assess the performance of the two algorithms. Over 1000 runs, excluding the first 100
for warm-up, the strided version achieves an approximate speedup of 17.2 times compared
to the naïve loop version, with an average time of 0.83 ms per scan with a window size of
100
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:129==
89, compared to a 14.2 ms on the loop version. Additionally, the strided version to the
3 × 1 open filter remains approximately 14 times faster than the strided MAD solution.
These results were collected on an AMD Ryzen Threadripper 3960X 24-Core Processor.
In conclusion, it was found that using an open filter can reduce the noise of the pre-
filtered image further without affecting the refresh rate, possibly enhancing the leading
edge detection. Another approach, MAD, has shown to be promising for general outlier
detection if a suitable window size has been chosen. However, too much noise increases
the threshold, reducing its effectiveness. In addition to that, fewer features are detected,
as the real ones are ”hiding” behind the noisy ones that are removed after detection. The
NumPy strided version has proven to be computationally eﬀicient and feasible to use in
the implementation. However, the comprehensive pre-filter, in combination with the open
operation using a 3 × 1 kernel, is found suﬀicient for the final implementation.
Artifact avoidance on the vertical sonar
In Section 5.1.1, the presence of artifacts was significantly reduced, improving the condi-
tions for leading edge detection. However, further steps can be taken to increase robust-
ness.
Figure 5.8: Comparison between RANSAC (left) and MAD (right) on the same vertical
sonar image. Both plots are in Cartesian coordinates and have been cropped to highlight
the relevant data.
Initially, classic image filtering, such as morphology and median filters, was used. However,
since the artifacts in the vertical SONAR images are larger patches and not grainy noise,
these methods are suspected to be ineﬀicient. Line-fitting and MAD involve assuming
the observed structure, which is mainly the seabed represented in the images as a line.
Therefore, performing simple line fitting, using Random Sample Consensus (RANSAC) to
remove outliers, could be effective. However, this approach places some heavy constraints
on what is observed and will not work well for detecting abnormalities in the seabed.
To investigate this further, a comparison between using MAD and RANSAC for outlier
detection is conducted.

Figure 5.9: Conceptual diagram of the proposed method. The input images are already
pre-processed, following the steps presented in section 5.1.1. After being transformed into
Cartesian coordinates, a transformation to the horizontal SONAR frame is performed.
The images are then trimmed based on intersecting areas, reducing processing time in
the following steps. Features are then extracted from each image and clustered. These
clusters are then matched based on the descriptors of the clusters.
Features are then
matched between the two images, restricted by the assigned cluster pair. The matched
features are at last converted into 3D points in Cartesian space.
RANSAC is a commonly used iterative method for fitting a model to experimental data
and is particularly robust against outliers under the assumption that the data can be
described by the fitted model [158] [159]. See Appendix I for a more detailed explanation.
Figure 5.8 shows the outcome of using RANSAC on the SONAR data compared with
using the MAD from described in previous section.
For the implementation, scikit’s RANSACRegressor is used. The computational eﬀiciency
of RANSAC depends on several factors, such as randomness, the data, residual thresholds,
and a maximum number of trials. In general, it is observed that a single trial takes less
than 0.5 ms. However, in most cases, only 5-7 trials are used before achieving a good fit
on the data. To reduce the risk of latency, the number of max trials should be determined
based on the available computational time. If 40 ms is available, the maximum iterations
should be 80.
Going forward, RANSAC will be used for outlier removal by applying the algorithm to fit
a model to the (x, y) cartesian coordinates extracted from the leading-edge detection. A
measurement will be considered an outlier if the Euclidean distance to the line is above 1
meter.
5.1.3
FLS Stereo Vision
Given the limitations of using a single Forward Looking Sonar (FLS) for 3D point cloud
mapping, an approach is fusing two FLSs.
This method is utilized to take advantage
of information that the leading-edge approach fails to capture from the SONAR images.
By doing so, it aims to create a more dense and feature-rich mapping of the underwater
environment. The implementation is based on [35], who are fusing concurrent orthogonal
FLS’ to construct 3D points in the overlapping field of view between the SONARs.
102
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:131==
Given that the range R, bearing θ and elevation ϕ is obtained for an observed point in
space, Cartesian coordinates can be obtained using Equation (5.1). The main point for this
method is that the elevation can be obtained from a SONAR mounted vertically, pointing
in the same direction as the horizontal SONAR. The images from this SONAR will, there-
fore, provide the elevating angle of the features observed in the intersection between the
two sonars. By matching the two SONAR images based on how far away (range) the object
is observed and the intensity of the points, 3D-points can be constructed. A conceptual
overview of the method is provided in Figure 5.9, which will be presented in detail below.
Figure 5.10: Overlap (yellow) between the
horizontal SONAR (red) and the vertical
SONAR (blue). Drawn in Fusion 360.
In [35], everything from feature extraction
to point matching is done in polar coor-
dinates. One issue is that this introduces
several constraints regarding the physical
setup. To make sure that the ranges are
aligned, both SONAR heads need to be
aligned so they are not displaced horizon-
tally. Additionally, one cannot be further
ahead than the other.
The vertical dis-
placement can also introduce additional er-
rors as the correction that was applied in
the paper is subtracted after matching the
images. At last, working in polar coordi-
nates also constrains the setup to be or-
thogonally aligned, which is potentially a
suboptimal approach regard combined Field of View (FOV) optimization. In the follow-
ing, we propose a more generalizable approach that can account for non-orthogonal setups
and displacement between the two SONARs. The setup operates in polar or Cartesian
coordinates, depending on the task at hand. Instead of representing an intensity image I
as a matrix, they are viewed as x-y-z intensity vectors;
z(h) = [x(h) y(h) z(h) γ(h)]T ,
z(v) = [x(v) y(v) z(v) γ(v)]T ,
(5.16)

respectively, and γ is the intensity. The coordinates are obtained for all polar coordinates
by using Equation (5.2). Additionally, the relative translational difference between the
two SONARs should be accounted for immediately.

A small note on image preprocessing
In the original implementation, resizing was performed to make the range and bearings
of each pixel of the image correspond to the other, potentially losing resolution. This has
now been avoided by using Cartesian coordinates.
The de-noising filter used in the original paper was the OpenCV implementation of Fast Non-
local means denoising. However, it is computationally slow compared to classical image
processing methods, as the ones presented in Section 5.1.1. The Fast Non-local means
denoising takes 159.9 ms for 900 iterations on a horizontal sonar image using an 8 core
Intel(R) Core(TM) i7-8750H CPU @ 2.20GH laptop processor (same setup as presented
in Section 5.1.2). The total computational time for our denoising approach is 2.32 ms.
Image Trimming
When receiving the preprocessed images I(h)
f
and I(v)
f , trimming is performed to reduce
the image to the intersecting areas. By doing this before feature extraction, processing
time is reduced in the successive steps.
Feature Extraction
For feature extraction of the images, a technique called constant false alarm rate (CFAR)
is being used as this technique has proven to work well for radar images as well as sonar
images. This is also what is being used in [35]. This technique produces a dynamic thresh-
old by simply taking the values of the surrounding cells into account when considering each
cell. There are three different types of cells when working with CFAR;
Cell under Test (CUT) This cell is the cell under current investigation. This is the cell
for which a new threshold is calculated.
Gap Cells To prevent portions of the signal to leak into the noise estimate, gap cells are
introduced which are a number of cells, Ngc, from the CUT that is omitted when
calculating the threshold. These are sometimes referred to as guard cells.
Reference Cells The Nrc next cells surrounding the gap cells and are the ones that are
used to calculate the threshold. One way to calculate this threshold is by simply
averaging the reference cells. These cells are sometimes referred to as training cells
[160] [35].
For simplicity, a 1D example is given in Figure 5.11, where the different types of cells are
represented.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
105
==PAGE:134==
CUT
Gap
Gap
Reference
Reference
Figure 5.11: 1D example of the CFAR cell types. CUT is the cell under investigation.
The reference frames are used to determine the threshold, while the gaps are a number of
cells surrounding the CUT. Inspiration to figure from [160].
In this example, Ngc = 2 and Nrc = 3. Now, the most obvious approach from here could be
to simply average these results and use that result for further calculation of the threshold.
This approach is called cell average or CA-CFAR. However, CA-CFAR unfortunately tends
to perform poorly when the reference window contains interfering targets (other targets
appear in the reference windows of the CUT).
One approach to address this issue is the Smallest Of Cell Averages (SOCA-CFAR). In
this approach, the threshold is calculated using the smaller of the two cell averages, µleft
or µright, as illustrated in the example above.
Additionally, two other well-known approaches are often discussed in the literature:
• Greatest Of Cell Averages (GOCA-CFAR), which uses the larger of the cell averages
for threshold calculation.
• Order-Statistic (OS-CFAR), which replaces averaging with an order statistic, such
as the sample median instead of the sample mean [161].
Figure 5.12: CFAR cell types in a 2D
image.
The figure shows how the four
cell averages are calculated for a 2D im-
age, where as in Figure 5.11 Ngc = 2
(red) and Nrc = 3 (blue and light blue).
The figure is presented in steps to clarify
what is included in the individual aver-
age. Adapted from [35].
CFAR can be extended to two dimensions, as il-
lustrated in Figure 5.12. In this work, we adopt
the SOCA-CFAR approach in 2D. For each
Cell Under Test (CUT), denoted as xCUT, the
method calculates four averages corresponding
to adjacent regions (left, top, right, bottom).
The smallest of these means, µmin, is defined
as:
µmin = min{µleft, µtop, µright, µbottom}, (5.29)
where each mean µ is computed as the average
over all reference cells, xrc, within the respective rectangular region. The number of cells
in a rectangle is denoted as N.
Finally, a bias term, or detection constant, α, is introduced. This term adjusts the thresh-
old level to account for noise characteristics and system settings:
α = N

P −1/N
fa
−1

,
(5.30)
where Pfa is the false alarm rate. Increasing Pfa lowers the threshold, thereby allowing
more cells to be classified as features.
Using the SOCA-CFAR approach, the threshold for the CUT cell is expressed as:
τCUT = µminα.
(5.31)

Clustering and Cluster Association
To make the feature matching more robust, [35] proposed that clustering and association
between clusters are performed, taking advantage of the underlying surfaces that the fea-
tures represent4. Density-Based Spatial Clustering of Applications with Noise (DBSCAN)
is used to cluster the features. Some of the main arguments for using this method are that
it does not require prior information regarding the number of clusters being searched and
knowledge about the sonar image is needed beforehand. In practice, an implementation
provided by SciKit Learn is utilized. Since this is a very common method, we refer to the
online documentation for further information regarding the algorithm. Through iterative
tuning on experiment images, two hyperparameters are set: ϵ = 0.20 meters which is the
maximum distance to a nearby candidate, and the minimum samples per cluster is n = 20.
When clusters have been defined for each image, cluster descriptors ct are made to associate

5.2
LiDAR SLAM
The following sub-sections will first concern graph based SLAM from a more general
perspective, followed by a description of how LIO-SAM works.
The choice of SLAM
algorithm is based on the review of LiDAR based SLAM algorithms conducted in [13].
Some of the main arguments are that the algorithm supports real-time execution, supports
Docker containerization, is implemented in ROS as well as ROS2, and has proven to be a
robust solution in the same environment as explored in this work.
5.2.1
Simultaneous Localization and Mapping
As described in Section 1.1.5, SLAM is simultaneously getting a joint estimation of a
robot’s states, including pose, and a representation of the surrounding environment. View-
ing this in a Bayesian setting, this corresponds to maximizing the posterior p(X|Z) of the
108
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:137==
state variables X that is composed of the robot’s states, x, and map given a set of measure-
ments Z that is obtained by the robot. This maximization problem is currently divided
into two main approaches: filtering and smoothing. Filtering is the process of iteratively
updating the current state X at each new measurement. Smoothing, on the other hand,
calculates the marginal posterior distribution of Xk at step k after receiving measurements
up to time step T, with T > k [162]:
p(Xk|Z1:T ).
(5.40)
By this, smoothing incorporates future measurements when computing the estimates. A
popular technique is Maximum A Priori (MAP) estimation. In MAP estimation, the goal
is to find the state X∗that maximizes the posterior probability. This state can be found
by utilizing Bayes’ rule for continuous random variables;
p(X | Z) =
p(Z | X) p(X)
R
p(Z | X) p(X) dX .
(5.41)
Since the denominator is independent of X, maximizing P(X | Z) is equivalent to maxi-
mizing the product p(Z | X) p(X):
X∗.= argmax
X
p(X | Z) = argmax
X
p(Z | X) p(X).
(5.42)
This approach combines the likelihood of the measurements p(Z|X) with a prior distri-
bution p(X) over the robot’s trajectory, yielding the most probable estimate of both the
robot’s path and the environment map. This optimization is typically performed using
iterative nonlinear least squares methods, such as Gauss-Newton or Levenberg-Marquardt
[19]. This probabilistic description can be formalized as a graph-based formulation, more
specifically, a dynamic Bayes network, which allows the utilization of graph optimization
methods and creates an intuitive understanding of the proposed system. The following is
based on [163]. Let
X = {xi}, i ∈[0, M],
L = {lj}, j ∈[0, N],
U = {ui}, i ∈[0, M],
Z = {zk}, k ∈[1, K].
(5.43)
Here, robot poses xi are influenced by the underlying robot controls ui, while the mea-
surements zk observes the real world landmarks l. Both the states and the measurements
of the environment can be modeled as;
xi = fi (xi−1, ui) + wi,
wi ∼N 0, Ω−1

	 ,
zk = hk (xik, ljk) + vk,
vk ∼N 0, Ω−1
k
	 ,
(5.44)
where fi and hk are the underlying state and observation models and v and w are noise
variables. The joint probability of all our variables can be written as a product of all
conditionals,
P(X, L, U, Z) ∝P (x0)

Similar to Equation (5.42), this can be formalized into a maximization of the set of states
as well as the underlying landmarks.

control inputs and individual landmark representations are omitted, focusing directly on
the trajectory. This allows us to express the SLAM problem as:

5.2.2
LIO-SAM
This section presents the LiDAR Inertial Odometry Smoothing and Mapping (LIO-SAM)
framework published in [107]. This graph-based Smoothing and Mapping (SAM) algorithm
addresses several issues with LiDAR based SLAM approaches, including; drifting induced
by raw skewed LiDAR measurements, ineﬀicient scan matching, lack of tightly-coupled
LiDAR-IMU systems and systems that can provide real-time performance. How these
issues are tackled and how the framework is implemented will be presented in this section.
The state estimation problem, previously described as a MAP problem, also serves as
the foundation for LIO-SAM. In this approach, the problem is modeled using a factor
110
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:139==
graph, which, under the assumption of a Gaussian noise model, becomes equivalent to
solving a nonlinear least-squares problem [107][165]. The use of factors allows for further
in-cooperation of sensors without loss of generality. One could, for instance, add SONAR
measurements as an extra factor as long as the noise is assumed to be Gaussian.
In LIO-SAM, each state is represented as
x = [R⊤p⊤v⊤b⊤]⊤,
(5.53)
which include the rotation matrix R ∈SO(3), the position vector p, speed v and the IMU
bias b. As described in Equation (5.19), the transformation matrix from the body frame
B to the world frame W can be written as T = [R|p]. It should be noted that the world
frame in this section refers to the local estimate of W and that this concept will be further
expanded in Chapter 9.
Figure 5.14: The structure of LIO-SAM. The figure shows how the different measurements
are providing information to construct a factor, which is used to estimate the individual
robot state nodes. In this example, four types of factors are introduced: IMU preintegra-
tion, LiDAR odometry, GPS, and loop closure factor. Source: [107].
Figure 5.14 presents an overview of the LIO-SAM system which uses measurements from
IMU, LiDAR and optionally GPS. These measurements are then constructed into indi-
vidual factors for each modality, which are added to the factor graph. A fourth factor
is also introduced, called the loop closure factor, which is added to the graph if a loop
closure is detected. The composed factor graph is optimized every time a new node (i.e.,
state x) is inserted using a Bayes tree through the iSAM2 implementation [166]. To main-
tain a relatively sparse factor graph to maintain real-time optimization, the concept of
keyframes is introduced. Both the IMU and LiDAR operate at relatively high frame rates.
Creating a node for each measurement would increase the graph complexity, affecting
the eﬀiciency. Therefore, before both the IMU and LiDAR factors are added, additional
preprocessing of the intermediate measurements is performed. A new keyframe is added
when the estimated change in pose exceeds a user-defined threshold. Additionally, this
approach maintains a lower memory consumption and map-density. The following will
describe each factor and how they contribute to the state estimation.
IMU Pre-integration Factor
The IMU model and the implemented pre-integration are based on the work done in [167].
The following will closely resemble that explanation. Expanding Equation (3.1) to include
all 3 axis, the IMU angular velocity is described as
B ˜ωBW(t) = BωBW(t) + bω(t) + ηω(t),
(5.54)
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
111
==PAGE:140==

frame B relative to the world frame W. bω and ηω are the bias and noise terms of the
angular velocity, respectively. The measured linear acceleration ˜a can be described as;
B˜a(t) = R⊤
WB(t) (Wa(t) −Wg) + ba(t) + ηa(t),
(5.55)
where R⊤

the linear acceleration.

LiDAR Odometry Factor
Since the LiDAR is in motion while performing scans, the obtained measurement becomes
skewed, which affects the state estimation and, over time, results in larger drifts [107].
Fortunately, this skewness can be mitigated by reprojecting the point cloud using the
motion model obtained by the IMU [168].
In practice, this is done by keeping track
of the recent rotation reported by the IMU in a queue structure. Let Rk be latest IMU
measurement. Using the measured angular velocity ˜ω and the time since last measurement
∆t, the subsequent rotation matrix is given by:
Rk+1 = Rk + ˜ωk∆t,
(5.60)
using Euler integration. Given a scan, i.e. point cloud P at time t, the rotation for each
point pi ∈P obtained at time ti can be obtained by finding the R closest to point time
and interpolate between the two rotations, resulting in R(ti). For each point, de-skewing
is applied by:5
p′
i = R(ti) · pi.
(5.61)
Having de-skewed the point cloud P, feature extraction is performed by evaluating the
smoothness over a local surface. Let S be the set of consecutive points of p′
i, where half
is split on each side of the point. The smoothness denoted by c can be calculated as
ci =

(5.62)
as proposed in [168]6. Additionally, if the difference between two consecutive points ex-
ceeds a specified threshold, the points in that region of the ring are discarded, as they are
deemed unreliable, often resulting from surfaces parallel to the beam or occlusions. The
points with the maximum value of c are selected as edge points, while the lowest values
are selected as planar points [168].

GPS Factor
The GPS factor can be used to correct long-term drifts by providing absolute measure-
ments. In practice, the GPS factor is only included when the estimated covariance of the
measurement is below a predefined threshold and when the estimated pose in the current
state is larger than a predefined threshold.
In practice, LIO-SAM requires that the GPS message is given as an nav_msgs/msg/Odometry
message with a covariance and the absolute position in ENU frame. Therefore, additional
conversion may be required, depending on the setup.
Loop Closure Factor
Loop closures in a SLAM context allow for the correction of nodes by comparing previous
observed measurements and incoming measurements associated with the state xi+1. The
utilization of factor graphs allows for the seamless integration of loop closures by adding
another factor. In LIO-SAM, a Euclidean distance approaches used by first searching the
graph for states that are close to xi+1 followed by scan-matching between the incoming
frame Fi+1 and m · 2 + 1 sub-keyframes as the closest keyframe and m keyframes on each
side is used to obtain the relative transformation. It is possible to adjust m and the search
distance for loop closures manually [107].
114
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:143==
5.2.3
ROS Implementation
The authors of [107] provide both a ROS and ROS2 implementation of the framework,
which is mainly programmed in C++. The package leverages the GTSAM (Georgia Tech
Smoothing and Mapping) C++ library, which implements smoothing and mapping by using
factor graphs and Bayes networks [169].
As shown in Figure 5.16, the implementation consists of four different nodes: IMU Prein-
tegration Node, Image Projection Node, Feature Extraction Node, and Map Optimization
