5.2.4
Seabed Mapping
By using LIO-SAM for robust localization, dense 3D ground maps can be constructed by
using the extracted features from the LiDAR measurements that are associated with each
node in the factor graph. To incorporate the SONAR points obtained in Section 5.1, several
approaches are considered. In [13], the obtained SONAR points were treated as LiDAR
points by concatenation of the two-point clouds before using them in LIO-SAM. This way,
SONAR points become a part of the LiDAR factor, requiring minimum alteration of the
LIO-SAM source code. However, this approach had several shortcomings, such as seabed
mapping becoming sparse due to feature extraction, downsampling, and the fact that only
keyframes are mapped.
To mitigate these issues, two other approaches were considered. 1) Creating the seabed
map in a separate ROS node based on the states estimated by LIO-SAM. 2) Modify LIO-
SAM to include the SONAR points that are associated with the time-wise corresponding
LiDAR keyframe in the mapping. The first approach benefits from being separated from
LIO-SAM, making the system more modular. However, this also becomes its weakness as
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
115
==PAGE:144==
the factor graph is not stored within the node, making large updates due to loop closures
intractable. Instead, modifying will LIO-SAM maintain the eﬀicient graph representation
and open up for expanding the factor graph with a SONAR factor. This will be the method
of choice. This project will only focus on how the measurements can be associated with
estimated nodes to create a map and will not be part of the graph optimization.
LiDAR Frame:
Horizontal Edge:
Vertical Edge:
Stereo SONAR:
Keyframes
Figure 5.17: The above illustrates how the alignment of measurements impacts which
are included in the added key-frame. i and j are two consecutive nodes, each having an
associated key-frame marked as a blue rectangle. Figure is inspired by [168].
The current implementation works by aligning extracted SONAR point clouds, including
both the leading-edge and FLS stereo vision approach with the following LiDAR points
through cloud concatenation. This will result in a low delay between SONAR and LiDAR
points as the LiDAR has an update rate of 20 Hz, being faster than the SONARs leading
edge detection and much faster than dual setup which runs at 1−5 Hz. At concatenation,
the SONAR points are assigned virtual8 ring numbers depending on the topic;
• Ring 128: Horizontal Leading Edge.
• Ring 129: Vertical Leading Edge.
• Rings ≥130: FLS stereo vision.
Each ring contains at maximum 1024 points (LiDAR resolution), which the leading edge
technique won’t exceed given the lower beam resolution. For the FLS stereo vision ap-
proach, the number of features depends on image resolution and detected features, poten-
tially exceeding this limit. During concatenation, SONAR points are transformed to the
body frame B, which is equivalent to the frame of the LiDAR.
The fused point cloud is used as input for the Image Projection Node as illustrated in
Figure 5.16. Here, only LiDAR points are de-skewed before being processed by the Feature
Extraction Node, which is also only concerned about the LiDAR points by looking at the
ring number of the point.
Ultimately, the Map Optimization Node receives additional
features from the SONARs, Fs
i, which means Equation (5.63) is now defined as;
Fi = {Fe
i, Fp
i , Fs
i}.
(5.68)
Thus, Fs
i are aligned with the associated transformation Ti, enabling conversion from B
to W. By avoiding additional feature extraction on the incoming SONAR points, more
features are preserved. However, a challenge remains: only key-frames are stored and used
for mapping. Due to slower and different update rates of SONAR topics compared toLi-
DAR, some SONAR points may be excluded from key-frames. Figure 5.17 illustrates this
8They have no physical implication
116
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:145==
issue. Additionally, SONAR measurements between key-frames could enhance mapping if
utilized. The following solutions are considered:
1. Assign the latest SONAR points to the next LiDAR measurement until updated.
This increases the delay between the LiDAR and SONAR clouds but ensures all
types of SONAR points are included.
2. Increase key-frame frequency by lowering pose-change thresholds.
This increases
graph complexity and might affect performance but includes more SONAR mea-
surements.
3. Interpolate SONAR data between two consecutive states and add them to corre-
sponding key-frame. This method might reduce accuracy depending on the choice
of interpolation and frequency of state estimation frequency.
To avoid compromising accuracy induced by delays, the first solution is ruled out. The
remaining two will be evaluated at the end of this chapter.
LIO-SAM for Seabed Mapping Configuration
LIO-SAM is highly configurable and should be properly adjusted to the environment it
operates in. This includes both the particular hardware, the available resources and real
world environment. In general, the setting recommended in the original implementation
for outdoor use is applied.
LiDAR Range: To avoid scanning the USV itself, a minimum range of 1.5 meters is set
to filter any points that are below.
Used Rings: As the acquired LiDAR has a very high vertical resolution, it can be bene-
ficial to down-sample to maintain a sparser representation of features above water,
ultimately reducing computation time and memory consumption. The LiDAR is
down-sampled by a factor of two, which in practice removes every second ring.
IMU: Originally, a 9-axis IMU which is recommended by the author, should have been
used, but wasn’t functioning during deployment. This has led to falling to the in-
ternal 6-axis IMU in the Ouster running at 100 Hz. The transform between internal
IMU and LiDAR can be obtained using the transforms stated in the Ouster docu-
mentation.
GNSS: The GPS factor will not be used. It has been implemented and tested with GPS,
but yielded worse results. This is likely due to the missing initial heading from the
IMU.
As discussed in the previous section, two approaches for seabed mapping are considered:
(1) increasing the number of keyframes, referred to as Many Keyframes and (2) inter-
polating the position of SONAR frames between consecutive keyframes, referred to as
Interpolation. In both methods, a keyframe is added when the angular displacement ex-
ceeds 0.05 radians, following the intuition that significant angular changes severely impact
the accuracy of interpolation. However, when moving in a nearly straight line at a con-
stant speed, interpolation can provide a suﬀicient approximation. To balance accuracy
and eﬀiciency, the distance threshold is set to 1 meter for the Interpolation method, in
which a much finer threshold of 0.01 meters is used for Many Keyframes to capture more
precise details.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
117
==PAGE:146==
5.2.5
Evaluation of LIO-SAM
Figure 5.18: The points corresponding to the
reference points are picked manually. Here,
the LiDAR viewer tool in matlab is used to
pick out point 1-6.
For performance evaluation of LIO-SAM,
RTK reference points collected in [13] are
used for evaluation. These points has an
average standard deviation of 0.011 me-
ters, but are still more accurate than us-
ing points selected on Google Maps. This
will be referenced to as the ground truth
when addressing the residual of the corre-
sponding selected points in the produced
maps. As shown in Figure 5.18, these cor-
responding points are manually selected by
inspecting the point clouds.
As the sur-
veyed areas is small than in [13], only RTK
point 1-6 are determined, which are listed
in Appendix F.4.
The RTK points are
given in the European Terrestrial Refer-
ence System 1989 (ETRS89) UTM32.
As the points are in a local reference frame
with an arbitrary heading, the rotation and translation to the ENU frame, which the
RTK points are defined in has, to be estimated. This is achieved using Singular Value
Decomposition (SVD). Having applied the obtained rotation and translation to the picked
points, error can be estimated using the Euclidean norm.
Besides evaluating LIO-SAM in the Many Keyframes and Interpolation methods described
Section 5.2.4, 4 different runs within each method are conducted based on the data ac-
quired:
Round 1: Clockwise survey in the canal without objects of interest deployed.
Round 2: Clockwise survey in the canal with objects of interest deployed.
Round 3: Lawnmower pattern survey at deployment area, before passing the first bridge.
Both objects are deployment.
Full: One continuous run over all 3 rounds. This is used to address data collection over a
longer time period.
The 4 runs will be treated as individual experiments, and reference points are selected for
each of the 8 maps collected.
5.3
Calibration through Experiment
As a consequence of the stereo SONAR setup, misalignment in terms of their relative
placement becomes visible on the seabed as consecutive horizontal leading edge should
overlap with the line produced by the vertical SONAR. Ensuring alignment will ultimately
increase the accuracy and performance of the stereo method.
118
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

Figure 5.19: The features plotted in the constructed 3D environment in the 40◦tilt setting.

the side walls, indicating correct rotation. In b), the discrepancy of the horizontal sonar
is clear as the points are not at the bottom of the pool. c) shows the clear vertical edge
of the pole, which before calibration is ahead of the pole. d) gives a 3D overview.
Figure 5.20 shows a snippet of the seabed constructed using LIO-SAM using Many Keyframe.

Figure 5.21 shows the result of applying these adjustments. The tilt in the vertical can
appear for several reasons. However, mainly three things are suspected:
1. The aluminum adapter at the end of the pole connecting the SONARs appeared to
have some slack, potentially causing a slight tilt in the mounting configuration.
2. The Blueview SONAR was not mounted perfectly orthogonally. Its round shape,
along with that of the mount, allows for unintentional rotation around the x-axis.
Alignment relied on subtle indicators that allowed human error.
3. The pole, detachable from the USV, was slightly tilted. However, this is unlikely to
be the cause of the observed tilt.
Both the first and second possibilities have been examined. Rotating the BlueView around
its axis did not improve mapping accuracy. However, adjusting the tilt angle of the SONAR
joint by 6 degrees appeared to align the extracted features, as shown in Figure 5.22.

5.4
Results
The following results evaluate the mapping capabilities of the USV SLAM system. First,
the accuracy of the SONAR configuration is assessed through experiments in ASTA using
the Optitrack system. This is followed by tests of the complete USV setup in Sydhavnen,
where the accuracy of LIO-SAM is evaluated against RTK reference points. Potential
configuration issues are then examined through the overlap between sonar configurations.
Finally, a comparison between the ground and the seabed is conducted in areas that share
structures.
5.4.1
ASTA Experiment
To evaluate the accuracy of the ASTA experiments, a simplified geometric representation
of the pool is used, consisting of 5 planes (4 walls and the seabed) and the two cylinders
in the center of the pool. The error of a point estimate is determined by the minimum
absolute Euclidean distance to any of the defined planes or cylinders. Let p be a point
in world coordinates si The absolute distance to a surface defined as point si and normal
vector ni is:
dplane(p, si, ni) =

∥ni∥

(5.69)

dcyl(p, cj, dj, rj) = |∥(p −cj) −((p −cj) · dj) dj∥−rj| .
(5.70)
The combined error function given a point and the set of surfaces S and cylinders C is:
e(p) = min

5.5
Discussion
The results demonstrate that LIO-SAM can generate a centimeter-level accurate map in
the given marine environment, with a mean error ranging between 0.278 and 0.066 meters
across experiments. The two approaches, Interpolation and Many Keyframes, produced
mixed results across experiments, with Many Keyframes exhibiting the highest and lowest
performance based on the mean values. Given the limited number of reference points, it
remains inconclusive which approach performs best based on Euclidean distance to the
reference point.
However, an inspection of the walls constructed canal walls from above reveals a shift in
the wall, introducing noise and inaccuracy. This was not observed for the interpolation
method. It is suspected that an excessive number of keyframes introduces more noise
than useful information to the map, leading to gradual accumulation of noise and a risk
of misaligned keyframes.
Another drawback with the Many keyframes approach is the sheer amount of accumulated
data given the increased number of keyframes that accumulate during longer surveys.
Appendix F.5, Table F.2 shows that the file size of the saved maps increased with up to
285% compared to the Interpolation approach, which could pose challenges for long-term
deployments and mapping of larger environments.
A key question that remains to be answered is whether the loss in accuracy of using in-
terpolation is significant compared to the alternative. While the similarity measures did
not reveal anything conclusive evidence of major accuracy loss, comparing the wall plots
showed minor misalignments between the surface and seabed map when using interpola-
tion. However, given that the primary goal is object detection for further inspection, the
margin of error might allow this. To further improve the interpolation approach, IMU
measurements could be incorporated to refine the scans’ rotation and position in between
updates, similar to the de-skewing process of LiDAR clouds in LIO-SAM.
Beyond localization errors, SONAR system inaccuracies also contribute to mapping errors.
Using two SONAR units has uncovered configuration errors that might have gone unno-
ticed in a single SONAR setup. Tests in a controlled environment revealed unexpected
offsets of the acoustic centers of both SONARs. The found offsets raise additional ques-
tions as this places the acoustic center outside of the physical device. Therefore, further
testing and clear documentation are required However, applying the center offsets indi-
cated a substantial decrease in error for the vertical SONAR. Despite efforts in aligning
the horizontal SONAR, no real decrease in error was observed in the pool. This can be
caused by the reflections in the pools, which were observed to be more prominent on the
Oculus sonar. The stereo approach didn’t improve by calibration based on the distribution
of errors, indicating that other factors induce the high level of residuals. It is suspected
that using two different SONAR models that are differently calibrated can cause issues
during feature matching, as the intensity level of the inspected and surrounding pixels are
used. To increase the robustness of the algorithm, further investigation is recommended.
To address the accuracy of the system, additional testing should be conducted in a larger
pool, lowering the amount of noise from reflections. Another suggestion is to validate
the implemented stereo setup through simulation, to address the system in a controlled
environment.
Another source of errors is mechanical and deployment-related errors. The misalignment
of horizontal and vertical scans revealed an unintentional tilt applied to the joint SONAR.
Though the exact cause remains uncertain, it is suggested that it is likely due to mechan-
ical slack in the mount between the joint and pole. However, the applied correction may
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
131
==PAGE:160==
have introduced additional inaccuracies. Furthermore, the large Hellinger distances be-
tween SONAR and LiDAR points at walls suggest that the orientation of the pole or the
connection between the SONAR mount and pole has not been aligned accurately enough
during deployment, resulting in the displacement observed between the KDEs.
Inspection of the seabed map reveals that the both seabed and unknown objects are
mapped in a meaningful representation, indicating that denoising has proved effective on
the collected data. Investigation the different methods contribution to seabed mapping,
shows that the horizontal leading edge has the most significant contribution to the map-
ping, revealing irregularities of the seabed. It is yet to be proved that the stereo SONAR
setup provides useful information that cannot be captured by the horizontal scan. Besides
the already addressed potential issues, mounting the SONARs at a less tilted angle is
suggested as this expands the covered area significantly which the stereo setup can take
advantage of. Another suggestion is to optimize the eﬀiciency of the code, as the current
implementation operates at approximately 2 Hz, requiring the USV to sail at a lower speed
for full coverage in between scans.
5.6
Chapter Summary
This concludes the chapter on the USV SLAM which covered the following:
• Custom filtering of SONAR images which are contaminated with excessive noise and
artifacts.
• There are 3 different contributions to seabed mapping, two of which are the SONAR
to cartesian mapping of features extracted using leading edge and the last being a
Stereo SONAR method for 3D mapping. The latter has been extended from the
original paper to allow arbitrary SONAR configurations.
• Post calibration of the stereo sonar setup was required as the stereo sonar setup
revealed misalignment.
• The LIO-SAM method and how it has been extended to include the extracted seabed
features from the SONARs, proposing two approaches to how mapping can be im-
proved. The ground has been assessed to be cm-level accurate. Interpolation results
in a sparser graph at the price of lower accuracy but maintains a detailed mapping
of the seabed.

This section will illuminate how the problem of detecting un-
derwater objects utilizing the point cloud map was tackled,
which reflects the subsystem of the overall architecture shown
in 6.1. Firstly, a data generation process for synthetic data
will be presented. Subsequently, both a baseline and the 3D

6.1
Data Generation
As previously mentioned deep learning methods make use of large and diverse datasets to
learn the inherent patterns in complex tasks. Under the paradigm of supervised learning
for 3D object recognition, models must be exposed to an extensive collection of labeled
data to capture the variations of poses, shapes, occlusions, and environmental conditions.
This requirement for large datasets also introduces significant challenges, such as the cost
for data acquisition and data enrichment by 3D annotations. The lack of large-scale 3D
underwater datasets leads to exploring synthetic data generation. Harnessing synthetic
data provides a way to gather domain-specific samples at scale, and allows for testing
the model under controlled and reproducible conditions, all without the need for manual
annotation.
Figure 6.2:
Section of canal bottom gen-
erated applying 2D Perlin noise, where the
color represents the Z height of the points,
with blue indicating lower elevations and
warmer colors (yellow to red) representing
higher elevations.
This thesis only focuses on synthetic data
that imitates the environment reflecting
the data campaign, namely a narrow canal.
In addition multiple simplifications have
been made to the environment, etc. lack of
vegetation, lack of significant elevation dif-
ferences, lack of structure inherent to walls,
and disentangled objects.
2D Perlin Noise
To
mimic
the
naturally
irregular
and
smoothly
varying
topography
of
the
seabed, 2D Perlin noise is employed. This
technique generates continuous, pseudo-
random gradients that replicate the subtle
undulations and texture variations found
in underwater terrains. The following de-
scription is based on [175].
2D Perlin noise builds on the assumption that an area can be covered by a n × n unit
grid.
For any point (x, y) in this unit grid where 0 ≤x, y ≤n, the corner points
(x0, y0), (x1, y0), (x0, y1) and (x1, y1) are given by:
x0 = ⌊x⌋
and
x1 = (x0 + 1)
mod n,
(6.1)
y0 = ⌊y⌋
and
y1 = (y0 + 1)
mod n,
(6.2)
where ⌊⌋defines the flooring operation.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
133
==PAGE:162==
Figure 6.3: 2-dimensional Perlin noise generation. Source [175].
The next step is to produce a random 2-dimensional gradient vector at each vertex of the
grid, defined as g[0,0], g[0,1], g[1,0] and g[1,1]. If a single square of the grid is considered, for
each point in the interior grid (x, y), the effects of this gradient are blended. An initial
step for this is to create the following vectors from each corner to the point (x, y).
v[0,0] = (x, y) −(x0, y0)
and
v[0,1] = (x, y) −(x0, y1),
(6.3)
v[1,0] = (x, y) −(x1, y0)
and
v[1,1] = (x, y) −(x1, y1).
(6.4)
The vertical displacement δ is defined for each corner in the square, which indicates the
height of the point (x, y). The intuition is that if the corresponding v and g vectors are
directed in the same direction, the vertical displacement will increase, while δ will decrease
when pointed in opposite direction. The dot product fulfils this behavior. It also scales
linearly with the length.
δ[0,0] = v[0,0] · g[0,0]
and
δ[0,1] = v[0,1] · g[0,1],
(6.5)
δ[1,0] = v[1,0] · g[1,0]
and
δ[1,1] = v[1,1] · g[1,1].
(6.6)
However, this linear dependency, as well as the displacement values being affected by all
the corner values, introduce unwanted behavior. The gradient effect should apply closely
to the corner points and quickly drop off when approaching another vertex. For this the
fade function ψ(t) is defined in eq.6.7. As t ∈[0, 1] approaches 1, the fading becomes more
aggressive while no fading is applied at ψ(t) = 0. Also, both the first and second order
derivatives at the endpoints are zero.
ψ(t) = 6t5 −15t4 + 10t3,
t ∈[0, 1].
(6.7)
The joint fade function Ψ(x, y) is defined as the fade function product of x and y
Ψ(x, y) = ψ(x)ψ(y).
(6.8)
Now this joint fading function is applied differently to each of the vertices, such that the
final noise of the point (x, y) is a weighted average of the gradient displacements.
noise(x, y) = Ψ(1 −x, 1 −y)δ[0,0] + Ψ(x, 1 −y)δ[1,0] + Ψ(1 −x, y)δ[0,1] + Ψ(x, y)δ[1,1]. (6.9)
134
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:163==
Finally, the concept of octaves and persistance is introduced. Octaves refer to different
layers or frequencies of sound. The idea is to sample Perlin noise at increasing frequencies
and sum together their contributions.
To prevent high-frequency noise from dominating, each subsequent octave is scaled down
by a persistence factor p. Thus, lower frequency octaves will have a higher impact on
forming large-scale structure, while higher frequency octaves contributed to more fine-
grained variations.
perlin(x, y) =
k
X
i=0
pi · noise(2i · x, 2i · y).
(6.10)
For the canal generation, points are first sampled uniformly from a rectangular plane acting
as the seabed, and Perlin noise is added as the z coordinate to emulate a more naturally
occurring terrain.
Hereafter, from two perpendicular planes to the seabed, points are
again uniformly sampled to create the canal walls. Each canal dimensions are randomly
sampled with the following parameters:
h ∼U(2, 6), l ∼U(12.5, 37.5), w ∼U(6, 18),
(6.11)
to reflect the dimensionality of the real-world environment. All units are in meters.
Simple shapes, like cubes, spheres, cylinders, triangular prisms, and toruses, are then
generated by again uniformly sampling along their surface area. A center point for the
object is selected by randomly sampling a point within the limits of the canal. Hereafter,
the objects are generated with the following sampled parameters:
• Sphere: Radius r ∼U(0.1, 0.5).
• Cube: Side s ∼U(0.1, 1).
• Cylinder: Radius rinner ∼U(0.1, 0.5), router ∼U(0.5, 1), height h ∼U(0.25, 0.75).
• Triangular Prism: Length ℓ∼U(0.25, 0.75), height h ∼U(0.25, 0.75).
• Torus: Radius r1 ∼U(0.21, 0.45), r2 ∼U(0.1, 0.2).
By again sampling three random angles {α, β, γ} ∈[0, 2π], the object is rotated along all
three axes by the following rotation matrix.
R = Rz(α)Ry(β)Rx(γ)
(6.12)
Utilizing that the object’s limits are known at generation time, an axes-aligned bounding
box is associated with each object along with the object class. Pseudocode demonstrating
this approach is provided in Appendix G.1.
6.2
Point cloud object detection
Object detection refers to the task of identifying and localizing specific objects within a
given scene. While 2D object detection aims to localize objects by rectangular bounding
boxes within images, 3D object detection extends this concept into three-dimensional
space. Here, the goal is to detect objects not only by their spatial position but also by
their volumetric space and orientation.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
135
==PAGE:164==
(a) Examples of generated shapes, including a
sphere, cube, cylinder, torus, and triangular
prism.
(b) A generated canal containing a random num-
ber of objects, with corresponding bounding
boxes.
Figure 6.4: a) shows the generated shapes, while b) shows the objects placed in the
generated canal
6.2.1
Baseline
In order to evaluate the performance gain offered by more advanced deep learning meth-
ods for point cloud object detection, a more well-understood and interpretable baseline
is established. The following section details the baseline’s components, starting with the
application of Random Sample Consensus (RANSAC) for identifying large planar struc-
tures, followed by a graph-based clustering technique to detect and segment individual
objects within the remaining point cloud.
Initially, RANSAC is used to localize the wall and seabed of the canal. RANSAC is an
iterative process used to robustly fit a parametric model, in this case a plane, to a dataset.
This method is effective here as it prioritizes the model supported by the largest number
of inliers, ensuring robustness against the presence of objects within the scene. A more
detailed description of RANSAC in 3D can be found in Appendix I.
After removing the dominant planes detected by RANSAC, the remaining point cloud is
represented as a graph, since a graph G per definition encodes the pairwise relationships
between a set of objects. A graph consists of two components - a set of V vertices and a
set of E edges, each of which connects two vertices. Thereby, an edge is represented by
e ∈E as a two-element subset of V : e = {u, v} for some u, v ∈V [176]. Each vertex has
a degree deg(v), which is the number of edges that are connected with the vertex. In this
context, this will be used to locate zero-degree or isolated vertices that are removed from
the graph.
The entirety of the filtered point cloud is viewed as a single graph.
Two vertices are
connected if they are within a radius of 4 cm of each other. This graph can be decomposed
into subgraphs based on the connected components in the graph. A component in a graph
is based on the vertices that are connected via an edge, where small components |V | < 10
are removed.
The pseudocode presented in Algorithm 2 summarizes the approach.

Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:165==

17: end procedure
6.2.2
Attention
This section introduces the concept of attention, a foundational component of the trans-
former architecture utilized by the 3D DEtection TRansformer (3DETR)[87]. Understand-
ing attention mechanisms is crucial because they enable the model to capture complex
relationships within spatial data without relying on localized, convolution-based opera-
tions. Attention equips transformers with the ability to selectively consider interactions
across the entire input, enhancing their capability to identify global patterns for 3D object
detection[177]. This is a departure from the previously dominating model architecture ex-
ploring sequences or sets, namely recurrent neural networks (RNN). Instead of processing
the entire input set in a strictly sequential manner, attention allows the model to draw
correlations among elements regardless of their positions.
At the core of the transformer architecture are queries and keys k with dimension dk and
values v with dimension dv. These inputs are collected into the matrices K, V, and Q
respectively. The intuition is that the values contain something interesting about the data
that the model has inferred during training. The keys act as an index, while the query asks
the model about certain things in the input. Attention can thereby be seen as mapping a
query and a set of key-value pairs to an output. The attention operation is computed as
follows:
Attention(Q, K, V) = Softmax
 
QK⊤
√dk
!
V
(6.13)
The attention operation utilizes the dot product to determine the angle between vectors,
and in high-dimensional space, many of these angles are close to orthogonal. Thereby,
when the model is queried, the keys that are more co-directional will return higher values
in the dot product. Orthogonal vectors return zero, due to the definition of the dot product
a · b = ||a||||b|| cos(θ). Since QK⊤measures the compatibility between queries and keys, a
higher dot product means a closer relationship. Now, essentially, the keys that yield the
highest results from a given query are desired. The issue is that the maximum function is
non-differentiable.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
137
==PAGE:166==

process[177].
Finally, the attention weights are used to weight the values of V. After determining how
closely each key aligns with the query, the corresponding values are scaled accordingly and
summed. This allows the model to retrieve precisely the pieces of information (from V)
that are most relevant to the current query. Thereby effectively selecting and combining
the information in a non-sequential manner. The mechanism is illustrated in Figure 6.6.
Figure 6.6: Illustration of attention mechanisms. Left: Scaled Dot-Product Attention,
which computes attention scores using the dot product of queries (Q) and keys (K), scales
the result, applies a mask (optional), and normalizes with a SoftMax function before
weighting the values (V ). Right: Multi-Head Attention, which extends the scaled dot-
product attention by applying multiple attention mechanisms in parallel (each with its
own set of linear projections) and concatenating their outputs, followed by a final linear
transformation. Figure is from: [177].
A single attention mechanism may struggle to capture all the diverse relationships present
138
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:167==
in the data. Therefore, the concept of multi-head attention is introduced, allowing the
model to jointly attend to information from different representation subspaces at different
positions. This is achieved by running multiple attention operations, known as heads, in
parallel, enabling each learning to focus on different types of information within the data.
Formally, multi-head attention is defined as follows:
MultiHead(Q, K, V) = Concat(head1, . . . , headn)W0,
(6.14)
headi = Attention(QWQ
i , KWW
i , VWV
i ).
(6.15)
Here, h denotes the number of heads. Each head i independently computes a scaled dot-
product attention (eq. 6.13) but with its own set of learned parameters. The projection
matrices for each head have dimensions: WQ
i
∈Rdmodel×dk, WK
i
∈Rdmodel×dk, WV
i
∈
Rdmodel×dv. The outputs from all heads are concatenated and transformed by the final
linear layer WO ∈Rhdv×dmodel to produce the final multi-head attention representation.
By splitting the model’s latent dimension dmodel into h smaller subspaces, each head
learns to capture different relationships or patterns in the data. After each head computes
its attention output, these outputs are concatenated along the feature dimension and
transformed by W0.
This linear transformation fuses the information from all heads,
enabling the final representation to encode multiple views of the input. This allows multi-
head attention to model complex dependencies because each head’s learned perspective
can emphasize different parts of the inputs. This multi-head attention is visualized in
Figure 6.6, right side. In the context of 3D object detection, different heads might specialize
in capturing various spatial cues or shape characteristics from the input point cloud [87].
The mechanisms described above are often implemented as self-attention, where the queries,
keys, and values all originate from the same source. Self-attention allows each element of
the input to interact with every other element, enabling the model to capture dependencies
regardless of their positional distance.
In contrast, cross-attention is employed when the queries and key-value pairs come from
different sources. For example, in an encoder-decoder architecture, the decoder may use
cross-attention to focus on different parts of the encoder’s output. This enables the model
to combine information from two distinct modalities or sources, making cross-attention a
powerful tool for tasks such as machine translation, where the relationship between the
source and target language is critical. In the context of 3D object detection, self-attention
might be used to capture relationships within the spatial features of a point cloud, whereas
cross-attention could be useful when combining information from multiple modalities or
different feature representations.
6.2.3
3DETR
Unlike traditional convolution-based approaches, which constrain the receptive field to lo-
cal neighborhoods, attention mechanisms flexibly adapt to global context. This capability
enables the archetecture to capture long-range spatial relationships within the point cloud,
leading to enhanced detection performance, especially for objects distributed sparsely or
at varying scales throughout the 3D environment.
The idea behind 3D DEtection TRansformer (3DETR)[87] is that it takes a point cloud
and predicts the position of potential objects in a given scene based on bounding boxes.
More specifically, the object detection is framed as a set prediction problem, which means
that a set of bounding boxes are simultaneously predicted with no specific order[88].
To make the computations tractable, due to the large number of points, the input point
cloud is downsampled with farthest point sampling[178]. Farthest point sampling is a
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
139
==PAGE:168==
greedy algorithm that works by selecting a random initial point and adding it to a set
S. For each iteration, the point that is the farthest distance from S is added until N
points are sampled this way.
This approach ensures that the sampled points are well
spread out across the point cloud, leading to a uniform coverage. This uniform sampling
preserves the representativeness of the input data without biasing certain regions.
A
potential downside of this compared to a density-based sampling approach is the loss of
region-specific resolution under the assumption that regions of interest are more frequently
sampled during data collection. In this work, the input cloud is downsampled to N´ =
2048 points.
Figure 6.7: (Left) The model processes an input point cloud and produces a set of 3D
bounding boxes in an end-to-end manner. A Transformer encoder computes per-point
features through multiple layers of self-attention. These features, together with learnable
query embeddings, are then passed to a Transformer decoder that outputs bounding box
predictions.
A set-based matching loss aligns the predicted boxes with their ground-
truth counterparts. Color information is shown only for visualization and is not used by
the model. (Right) A random subset of points is sampled as “query” points, which are
embedded and subsequently decoded into the final bounding box predictions. Figure is
from: [87].
Next step is set-aggregation that provides a set of N´ features of d = 256. This pro-
cess ensures that the model’s operations are permutation invariant, meaning the output is
consistent regardless of the order of input points. This invariance is important for point
cloud data, where the sequence of points is arbitrary. To achive permutation invariance,
each point xm in the set X is transformed into a latent representation ϕ(xm) through a
neural network of fully connected layers. In the context, the transformation ϕ consists of
two hidden layers with idmensions 64 and 128, respectively. The trasformed representa-
tions are then aggregated by P
x∈X ϕ(x). This aggregated result is subsequently passed
through another function ρ, implemented as an additional neural network, to produce the
final output:
f (X) = ρ
 X
x∈X
ϕ(x)
!
.
(6.16)
This formulation aligns with the theoretical framework established in Deep Sets[179],
which asserts that any permutation-invariant function can be decomposed into such a
form, with appropriate choices of ϕ and ρ. By structuring the set aggregation operation
in this manner, order-independent processing of the input data is achieved.
From this an encoder/decoder transformer architecture is applied to the resulting features
X ∈RN´×d, as seen in figure 6.8. Here the encoder maps an input x = (x1, . . . , xN´) to a
set of continuous representation z = (z1, . . . , zN´)[177].
More specifically, the encoder preserves the embedding size during the forward pass.
Here, the multiple layers of self-attention with the standard attention formulation Equa-
tion (6.15) and non-linear projections are done. Positional embeddings are omitted from
the encoder as the input already contains geometric information about the (x, y, z) co-
ordinates. A point here is that no specific modifications are done to accommodate 3D

Hereafter, two learnable parameters γ and β that respectively represent scale and shift are

shown to ensure more stable hidden-state distribution, improving training stability[181].
Bounding box prediction
Now, in this context, a 3D bounding box is defined by four main attributes: (a) location,
(b) size, (c) orientation, and (d) semantic class. In the approach adopted here, the en-
coder/decoder architecture produces a set of B latent features that, after a forward pass
through multiple FNNs, predict the bounding boxes.
The location of the bounding box is determined by the (x, y, z) coordinates of its center,
denoted by c.
Due to the transformer architecture of selecting queries, the center is
predicted with an offset ∆q from the query point q to the center c, that together forms
the center c = q + ∆q.
Each box is parameterized by the size around the center coordinate c using xyz dimensions
d. For the orientation of the bounding boxes, the models splits the angles into two pa-
rameters. Firstly, the angles are quantized into 12 bins in the interval [0, 2π], creating an
angle ”class”, where as the residual angle is noted as its own parameter. The angular pre-
diction is done by predicting the quantized angle class together with the residual to form
the continuous angle a. Lastly, the semantic class is represented via a one-hot encoded
vector s, reflecting the class within the bounding box. A ”not object” or ”background”
class is also included, as some of the predicted bounding boxes do not contain any objects.
All together this form two different sets for each scene, one for the ground truth b =
[c, d, a, s] and one for the predictions ^b = [^c, ^d,^a,^s]. Specifically, ^a = [^ac,^ar] again de-
fined the quantized class and residual angle, while ^s ∈[0, 1]k+1 return the probability
distribution over the k different classes in addition to the background class.
The bounding box predictions are generated by applying separate MLPs to the decoder’s
output features (B ×256). Each MLP predicts one of the following parameters: (1) center
location offset ∆q ∈[0, 1]3, (2) angle quantization class, (3) angle quantization residual
∈R, (4) box size s ∈[0, 1]3, and (5) semantic class of the object. Each MLP consists of
one hidden layer with 256 dimensions and uses ReLU activation. Outputs for the center
offset and box size are further processed by a sigmoid function to constrain predictions
within [0, 1].
Putting this together the following loss function is defined as a bipartite matching problem,
between the set of B predicted bounding boxes {^b} and the set of ground truth bounding
boxes {b}, using the Hungarian method[182]. The matching cost for a pair of bounding
boxes is defined with both a geometric and semantic term:
Cmatch(ˆb, b) = −λ1GIoU(ˆb, b) + λ2∥ˆc −c∥1
|
{z
}
geometric
−λ3ˆs[sgt] + λ4
 1 −ˆs[sbg]

|
{z
}
semantic
.
(6.19)
The first term, the Generalised Intersection over Union GIoU[183] is an extension of the
Intersection over Union (IoU), also known as the Jacard index, for measuring the similarity
between two arbitrary volumes A, B ⊆S ∈Rn is defined as:
IoU(A, B) = |A ∩B|
|A ∪B|.
(6.20)
142
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:171==
IoU is an important and widely used tool in the training and evaluating for many different
2D/3D computer vision applications due to its appealing properties. One is that IoU is
a metric, namely that LIoU = 1 −IoU, also known as the Jacard distance[184]. LIoU, for
instance, fulfills all the properties of a metric, namely the triangle inequality, symmetry,
non-negativity, and identity of indiscernibles. Another property is that IoU encodes shape
properties into region properties normalized over the common volume.
This makes it
invariant to scale, meaning that the similarity of A and B are independent from the scale
of the subspace S. The issue of IoU arises if |A ∩B| = 0, which results in IoU(A, B) = 0.
In this case the IoU does not reflect weather the prediction are far from or close to the
ground truth.
In the cases with no intersection, IoU has no value and therefore, no
gradient. Posing an issue from a machine learning standpoint.
To address this, the Generalised Intersection over Union GIoU was proposed. Here for
two convex volumes A, B ⊆S ∈Rn, the smallest convex hull C ⊆S ∈Rn that surround
both A and B are found. Then a ratio containing the volume of C excluding A and B is
computed and normalized with the total volume of C. GIoU is obtained by subtracting
this ratio from the IoU[183].
GIoU(A, B) = IoU −|C \ (A ∪B)|
|C|
.
(6.21)
As for IoU, GIoU acts as a metric LGIoU = 1−GIoU and is invariant to scale. In addition,
GIoU acts as a lower bound as ∀A, B ⊆S, GIoU(A, B) ≤IoU(A, B). This lower bound
becomes tighter as limA→B GIoU(A, B) = IoU(A, B).
The sign of the λ parameters in eq. 6.19 reflects that the GIoU(^b, b) and ^s[sgt] should be
maximized while the ||^c −c|| and (1 −^s[sbg]) terms should be minimized to get an optimal
matching cost. [sgt] indicates taking the index of the vector that has the ground truth,
while [sbg] is for the background.
The complete loss function is given by:
L = λc∥ˆc −c∥1 + λd∥ˆd −d∥1 + λar∥ˆar −ar∥Huber −λaca⊤
c log ˆac −λss⊤

(6.22)

more robust to outliers.

For predictions matched to the ”background” class, only the semantic classification loss is
computed.
The implementation uses the PyTorch framework with the standard nn.MultiheadAttention
module for the transformer architecture. The encoder consists of 3 layers, each employing
multi-head attention with 4 heads followed by a two-layer FNN, featuring a bottleneck of
128 dimensions. The decoder is composed of 8 layers and mirrors the encoder structure,
except that the FNNs in the decoder have a hidden dimension of 256. Bounding box
predictions are generated by dedicated two-layer FNNs with a hidden dimension of 256.
All FNNs and self-attention modules utilize a dropout of 0.1 for regularization, except
for the decoder, which employs a higher dropout of 0.3. For optimization an AdamW
optimizer[185] with a cosine learning rate decay schedule to 10−6, a weight decay of 0.1,
and a gradient clipping applied at a norm of 0.1. Gradient clipping limits the maximum
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

norm of gradients during back-propagation by rescaling them when they exceed a spec-
ified threshold, in this case, 0.1. This helps stabilize the training process by preventing
excessively large updates that could lead to divergence or instability.

during the synthetic data curation pipeline are all axis-aligned. This means that the terms

6.3
Results
The Results section begins with a presentation of the training loss, followed by an eval-
uation of the baseline performance on the synthetic dataset. Next, the performance of
3DETR on synthetic data is discussed, and finally, its performance on real-world maps
constructed in Chapter 5 is assessed.
6.3.1
Training
The 3DETR model was trained on a dataset comprising 25,000 synthetically generated
seabed scenes, with an additional 1,000 scenes reserved for validation. Training was con-
ducted over 350 epochs using an NVIDIA RTX 4080 GPU, completing in just under three
days. A batch size of 8 scenes was employed, and the training process utilized an average
of 10.83 GB of GPU memory, setting an upper limit on the batch size given the hardware.
Figure 6.9 illustrates the loss progression as defined in Equation (6.25).
The training
loss exhibits an initial sharp decline during the early epochs, followed by a slow, gradual
decrease accompanied by significant fluctuations. This pattern suggests that the model
quickly captures basic patterns in the data but encounters challenges in refining its per-
formance.
6.3.2

The baseline effectively clusters the various objects, as shown in Figure 6.10. However,
it has been noted that some areas of the seabed are grouped together as a single object.

For evaluating the performance of the object detection, the predictions will be compared
to the ground-truth. In object detection, each detection is considered a positive match
if it has a suﬀiciently large overlap between the ground truth and prediction, typically
measured by the previously mentioned IoU (eq. 6.20). If IoU(Bprediction, Bgroundtruth) ≥τ
it is considered a true positive (TP). If IoU(Bprediction, Bgroundtruth) < τ or if the model
predicts an object that is not in the ground truth it is denoted as a false positive (FP).
And lastly if an object is present but the model does not predict it, it is counted as a false
negative (FN). From these definitions, precision and recall are computed as follows:
Precision =
TP
TP + FP,
Recall =
TP
TP + FN.
(6.26)
Precision is the measure of how accurate the predictions are, while recall reflects the ability
to find all positive cases. Thereby, a kind of pseudo inverse forms between precision and
recall.
As recall increases, more false positives are expected to be registered, in turn
lowering the precision.
From this, a precision-recall curve can be constructed class-wise that shows how precision
changes as recall increases. The average precision (AP) is calculated by taking the area
underneath the curve, resulting in the integral of precision p over the recall r from 0 to 1:
AP =
Z 1
0
p(r)dr.
(6.27)

==PAGE:174==

specific recall points rn. In doing so, the integral is approximated by small rectangular
segments under the precision-recall curve:
AP =
X

˜r≥rn+1 p(˜r).
(6.28)
Here, rn+1−rn is the width of a recall interval while pinterp(rn+1) is the maximum precision
value over all recall values ˜r greater than rn+1. The mean Average Precision (mAP) is

6.4
Discussion
Investigating the validity of the baseline quickly reveals negative results, primarily due to
the ”hardcoded” nature of the approach. Specifically, this approach heavily relies on prior
geometric knowledge of the environment, such as the assumption that the canal consists of
three relatively planar surfaces. In scenarios involving significant global depth variations,
RANSAC would fail to adequately separate the seabed, resulting in large portions being
incorrectly classified as objects. The clustering process itself is highly sensitive to the ra-
dius parameter, where even minor adjustments result in substantial changes to clustering
outcomes. Although this baseline implementation is simplistic, it effectively highlights
issues inherent to traditional object detection approaches, notably their dependence on
148
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:177==
handcrafted features, such as local or global feature descriptors, and sensitivity to hyper-
parameters. These issues become increasingly evident when examining traditional object
detection methods, which are manually engineered for specific objects but lack flexibility
in unknown environments characterized by deformable geometries, occlusions, and other
complexities [79].
The results from the synthetic data study are promising despite the relatively large area
a given scene spans.
For the 0.25 IoU threshold, generally, a high class-wise average
precision is observed, which, however, quickly decreases when increasing the IoU threshold.
Notably, a significantly lower average precision is observed from the triangular prism.
This is attributed to the heavy down-sampling of the point cloud before it is passed to the
encoder. Passing the entire point cloud that represents a larger area results in a significant
loss of local resolution, as seen in Figure 6.12. This can result in ambiguity between the
triangular prism and other objects. Another factor is that the prism is generally a smaller
object compared to the others, leading to even more information loss during FPS down-
sampling. A potential solution would be to divide the point cloud into sub-clouds, such
that more information is preserved.
The results obtained from the main data campaign coincide with the expected results,
namely that no objects were detected.
For the 3D reconstruction of the ASTA pool
experiments, a high level of noise was observed, which makes the detection task unlikely
due to the well-behaved and low-noise training data. This leads to an inconclusive analysis
of whether the approach is suitable for real-world object detection of similar geometry
compared to the training data, which would have to be further explored.
It is observed that object queries often attend to points far from the target object, as seen
in Figure 6.14. This behavior can be attributed to the model’s reliance on global context,
which, while capturing comprehensive scene information, may lead to attention dispersion
across the point cloud. Consequently, the model’s focus is not suﬀiciently constrained to
the immediate vicinity of the target object, leading to less accurate detections. This might
also be a factor in the significant decline in the average precision score when raising the
IoU threshold. If the model attends across the entire scene instead of focusing locally, it
is more diﬀicult to form tight and properly aligned bounding boxes. Thereby, introducing
mechanisms to enforce the locality principle in 3D object detection would assist in con-
straining the attention mechanism to focus on the vicinity of the object. One candidate
would be the V-DETR model[186] that incorporates a 3D vertex relative position encod-
ing, which for each 3D box predicted by the queries, computes a relative position encoding
for each point.
One of the key insights of 3DETR is the ability to exploit global context among point
features rather than relying heavily on localized operations (e.g., 3D convolution or region
proposal pooling). The Transformer’s multi-head self-attention layers can capture inter-
actions among all points, which can be particularly powerful in 3D scenes that contain
clutter, occlusions, or complex arrangements. Still, 3DETR retains a simple single-stage
philosophy: After extracting the global and local features through self-attention, it pre-
dicts bounding boxes and class labels directly from these features, avoiding additional
refinement networks or proposal modules. This minimalistic approach makes 3DETR a
natural base for further innovations in 3D detection. Further innovations have occurred
primarily in the 2D space for object detection using the DETR architecture, as touched
upon in Section 8.1. However, some have shown that these teachings are also directly
transferable to the 3D domain. This means that currently, much performance is still on
the table, as the initial formulation of the transformer encoder-decoder architecture has
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
149
==PAGE:178==
been demonstrated to have problems with accuracy on small objects[187]. Other known
issues are as seen in Figure 6.9, that the model has a slow convergence rate that requires
training for a prolonged period of time. In addition, it is shown that the performance
decreases rapidly when decreasing the amount of labeled data, leading to large data re-
quirements[187].
These requirements also limit the possibility of ablation study, with
limited computational power available.
Transformers often require large-scale training data. While datasets like ScanNet [188],
SUN RGB-D [189], and KITTI [91] exist, there is still a gap in data volume compared
to 2D image benchmarks, potentially hindering the full potential of Transformer-based
architectures. Also, the given datasets are either highly focused on indoor environments
or in the context of autonomous driving. This leaves much to be desired for looking into
more domain-specific datasets for underwater environments. In this thesis, a synthetic
data generate pipeline for 3D data was suggested. However, as mentioned, the many sim-
plifications, such as lack of realistic noise, deformations, and occlusions, lead to unrealistic
information-rich representation compared to a real-world dataset.
Thereby, looking into the use of other simulation tools or game engines to include more
complex geometries or occlusions and viewing angles would be an appropriate continuation.
Also recently many foundation models have suggested their own synthetic data generation
pipeline based on learned features from real world data be able to generalize outside of
their training data distribution[190][191][114].
Under a synthetic data engine pipeline
that reflects the properties observed in real data, arbitrarily large dataset can be obtained
that still retains the original data’s statistical attributes but offers more variation than
allowed for under the constrains of collecting a real world large-scale dataset. For this
paradigm, establishing such a domain-specific pipeline would be beneficial in the pursuit
of creating generalization for learning-based approaches in a field with lacking amounts of
public available data.
Most existing 3D detection methods, including 3DETR, rely on fully supervised pipelines.
Extending these approaches to zero-shot or few-shot scenarios where objects are not well
represented in the training set remains an open challenge. In other domains within com-
puter vision, zero-shot generalization capabilities have been demonstrated via the neural
scaling law[192][190][114][193][115]. The neural scaling law is an empirical law that shows
that scaling compute, dataset size, and number of parameters smoothly increase the per-
formance. Empirically, it has been shown that performance (test loss) has a power-law
relationship with each factor as long as they do not act as bottlenecks. This finding fur-
ther highlights the critical importance of data availability for enhancing the generalization
capabilities of Transformer-based 3D detection methods.
6.5
Chapter Summary
• Leveraging 2D Perlin noise proved effective in creating realistic underwater terrain
features, addressing the scarcity of labeled underwater datasets. However, this ap-
proach introduced simplifications, excluding complex structures like vegetation and
detailed wall geometry, limiting the model’s realism and applicability to actual un-
derwater scenarios.
• The RANSAC and graph-based clustering baseline effectively segmented simple ge-
ometric objects from the synthetic canal environment, achieving an IoU of 0.87.
Despite its simplicity, this approach showed sensitivity to parameter tuning and
struggled with densely packed regions and lacked semantic understanding, high-
lighting limitations of traditional methods relying on handcrafted heuristics.
150
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:179==
• The transformer-based 3DETR demonstrated strong detection performance on syn-
thetic data, particularly at a low IoU threshold (0.25). However, performance sharply
declined at higher thresholds (0.5), revealing limitations in the model’s ability to
precisely localize objects. The model also showed diﬀiculty detecting smaller objects
(triangular prisms), likely due to information loss during the down-sampling process.
• Cross-attention analysis indicated that the model’s attention often dispersed across
the entire point cloud rather than concentrating locally around objects. This disper-
sion contributed to inaccurate bounding box predictions, suggesting the potential
benefit of enforcing locality constraints within attention mechanisms.
• Evaluations on real-world data from field experiments highlighted significant perfor-
mance drops due to noisy and incomplete data. This demonstrated a crucial gap
between model performance on synthetic versus real-world datasets, emphasizing the
need for improved realism in synthetic data or robust methods capable of bridging
domain differences.

This chapter covers the implementation of the Robust Vi-
sual Inertial Odometry (ROVIO) Visual Inertial Odometry
(VIO) algorithm and the processing of the data obtained
from the UVision UScanner to ensure compatibility. It also
describes the measures taken to enhance the image quality
of the data captured in the low-light conditions of Sydhav-
nen. The algorithm is applied to determine the trajectory
in two distinct settings: A controlled experimental setup in
Autonomous Systems Test Arena (ASTA), collecting base-
line data with the OptiTrack motion capture system, and a
real-life Remotely Operated Vehicle (ROV) setup attached
to the BlueROV2 Heavy ROV in Sydhavnen, with baseline
data captured from a Ultra-short baseline (USBL) system.
The post-processing steps required to obtain meaningful re-
sults and evaluate the algorithm’s performance against the
baselines will also be accounted for, before the results of testing and deploying the algo-
rithm are presented. Finally, the algorithm’s performance will be concluded, and possible
improvements and potential for further deployments will be discussed.
7.1
ROVIO - RObust Visual Inertial Odometry
The algorithm chosen to perform VIO for the BlueROV2 Heavy is the ROVIO: RObust
Visual Inertial Odometry algorithm, developed by Bloesch et al., at the Autonomous
Systems Lab at ETH Zürich. The algorithm is based on the iterated extended Kalman
Filter (IEKF) and tightly fuses inertial and visual measurements.
Image patches are
deployed as landmark descriptors, allowing for the calculation of a photometric error,
which is used directly as innovation in the update step of the filter. The filter is formulated
in a robocentric fashion, such that landmarks are represented by a bearing vector and a
distance, allowing for undelayed initialization[60].
7.1.1
The Iterated Extended Kalman Filter
To form a complete understanding of the ROVIO VIO algorithm, it is required to know
the mathematical framework behind the state propagation and update steps that make
up the iterated extended Kalman filter. The following mathematical explanation relies on
the concepts and methods outlined in the paper for ROVIO itself[60] and [194] by Jianzhu
Huai and Xiang Gao in which a concise review of the IEKF on manifolds is presented.
The regular Kalman filter can be viewed as recursively providing the optimal solution
to the maximum likelihood estimation (MLE) problem over two subsequent time steps.
The filter’s recursive nature stems from the ability to incrementally execute the state
propagation step over discrete time steps and update estimates as new data becomes
available. Extending the MLE problem to the nonlinear domain, the extended Kalman
filter (EKF), provides an analogous solution to the first iteration step of the corresponding
Gauss-Newton optimization, making the EKF a common framework for estimating states
in discrete nonlinear systems. However, the EKF does not guarantee the optimality of its
solution since linearization errors tend to increase in cases where the linearization point
is too far away from the real solution, i.e. if the estimated state is too far away from the
true state.
152
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:181==
To mitigate this issue, the IEKF refines the estimation during the update phase by repeat-
edly linearizing the measurement model, in contrast to the EKF, which only performs this
linearization once. This multiple linearization is implemented in practice by iteratively
solving a least squares optimization problem, hence the name ”iterated” extended Kalman
filter.
Defining the Problem and its assumptions
The problem is estimating the state vector x and the associated covariance P, given a
motion model f, measurement model h, and innovation term y in the following nonlinear
discrete-time system
xk = f(xk−1, wk−1),
(7.1)
yk = h(xk, nk).
(7.2)
It should be remarked that the system is discretized at time steps ti ∈[t1, t2, · · · , tk],
implying that xk = x(tk). In the system outlined in Equation (7.1) and Equation (7.2),
wk−1 ∼N(0, Wk−1) thus represents the process noise, i.e., uncertainty encountered during
the transition from one state to the other, at timestep tk−1. Likewise, nk ∼N(0, Rk) is
the update noise, i.e. the noise introduced from new observations/measurements into the
measurement update step at timestep tk.
A Quick Note on Manifolds
Before explaining the inner workings of the IEKF further,
it is suitable to make a small note on notation in regards to manifolds. In the case of
the IEKF constituting the framework of ROVIO, it is assumed that the state vector x lies
on a differentiable manifold. Having a differentiable manifold ensures that small changes
in the state can be meaningfully approximated using linear methods. This allows for the
definition of the boxplus, (⊞), and boxminus operators, (⊟), given that y is an element
on the manifold close to x with the distance between represented by distance vector δ:
x ⊞δ = z,
(7.3)
z ⊟x = δ.
(7.4)
For the Euclidean space, the ⊞and ⊟operators correspond to ordinary + and -.
The Iterated Extended Kalman Filter
Having defined the discrete dynamic system in Equation (7.1) and Equation (7.2), repre-
senting the discrete dynamic model and the measurement model respectively, the estima-
tion process of the IEKF can be presented. First, consider the concept of a-posteriori and
a-propriori estimates: where an a-posteriori estimate is produced by the update step of
the IEKF, posterior to incorporating the measurement, the a-priori estimate is produced
by the prediction step of the IEKF prior to taking a measurement. Given an a-posteriori
estimate of the state vector x+
k−1 at timestep tk−1 and associated covariance P+

7.1.2
Multi-Level Patch Features and Photometric Error
The following section will focus on how multi-level patch features are extracted, how these
patches are employed as landmark descriptors, and how the photometric error is calculated
between a patch and an image.
ROVIO, is a landmark-based, visual inertial odometry method, which implies that land-
marks are detected in the images and modeled as a distinct 3D location in the environment.
These landmarks are then tracked for each consecutive timestep, providing the informa-
tion required to perform robust localization based on visual input. Each landmark has a
positional estimate, which is represented by a bearing vector µ and a distance parameter
ρ. In the image plane, the pixel coordinates of a landmark, p can be obtained by apply-
ing the known camera model π to the bearing vector, p = π(µ). Likewise, the bearing
vector can be obtained from the pixel coordinate by applying the inverse camera model:
µ = π−1(p). The camera model is based on the known intrinsic calibration parameters of
the cameras.
Multilevel Patches and the Image Pyramid
Every individual landmark is assigned a multilevel patch feature, with the structure P =
{P0, · · · , PL}, where each patch, Pl, represents an n × n image path, which is extracted
around the landmark at level l of an image pyramid. The principle behind using an image
pyramid is to create a multilevel patch is depicted in Figure 7.2, for an 8 × 8 patch size,
where it can be seen how the image is downsampled three times and the patch extracted
is at the same location in all levels. In this implementation of the algorithm, an image
pyramid with four levels is used, giving a down-sampling factor of 3. This was chosen as
this configuration of ROVIO has been shown to achieve good underwater odometry results
in [76]. The advantage of using a multilevel patch feature is that local texture around
a landmark is captured at four different scales without introducing the computational
overhead of tracking a large patch in the image’s original resolution. Consider an 8 × 8
size patch and four pyramid levels: The patch at level zero of the pyramid, i.e. the original
image, covers an 8 × 8 area of the image, with the highest level of detail. The patch at
level four is also 8×8 pixels but covers a larger area of the image due to the three levels of
downsampling, corresponding to an area of 64 × 64 pixels in the original image. However,
this comes at a cost of reduced detail.
This capture of the local texture around the landmark further allows for the tracking of
non-corner-shaped features as information is gained along the direction of the strongest
image gradient. This makes ROVIO a better-suited algorithm in low-light environments
and environments with poor texture, as it is not as reliant on tracking single-point features
in the image using the reprojection error but can instead use the photometric error between
patches in the images contained at consecutive timestamps[60]. The actual detection of
new landmarks is based on the FAST Corner detection algorithm. The principles around
obtaining new landmark candidates with this algorithm as well as how the candidates are
selected based on a scoring will be explained in greater depth in Section 7.1.4.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
155
==PAGE:184==
Figure 7.2: The employed image pyramid with a down-sampling factor of 3, for a patch size
of 8 × 8, giving the multilevel photometric patch (green). Here is shown with an example
image from the pool environment in ASTA. As the image is downsampled, the 8×8 pixel-
wide patch covers progressively more of the image, capturing local information at a larger
scale. This results in a multilevel patch descriptor, where the information immediately
around the tracked landmark retains the highest level of detail. Figure inspired by [60].
The Linear Warping Matrix
As the camera moves through the environment, the landmark moves through the image
and is seen from different perspectives, which warps the axes of the patch, causing a
certain level of distortion. As the photometric error is calculated between image patches
extracted at different timesteps in the camera frame, by evaluating the pixel-wise intensity,
this distortion must be taken into account. This is done by using a linear warping matrix,
D, ensuring that the axes of the patches align.
The linear warping matrix D is tracked with each landmark. If a pixel is detected at
coordinate p1 in the image. The bearing vector is obtained by applying the inverse camera
model µ1 = π−1(p), then using an IMU based process model, f(), the predicted bearing
vector for the subsequent image can be obtained as µ2 = f(µ1). Once again, using the
camera model π, the predicted pixel coordinate can be obtained by projecting the bearing
vector into the subsequent image, p2 = π(µ2). By concatenating the Jacobians for this
transformation by the projection model, the linear warping matrix is obtained:
D = ∂π(µ2)
∂µ2
∂f(µ1)
∂µ1
∂π−1(p1)
∂p1
,
D ∈R2×2.
(7.19)
Using this linear warping matrix, the distortion around the patch feature can be tracked
so that the photometric error can be calculated when extracting a warped patch from the
subsequent image.
Calculating the Photometric Error
The photometric error is obtained by evaluating the pixel-wise intensity error between a
patch projected into the current image at a predicted location p and then the actual patch
extracted from the image, pixel by pixel. The process is depicted in a timewise fashion in
Figure 7.3.

Figure 7.3: Calculating the photometric error for a tracked landmark for one level of the
multilevel patch. The blue arrow indicates that the visual-inertial system moves, and the
passage of one timestep.
In this way, the computation of the photometric error uses an image patch from the camera
frame at a previous timestep. Let P be some multilevel patch for a landmark, with bearing
vector µ1. The camera moves, and as the filter tracks the bearing vector as part of the
state, a new bearing vector µ2 for the subsequent timestep will be predicted by the filter.
Based on the bearing vectors for the same landmark in a subsequent image set, the linear
warping matrix D is calculated, as shown in Equation (7.19). Using the camera model,
the predicted location p of the patch in the image is calculated as p = π(µ2), in image
coordinates. Then, for each pixel with pixel coordinates pj of the patch at level l in the
multilevel patch P from the previous timestep, the photometric error between the patch
and the image Il at the corresponding pyramid level is calculated as
el,j(p, P, I, D) = Pl(pj) −aIl(psl + Dpj) −b,
(7.20)
where sl = 0.5l is a scaling factor that accounts for the down-sampling at pyramid level
l. In addition, an aﬀine intensity model consisting of scalars a and b is used to handle
changes in illumination between subsequent images.
In this manner, for a given pixel in the patch P(pj), the corresponding pixel intensity
from the image Il(psl + Dpj) is subtracted.
The linear warping matrix D maps pixel
coordinates to image coordinates and takes care of the distortion of the patch that has
occurred due to the movement of the camera. The process of calculating the photometric
error between a patch and the measurement at predicted location p is shown in Figure 7.4.
With a definition of the photometric error for a single pixel at a specified level of the
multilevel patch, el,j, this is extended to the entire multilevel patch.
Minimizing the
squared photometric error for a multilevel patch, thus minimizing over several levels at
once, a patch alignment algorithm is obtained that tracks the patches over subsequent
images. Introducing the small change in notation that the predicted patch location is
now denoted with ˆp, the minimization is solved by a Gauss-Newton method, iteratively
linearizing the optimization problem around ˆp
b(ˆp + δp, P, I, D) = A(ˆp, I, D)δp + b(ˆp, P, I, D),
(7.21)
where b(ˆp + δp, P, I, D) is the stacked errors from Equation (7.20) and A(ˆp, I, D) is the
corresponding Jacobian. The normal equations are then
A(ˆp, I, D)TA(ˆp, I, D)δp = −A(ˆp, I, D)Tb(ˆp, P, I, D),
(7.22)
which is then solved for the correction δp, leading to the optimal alignment of the patches.
