7.1.4
Detection and Selection of Landmarks
Detection
The detection of new landmarks is based on the ”Features from Accelerated
Segment Test” (FAST) algorithm [83], which is a corner detection algorithm. The following
account of the FAST algorithm is taken from the OpenCV documentation[195]. The FAST
algorithm was presented as a less computationally expensive alternative to existing feature
detectors such as SIFT, Harris, and SUSAN detection algorithms[195]. The motivation
behind this was to develop a feature detector that could function in real-time use cases
such as SLAM[83].
The algorithm starts by selecting a pixel candidate p in the image. The next step is then
to determine whether or not the candidate is a valid interest point. Denoting the intensity
of the pixel value as Ip and defining thresholds t and n, a circle of 16 pixels is considered
around the selected pixel candidate. This process is depicted in Figure 7.5. The pixel
candidate p is then classified as a corner if there is a set of n contiguous pixels among the
16, with an intensity of either Ip + t or Ip −t. This guarantees that there is a semicircle of
either brighter or darker pixels around the candidate, indicating a corner. In the original
implementation, n was chosen to be 12, as this allows for a high-speed test, evaluating only
four pixels instead of all 12. This test evaluates only the pixels in the circle at position
1, 5, 9 and 13, and if p is a corner, it is required that three of these four evaluated pixels
must be either brighter or darker, using the threshold as before. Using this high-speed
test means that many corner candidates can be quickly discarded, and then the full test
criterion evaluating all n pixels can be performed on the remaining candidates.
Figure 7.5: Demonstrating the FAST corner detector, with a circle of 16 pixels around the
selected pixel candidate. The candidate is classified as a corner if there are n contiguous
pixels among the 16, with an intensity outside the limits given by threshold Ip ± t. Ful-
filling this criterion gives a semicircle of pixel values either all brighter or darker than the
candidate, indicating a corner.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
159
==PAGE:188==
This method is extremely eﬀicient but has four notable flaws. First, it does not generalize
well in cases where n < 12, as less candidates are rejected before the full test criterion.
Second, the selection of the four test pixels implies an assumption of the local texture
around the feature. Third, as the full test criterion is applied after the high-speed test,
the results of the high-speed test are not utilized. Finally, the algorithm has a tendency
to detect multiple features adjacent to each other.
In [83], the first three problems are addressed using a machine-learning approach, while
the fourth is handled using non-maximal suppression. As this is not directly relevant to
the rest of the method section, these implementations will not be discussed here but can
be found in the original paper and on the OpenCV website.
Selection
Having obtained an abundance of landmark candidates from the FAST algo-
rithm, ROVIO first disregards all those close to already tracked landmarks. In addition
to this, bucketing is used across the image to ensure good distribution of proposed land-
marks. Then, a patch gradient-based score is calculated to select new landmarks to add
to the state for tracking. The Hessian from this score can be obtained from the normal
equations for the patch alignment algorithm, presented in Equation (7.22)
H = A(ˆp, I, D)⊤A(ˆp, I, D).
(7.27)
For this Hessian, the minimal eigenvalue is analogous to the adapted Shi-Tomasi score
and has the advantage that a high score indicates the alignment accuracy of the multilevel
patch feature. Furthermore, using a Hessian-based scoring method allows for the obtaining
of other eigenvalue-based scores, such as the 1 and 2 norms, which allows for the detection
of edge-shaped features.
Management of Actively Tracked Landmarks
In the algorithm, to ensure good performance, only a limited number of landmarks can be
tracked as part of the state. Knowing how the landmarks are selected using the eigenvalue-
based scoring method on the Hessian in Section 7.1.4, the following framework is presented
for managing the landmarks within the state.
The algorithm maintains tracking and
visibility information for each given landmark, computing a combined heuristic quality
score, which is comprised of three sub-scores:
Global Quality: This score represents how often a landmark has been tracked since the
initialization of the algorithm.
Local Quality: This score represents how often a landmark has been tracked, when it is
expected that the given landmark is in the FOV of the cameras. This score is limited
to recent frames, emphasizing the local nature of this score.
Local Visibility: This score simply represents how often the landmark was in the FOV.
This is also limited to only recent frames.
Using this combined score, the tracked landmarks for the state are managed using adaptive
thresholding, enabling the control of the number of tracked landmarks currently in the
frame. If, for instance, the maximal number of landmarks allowed in the filter state and
only a subset of these landmarks are tracked properly, the landmark pruning can be made
stricter to accommodate the detection of new and improved landmarks.
Furthermore,
if a landmark has a high global quality, meaning that it has been tracked often since
the algorithm’s initialization, the pruning of the threshold for the local quality and local
visibility is relaxed.
160
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:189==
7.1.5
The ROVIO Algorithm
Figure 7.6:
The IMU-fixed frame
I in relation to the inertial world
frame W, at the time of initializa-
tion for ROVIO.
The overall ROVIO VIO algorithm can now be pre-
sented.
The framework of ROVIO is that of the
IEKF covered in Section 7.1.1, where inertial mea-
surements from the IMU are used in the prediction
step of the filter, thereby propagating the state, and
visual measurements are integrated during the filter
update step, correcting the state. As described in
the literature review and beginning of this chapter,
the filter in ROVIO is formulated in a robo-centric
fashion such that the tracking of landmarks is per-
formed in relation to the moving sensor frame. Fur-
thermore, this reduces issues related to nonlinearity
and observability and allows for undelayed initial-
ization as all landmarks can be parameterized at
initialization with a bearing vector and a distance
parameter, with low and high initial covariance re-
spectively.
Keeping in mind the three coordinate frames defined in Section 3.4.3, recall that the
inertial world coordinate frame is defined with W, the IMU fixed coordinate frame with
I and the camera frame with C. Figure 7.6, illustrates the way that the IMU-fixed frame
is initialized in relation to the inertial world frame, by ROVIO.
Using these frames, qWI represents a mapping from the IMU fixed coordinate frame to
the inertial world coordinate frame, which is fundamentally a rotation between the two
coordinate frames. Furthermore, IrWI represents the vector with the origin of the inertial
world frame to the origin of the IMU-fixed frame, expressed in the IMU-fixed frame.
The state of the filter is defined as:
x :=
 r, v, q, bf, bω, c, z, µ0, . . . , µN, ρ0, . . . , ρN

,
(7.28)
where
r :=I rWI The robo-centric position of the IMU-fixed frame.
v :=I vI The robo-centric velocity of the IMU-fixed frame.
q := qWI The attitude of the IMU, expressed as rotation from I to W.
bf Additive bias on accelerometer.
bω Additive bias on the gyroscope.
c :=I cIC Linear part of IMU-camera extrinsics.
z := zCI Rotational part of IMU-camera extrinsics.

In ROVIO, the inverse distance parameterization d(ρi) = 1
ρi is used.

Figure 7.7: The computational loop for the ROVIO algorithm, based on the IEKF. The
prediction step, the per landmark update step, and the landmark management step are
all indicated by their designated colors. Each block of the algorithm has a given number,
marked with red, showing the order in which it is executed in the loop. Note that due to
the iterative nature of the IEKF, the internal loop in the per landmark update step runs
multiple times. Figure inspired by [60].
To propagate the state, the acceleration measurement and the rotational rate measure-
ments are required. The acceleration measurement, ˜f = I˜fI, is the acceleration of the
IMU-fixed frame I, expressed in the same frame, where the rotational rate measurement,
˜ω = I ˜ωWI, is the rotational rate of the IMU-fixed frame in relation to the inertial world
frame W expressed in the IMU-fixed frame.
These values are sampled for every timestep k and fed into the IMU-based prediction
step of the algorithm, together with the a-posteriori estimate of the state vector x+
k−1 and
covariance matrix P+
k−1. This is shown in figure Figure 7.7, which shows the full algorithm
workflow for ROVIO. The figure is divided into three distinct parts; the prediction step
(yellow), the ”per landmark” update step (blue) and finally an intermediate landmark
management step (grep) before the next iteration of the filter is run. The red labels on
162
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:191==
each of the blocks represent the order of the operations.
Kalman Prediction Takes the acceleration ˜f and rotational rate ˜ω as input, as well as the
a-posteriori estimations of state x+ and covariance matrix P+ from the prior time
step. Using the inertial motion model, the state of the filter is propagated to the
current time step, obtaining the a-priori state x−and covariance matrix P−for the
timestep.
Extract Patch, Compute Linear Warping Matrix Takes the multilevel patch Pi for land-
mark i from the previous timestep, in addition to x+, also from the previous timestep
as well as x−for the current timestep. These states contain the bearing vector µ for
the landmark, used to calculate the linear warping matrix Di, as in Equation (7.19).
Camera Model Is responsible for returning the predicted location, pi of patch Pi, given the
bearing vector µ contained in the state. If this is the first iteration of the update step
of the IEKF, it simply uses the a-priori estimation x−from the Kalman Prediction.
Extract Warped Patch and Compute Photometric Error Takes as input Di and Pi. Then,
using the camera model π, the predicted location of Pi in the frame is obtained, and
the stacked photometric bi error is calculated, as in Equation (7.21).
QR-decomposition and Calculating Innovation Takes the error term bi and the corre-
sponding Jacobian Ai for the patch, calculates the innovation yi by means of a
QR-decomposition, as shown in Equation (7.24) and Equation (7.25).
Kalman Update, Outlier Detection Takes the innovation a-priori estimates of state and
covariance matrix, x−and, P−, the innovation for the given photometric patch yi
and the Hi Hessian. It then performs one iteration of the update as described Equa-
tion (7.10) and later Equation (7.17). Outlier detection is performed to account for
moving objects. The detection method is based on Mahalanobis distance, compar-
ing the obtained innovation residual with the predicted innovation covariance. If
the weighted norm between these exceeds a certain threshold, the measurement is
rejected[60].
Quality Score Computation, Prune and Add Landmarks As the iterative update steop of
the IEKF has completed, the a-posteriori state and covariance matrix for the current
timestep is passed. Within the estimated state, the tracked landmarks are pruned,
and new landmarks detected with the Fast Feature detection algorithm can be added
to the state following the quality score outlined in Section 7.1.4. The next step of
the IEKF can then be commenced.
In this manner, tracked landmarks are updated one after another, undergoing a certain
number of iterations in the update step of the IEKF, with the update step taking care of
aligning the patches using the linear warping matrix, which is itself tracked and updated
by the filter. As such, the entirety of the landmark tracking functionality is contained in
the update step of the filter.
7.1.6
Multi Camera Implementation
For the multi-camera setup used with this implementation, the strength is that it does
not require the same amount of excitation to make the distance parameter for a tracked
landmark converge, as epipolar geometry can be used to infer distance if the landmark is
seen in both of the image frames. For a tracked landmark, if the camera that observes
the landmark, Cm is not the same as the camera in which the landmark is tracked Cd, the
bearing vector must be transformed to the frame of the measurement camera:
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
163
==PAGE:192==
Cmµi = zm(cd + z−1
d (Cdµid(ρi)) −cm),
(7.29)
where c and z represent the linear and rotational extrinsics of the IMU to the cameras, as
described in the above filter state. Finally (C⌈µi, ρi) represents the parameterization of a
landmark location. The difference between a monocular setup and a multi-camera setup
is now given by the fact that Equation (7.26), representing the Jacobian for the innovation
term under the QR decomposition scheme, must be right multiplied by Jacobian of 7.29.
7.1.7
ROS Implementation
The implementation of ROVIO used for the project is the same as in [76], taken from their
ReAqROVIO GitHub repository. However, as the UScanner has domed lenses over the
cameras, with the camera placed in the center, refractive calibration is not required for
this case[196], and is therefore disabled for the project.
The algorithm is run as a node in ROS 1 Noetic, that subscribes to a set of predefined
camera topics and an IMU topic, as defined in the launch.xml file. As soon as visual
and inertial data is available, the filter will start, showing the tracking of landmarks
in real-time for each of the camera frames.
The predicted trajectory is published to
the /rovio/odometry topic, with message type nav_msgs/Odometry, which is saved to a
ROS-bag.
While bias parameters for the IMU given to the algorithm were initially based on those
obtained from the Allan Variance ROS tool, these were later hand tuned in collaboration
with Mohit Singh, co-author of the ReAqROVIO paper[75][76]. The final parameter sheet
with which the algorithm has been run can be found in Appendix H.2.
164
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:193==
7.2
Pre-Processing
To make the recorded ROS-bags compatible with the ROVIO algorithm, a few steps were
taken to process the timestamps and sequence numbers of the data. The full pipeline of the
data processing is seen below in Figure 7.8. Each of the steps in the pipeline corresponds
to a full pass of the ROS-bag.

 - (Improve grayscale images)

Figure 7.8: The pre-processing pipeline of a ROS-bag to ensure compatibility with ROVIO.
Extract and Reframe, Convert Image and Pressure
As the MAVROS interface and the UScanner both publish a large number of ROS-topics,
which were all recorded at the time of deployment, the ROS-bags are large in terms of
file size. To make the recordings easier to work with and to save space when multiple
recordings for one deployment have to be appended, only the topics required for the
ROVIO algorithm and for data processing are extracted.
These topics have already been presented in Table 4.10, of Section 4.2.3, but are quickly
recalled here: For the UScanner, both left and right camera topics, the topic for the 6-IMU
and the topic for the Bar30 barometer is extracted. For the data collected in Sydhavnen,
the topic from the magnetometer of the 9-axis IMU is extracted as well.
These topics are then given new frame IDs.
Both of the image topics are given the
frames ”cam0_sensor_frame” and ”cam1_sensor_frame” for the left and right image
topics, respectively. The IMU data is given ”imu_sensor_frame,” while the two remaining
topics are both given the frame ”base_link”.
Furthermore, both of the image topics are converted to grayscale with the ”mono8” encod-
ing to reduce filesize. This does not impact the performance of the ROVIO algorithm, as it
inherently converts color images to grayscale automatically. In addition to this, if desired,
the image data can be enhanced, which is relevant for the data collected in Sydhavnen.
This image enhancement pipeline will be presented in Section 7.2.1.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
165
==PAGE:194==
Finally the pressure readings contained in the /bar30/pressure are converted from
milibar (mBar) to pascal (Pa), and assigned the ROS message type ”sensor_msgs/FluidPressure”,
as this is required by ROVIO. The unit of mBar is assumed from the magnitude of the
value, as there is no oﬀicial documentation on the sensor from UVision. To convert form
mBar to Pa, the value is multiplied by 100.
Adjusting Sequence Numbers
In the ROS-bags, the captured stereo image pair might have slightly different ”seq” values
in the header of the image message. This is possibly due to the boot process of the UScan-
ner, with the ROS-node for one camera starting slightly before the other, nevertheless, it
must be accounted for as the next step that adjusts the timestamps for the stereo images
is based on a matching stereo image pair having matching sequence numbers.
Therefore, to solve this issue, the ROS-bag is inspected manually, and the offset in the
”seq” parameter between the images is determined. A script then loops over the image
topics and either subtracts or adds the offset to the ”seq” value in the header for the left
image message.
Timing the Stereo Images
The ROVIO algorithm allows for a maximum timewise displacement between a stereo
image pair, which is routinely exceeded by a stereo image pair from the UScanner due to the
relatively low recording frequency. This issue was addressed by loading in a stereo image
pair from the ROS-bag, identified as a pair by the ”seq” value in the header of the message,
and averaging their header timestamps, thereby giving them identical timestamps. The
calculation was done in nanoseconds to ensure that the timestep for an image pair was
not rounded to the nearest second.
In addition to fixing the header time for the stereo image pair, the ROS-time was likewise
averaged and overwritten for both messages.
Appending Data (Sydhavn Only)
For the large-scale data acquisition campaigns in Sydhavnen, as described, the data was
recorded in one-minute intervals. To run the data with the ROVIO algorithm that requires
either real-time publishing to the required ROS-topics or the playback of one ROS-bag, the
individually processed ROS-bags were appended into one, spanning the entire deployment.
7.2.1
Image Enhancement Techniques
As noted in the discussion for the primary data acquisition campaign, Section 4.2.4, the
underwater stereo images captured by the UScanner are quite dark and noisy, both in
color and when converted to grayscale. This problem is shown in Figure 7.9.
(a) Colored image.
(b) Grayscal image.
Figure 7.9: Image example from the primary data acquisition data campaign.
166
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:195==
This loss in image quality leads to the landmarks being further challenging to identify and
track as the noise obscures structures in the image. A few approaches were implemented
to suppress noise and enhance possible structures and texture in the images to increase
robustness in terms of landmark detection and tracking.
Custom Image Processing Pipeline
A simple image enhancement pipeline was implemented, which consists of first brightening
the image and normalizing it, then performing Gaussian smoothening, and finally increas-
ing the contrast of the image by means of a sharpening kernel. The results for each step
of this process are shown in Figure 7.10.
Brightening:
Adds a scalar of 20 to each pixel in the image, increasing its brightness.
Normalizing:
Normalize the grayscale image using minmax normalization to have pixel
values between 0 and 255, where 0 is black and 255 is white. Forcing the image to use the
full spectrum of values improves the contrast. For an image frame I the normalizatization
processes is given by
Inormalized = (I −min(I)) · 255
max(I) −min(I) .
(7.30)
Gaussian Smoothening:
A two step process to remove noise from the image.
First a
Gaussian blur with a 5×5 kernel, ks = 5, is applied with the cv2.GaussianBlur() func-
tion. This reduces sharp variations, and removes Gaussian noise. The standard deviation
of the kernel is calculated directly from the size
σ = 0.3 · ((ks −1) · 0.5 −1) + 0.8 = 1.1 .
(7.31)
Using the Gaussian function defined as Gσ =

Retinex
Another attempt to enhance the images obtained from Sydhavnen was made using the
Single Scale Retinex, (SSR), framework[197]. The used code can be found in Appendix H.1.
The details of this framework will not be covered in the report, however, the basic principle
is that each pixel of the processed image is defined as the difference between the original
pixel value, and its center-surround average, using the Gaussian function as the center-
surround function. This has been shown to improve texture in low-light settings[198].
The results of applying SSR to the original image, seen in Figure 7.10 a), with a chosen
variance parameter of 500 is shown below in Figure 7.11. This parameter was found by
manually tweaking the value.
168
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:197==
a) Original Image
b) SSR Image
Figure 7.11: The application of single-scale-retinex on the same image from the Sydhavnen
dataset. With this method, an excessive amount of noise is introduced, with the above
being the best result obtained by manually tweaking the variance parameter.
While this certainly improves the texture of the image, highlighting the poles on the
left side of the frame introduces an extreme amount of additional noise in the image.
Furthermore, the execution of the SSR for a single image frame takes approximately 9.67
seconds (averaged over 1000 executions of SSR) on an Apple Silicon M1 3.2 GHz arm
processor, making it unfeasible in this setting, as the ROS-bag containing the data from
Sydhavn contains 2 × 8400 = 16800 images, meaning that processing this bag would take
more than 45 hours.
7.3
Post-Processing
To compare the estimated trajectories from the ROVIO algorithm with the baseline tra-
jectories obtained from the OptiTrack system for the experiment in ASTA and the USBL
system from the primary data acquisition campaign in Sydhavnen, preprocessing of the
trajectories was necessary. In the remainder of this chapter, the trajectory estimated by
the ROVIO algorithm will be referred to as the estimated trajectory. In contrast, the
trajectory captured by the OptiTrack motion capture system will be referred to as the
baseline trajectory, or simply just baseline.
As described in Section 7.1.7, the recorded ROS-topic for a collection of the estimated tra-
jectory is the ”/rovio/odometry”, which contains the estimated position and orientation
of the ROV/UScanner, as well as the 6 × 6 covariance matrix (x, y, z, roll, pitch, yaw)
7.3.1
ASTA Experiment
To demonstrate the post-processing procedures of the ASTA-Experiment, the estimated
trajectory is taken from a deployment of the algorithm on the data, specifically the cap-
tured ROS-bag containing the VIO-oriented trajectory.
Timewise Alignment of ROVIO and OptiTrack Trajectories.
The first step of post-processing is ensuring that the trajectory obtained from ROVIO
and the baseline measurements from the OptiTrack system are aligned in time. This step
is necessary as the time on the OptiTrack computer and the UScanner is not synchro-
nized. Indeed, when obtaining data from the OptiTrack system, the system had to be
synchronized manually with local time using a phone as a reference.
As the recorded ROS-bag containing the recorded data starts while the UScanner is sta-
tionary at the edge of the pool and is then moved directly upwards before being placed in
the pool, the displacement in time between the two systems can be found by inspecting the
raw IMU measurements from the UScanner, with the calculated velocity of the rigid body
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
169
==PAGE:198==
overlaid on top. These velocities have been calculated from the OptiTrack positioning
data by taking the derivate of the position over time:
vk = |xk −xk−1|

where k is the current timestep, and t is the time associated with the step. The IMU
measurements and calculated velocities are shown in Figure 7.12, along with the estimated
time displacement. This displacement has been estimated by visual inspection.

the parts where it sits stationary at the edge of the pool, the relevant part lies in the
sub-section of the trajectory that features movement in the pool, below the waterline.

Aligning Reference Frames
The final step of post-processing involves aligning the inertial world frame (W) of the
ROVIO algorithm with that of the OptiTrack system (O), and as a result, have one
collective frame in which to compare the trajectories, both starting in origo.
As the
trajectory estimated by ROVIO has been cut, and therefore no longer starts in origo, the
first position for the baseline and ROVIO is subtracted from each position in the respective
trajectory, ensuring that both start in origo. Having ensured that O and W has origo in
the same position, the axes of the coordinate frames must be aligned.
The orientation of the IMU-fixed frame (I) in relation to W shown in Figure 7.6. The
UScanner is placed in such a way at the beginning of the recording that the W roughly
aligns with O, however, fine-tuning should be carried out.
Relative

Figure 7.15: The process behind aligning the inertial world coordinate frame W initialized
by ROVIO, with the OptiTrack coordinate frame O.
The fine-tuning in orientation is done by determining the relative rotation between the
baseline orientation and the estimated orientation at the initial position. For ROVIO,
estimated orientation is that of I in W. As the baseline orientation describes the orien-
tation of the UScanner (U) relative to O, the relative rotation between W and O can be
found by determining the relative rotation between I and U, when U is rotated 90 degrees
around the x-axis of O, to accommodate for the fact that I has an initial orientation of
90 degrees around the x-axis in W. This process is illustrated in Figure 7.15
Having determined this relative rotation (in practice done using the rotation object rep-
resentation of quaternions in SciPy), it is applied to all points and orientations of the
estimated trajectory by ROVIO. With the reference frames aligned, errors can now be cal-
172
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:201==
culated between the baseline trajectory and the estimated trajectory. For the results, the
Euclidean error between a predicted position in the trajectory and the time-wise closest
position of the baseline trajectory is used as the error metric.
7.3.2
Sydhavnen
The trajectory obtained from applying ROVIO to the data collected in Sydhavnen is
not cut, as the VIO algorithm is both initialized and stopped when the ROV is in the
water. However, the estimated trajectory still has to be aligned with that of the USBL
system. For this, the baseline data obtained from the USBL is first transformed to be in an
ENU coordinate system. The objective then becomes that of transforming the estimated
trajectory from ROVIO into the same coordinate system. For this, the magnetometer of
the ROV is used. The heading of the ROV in the global ENU coordinate system based
on the magnetometer can be calculated from the MagneticField ROS message as:
hMAG = atan2(mx, my) −π
2 ,
(7.35)
where mx and my are the respective x and y components of the magnetic field vector, given
in Tesla.
π
2 is subtracted to account for the fact that the magnetic field vector is given
in a local ENU for the ROV, with north pointing forwards and east pointing right, while
the IMU-fixed frame of the ROV is a forward, left, up coordinate system. The heading is
calculated such that if the ROV is pointing due north, the heading is 0, while if pointing
due east, the heading is −π
2 , keeping the convention that positive mathematical rotation
is in the counter-clockwise direction.
This obtained heading is then compared with the predicted orientation of the Uscanner
attached to the ROV. Recall from Figure 7.6, that in the IMU-fixed frame of the UScanner,
the z-axis points straight backwards, along the y-direction of W. Therefore, the predicted
heading can be obtained as
hV IO = atan2(zx, zy) −π
2 ,
(7.36)
where zx and zy are the x and y components of the z axis of the IMU-fixed frame I,
projected on the the xy-plane of the W. As W is rotated 90◦counterclockwise in relation to
the global ENU coordinate system used for the calculation of heading by the magnetometer
above, and for the fact that the z-axis points in the opposite direction of the heading, π
2
is subtracted. The difference in heading is then calculated
∆h = hMAG −hV IO.
(7.37)
This relative difference in orientation is then used to correct both the estimated trajectories
and orientations, as was done for the data from ASTA, except instead of using the mean
over orientations, the first predicted orientation and the corresponding magnetometer
measurement is simply used.
The final step is then that of ensuring the estimated trajectory starts in a known position
of the USBL baseline. Once more, repeating the process from above, the timewise closest
position of the baseline is found, and the coordinate systems of both the baseline and
estimated trajectory are zeroed around this point.

==PAGE:202==
7.4
Results
The following section of the report presents the results obtained from evaluating the
ROVIO VIO algorithm in the controlled environment that is the pool in ASTA at DTU,
and finally on the data obtained from the primary data acquisition campaign. The re-
sults obtained in ASTA are all based on the experiment specifically designed for VIO
development and evaluation. Using the data obtained from the pool in ASTA, it will be
determined whether ROVIO should be used in a monocular or stereo configuration, as
well as the best set of parameters for the algorithm. The parameters considered are the
patch size (in pixels) and maximum number of tracked landmarks in each image frame.
The best configuration of the algorithm found through the parameter search will be used
with the raw data from Sydhavnen, as well as data enhanced by the image processing
pipeline described in Section 7.2.1.
In the following description, a ”run” will be used to describe a deployment of the ROVIO
algorithm with a given configuration and a given set of parameters. For all results obtained
in ASTA, three runs with the algorithm were captured. For each of the three runs, binned
errors over traveled distance are calculated, as well as the complete set of errors over the
entire run from start to finish. These bins over traveled distance are for the distances of
0m - 1m, 1m - 5m, 5m -10m, 10m - 20m and 20m-end (29.95 m) respectively. The cutoff
points between the bins can be seen in Figure 7.16

for the analysis presented below.

and tighter around the median. This is furthermore supported by inspecting the histogram
values depicting the mean of the errors and the associated 95% confidence intervals, that
in all other cases than for the traveled distance of 1 meter, do not overlap. The only
exception is the errors in the interval of 20 meters to the end of the trajectory, where
the monocular setup outperforms that of the stereo. Finally, inspecting the rightmost
histogram values containing the mean over the entire set of errors, the errors from the
stereo setup are significantly lower than those of the monocular one.

x-axis and z-axis, the stereo configuration is quite a bit better at estimating the position
for the y-axis. The corresponding trajectories shown in the xy-plane and 3D can be found
in Appendix H.3.

7.4.2
ASTA: Parameter Exploration
Having determined that the stereo configuration performs significantly better than the
monocular, using a maximum of 25 tracked landmarks per frame and a patch size of 6×6
pixels, it is then desired to find the optimal set of corresponding parameters to use with the
data in the controlled environment of the ASTA. In particular, for the maximum number
of tracked landmarks, the investigated parameter values are 10, 15, 20, 25, and 30. For
the patch size of the multilevel photometric patches, the investigated sizes are 2, 4, 6, 8,
and 10 pixels in both width and height. All combinations of these values are examined,
running ROVIO on the recorded data for a total of 75 runs, and the results are presented
in Figure 7.20.

Figure 7.21: Heatmap over the mean and standard deviation of the errors over the full
trajectory for each possible combination of the parameters. A red border outlines the
best-performing set of parameters. The values are calculated based on the errors for the
best-performing run for each possible combination.
These heatmaps represent the mean error and standard deviation for all errors over the
entire trajectory. It can be seen in Figure 7.20a, that the best configuration of parame-
ters for ROVIO is that of a patch size of 8×8 pixels, with a maximum of 25 landmarks
178
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:207==
tracked per camera frame. The associated standard deviation for the errors is shown in
Figure 7.20b, where it can be seen that in addition to the lowest overall mean error, it
has the lowest standard deviation of the errors (tied with patch size 4×4 pixels and max-
imum 10 tracked landmarks per camera frame). Observing Figure 7.20, there is a vague
trend that using a combination of more landmarks and a larger patch size generally leads
to lower error until a certain point around which the error seems to resume increasing.
The combination of path size 10×10 pixels and a maximum of 25 tracked landmarks per
image frame could be considered an outlier. The full trajectory for all three runs of this
suspected outlier configuration can be found in Appendix H.4.
The same analysis has been carried out when selecting the best of the three runs for
each combination. The results hereof can be seen in Figure 7.21. For this analysis, the
trend disappears somewhat as the mean error values for the runs are closer. However,
from observing the marginal histograms in both Figure 7.21a and Figure 7.21b, both the
selection of the possible maximum number of tracked landmarks and size of the patch seems
to make a small difference in performance, with a patch size of 8 showing consistently good
performance compared to other sizes.

for the maximum number of tracked landmarks are examined. This is done vice versa

significantly different errors.

patch size matters for overall performance, repeating the trend that generally higher values
of both parameters increase performance to a point. Indeed, it seems that by allowing for
tracking more than 25 landmarks and a patch size larger than 8×8 pixels, performance
once again begins to deteriorate. In figure Figure 7.22, this is notable for both parameters.

7.4.4
ASTA: Experiments
Decreasing IMU and Camera Rate
To examine the limitations of the ROVIO algorithm, two experiments were carried out.
The data used for these experiments is the same as for the analyses above. The experi-
ments are designed to test the effects on the performance of the algorithm when the update
rates for the IMU and the cameras are decreased drastically. The first experiment entails
decreasing the rate of the IMU with a factor of 10, from 400 Hz to 40 Hz, while the second
experiment additionally decreases the rate of the cameras with a factor of two, from 10
Hz to 5 Hz. The results of these experiments can be seen in Figure 7.28 where the errors
from each experiment are compared with the non-altered update rates. In Figure 7.28a)
the boxplots comparing binned errors over traveled distance are shown, showing no monu-
mental difference between the distribution of errors for the original rates compared to that
of the reduced IMU rate, except for the final bin of errors. When both the IMU and cam-
era rates are reduced, the errors grow considerably. This is supported by Figure 7.28b),
showing the histograms over the mean errors with corresponding 95% confidence intervals.
The trajectories for the two experiments can be seen in Appendix H.6 and Appendix H.7,
for the downsampled IMU and downsampled IMU+Camera experiment, respectively.

184
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:213==
Introducing a Barometer
As it was observed in Figure 7.25 that the estimated position on the z-axis was a consistent
source of error, it was theorized that the addition of a barometer could help diminish this
error. As the UScanner comes equipped with a BlueRobotics Bar30 barometer and the
implementation of ROVIO in [76], which is used in this project. is compatible with a data
stream from a barometer, this experiment was performed. However, the Bar30 barometer
of the UScanner outputs data to a standard Float32 message type in the assumed unit of
milibar (mBar), while the sensor_msgs/FluidPressure of which ROVIO was compatible,
requires the readings to be in pascal (Pa). The assumption regarding the unit of pressure
from the barometer of the UScanner is based on magnitude of the readings, but ultimately
not known with certainty, as the UScanner is a closed system. However still, the values in
the ROS-bag were attempted converted to the correct message type and unit.
When deploying the algorithm with the added barometer input from the UScanner, ROVIO
fails immediately as it is placed underwater, causing the uncertainty of the estimated po-
sition for the tracked landmarks in the update phase of the filter to grow rapidly. As there
is no available documentation for the barometer on the UScanner it is not known exactly
whether the conversion from from the assumed unit of mBar to Pa is incorrect, or whether
the barometer is incorrectly calibrated.

7.4.6
ASTA: Convergence of IMU Biases
ROVIO tracks the IMU biases on both the gyroscope and accelerometer as part of the
state. According to the authors of ROVIO, these values must converge (with the shown
convergence time in [61] being <5 seconds). For a run with optimal parameters, i.e., a
maximum number of tracked landmarks of 25 and a patch size of 8×8 pixels, these biases
are shown in Figure 7.31 and Figure 7.32, for the gyroscope and accelerometer respectively.

Figure 7.34: The estimated trajectory for the data in Sydhavnen (blue), plotted against

Though many candidates are found using the FAST corner detector, these candidates are
predominantly extracted from the noisy side of the frame, looking into the ”open waters”
of the canal. In contrast, the structures in the left side of the image frame are ignored as
they do not stand out suﬀiciently in the frame. This can be seen in figure Figure 7.33.
Though the landmarks extracted from the noise are only tracked for a few consecutive
image frames, they drift very quickly in random directions, as the update step of the IEKF
essentially attempts to predict noise. When ROVIO does manage to identify a landmark
associated with a structure, the positions of the multilevel patch feature descriptors drift
vertically, as these patches are mainly associated with line features, with no valid corner
features tracked in the image as anchor points, throwing off the estimation of position.
The filter resets multiple times as the associated covariance for the distance parameter
188
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:217==
increases in an attempt to extract a completely new set of landmarks, however, with
the same outcome every time, as the poor image measurements throw off the tracking.
Nevertheless, the estimated trajectory is obtained and compared with the USBL baseline
data in Figure 7.34.

Data Enhancement
Section 7.2.1 described the approaches utilized to improve the image data obtained. Pro-
cessing all images in the entire ROS-bag, ROVIO is deployed once more to investigate
whether any improvements can be observed.
Full Pipeline
Observing the filter on the enhanced data shows the same tendencies as the
untreated data. While the structures in the image have been enhanced, the noise across
the frame has increased accordingly.
Figure 7.37: Red markings are FAST feature candi-
dates detected in the noise, looking out into the ”open
water” of the canal.
Figure 7.37 shows a frame from
the run, where a large amount
of possible feature candidates are
found by the FAST feature detec-
tor in the noisy part of the im-
age frame, but with no tracked
landmarks. The obtained trajec-
tory from the entire recording,
can be found in Appendix H.10,
and shows no noticeable improve-
ment over the untreated data.
Omitting Sharpening
In an at-
tempt to reduce the amount of

7.5
Discussion
In this chapter, the results of implementing and evaluating the visual-inertial odometry
algorithm ROVIO in an underwater domain have been presented. In the controlled envi-
ronment of the pool at ASTA, the algorithm has been shown to estimate trajectories with
varying degrees of accuracy depending on the setup and chosen configuration.
The initial test compared a monocular camera setup with a stereo, with the stereo per-
forming better, which is not surprising given the overlapping fields of view of multiple
cameras allowing for more accurately assessing depth. This is followed by a comprehen-
sive search over a range of values for two selected parameters: The maximum number of
tracked landmarks in each camera frame and the size of the patch feature. With this, an
attempt has been made to emulate the parameter exploration by the original authors in
[60]. Inspecting the heatmaps depicting the outcome, it is shown that tracking a large
number of landmarks with large patch sizes generally leads to better performance up until
a certain threshold, beyond which errors begin to rise. However, this trend is not strongly
pronounced: Considering the boxplots of errors binned over the traveled distance, the
distributions of errors are relatively similar, with only the lowest value for the number of
192
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:221==
tracked landmarks showing poor performance after 20 meters of traveled distance. Ana-
lyzing the bar plots shows that the resulting mean error is significantly different between
configurations, favoring setups tracking a moderately large number of landmarks (20-25).
However, this trend is not absolute. Interestingly, a patch size of 8 performed consistently
well in the bar plots, also showing generally lower distributions of error in the box plots.
These findings approximately mirror those of the original authors, who claimed that ”In
visual-inertial systems, the IMU provides a good prior on the motion of the systems and
merely needs to be stabilized using recurrent stationary landmark observations.”[60]. How-
ever, while the original authors also found that the algorithm performed well with small
patch sizes, it is suspected that the more uniform visual texture in the pool necessitates a
larger patch size for reliable performance.
Some interesting tendencies seen when inspecting the obtained trajectories across all con-
figurations are also considered. Generally, ROVIO seems to underestimate the traveled
distance when moving along the side of the pool from the start position, as seen in both
Figure 7.24 and Figure 7.29; however, this is corrected on the return part of the trajectory
after the 180◦degree turn in the far corner of the pool. A possible explanation for this
lies in the way the algorithm initializes landmark parameterization. To allow for unde-
layed initialization, each landmark is parameterized with a bearing vector and a distance
parameter, initialized with low and high covariance, respectively.
To ensure accurate
tracking, it is vital that this distance parameter converges, which usually happens quickly
as the camera moves, especially in the case of the stereo camera setup, as explained in
Section 7.1.6. However, some excitation of the camera system is still required[60]. In the
pool, the camera looks directly at the far end of the wall, with the ladder and pipe in the
middle of the pool as the only visual features. As the camera is moved directly past the
pipe, only the ladder is in view. It is theorized that due to the straight motion directly
towards the ladder, the distance parameter is not converging properly, thereby leading to
an underestimation of the traveled distance. This can be further backed up by the fact
that the return part of the trajectory is more accurate in this regard since the camera is
shown all possible visual features in the entire pool during the 180◦turn, possibly allowing
the distance parameters to converge before commencing movement.
While the algorithm generally seems to do well during turns, the final turn on the return
part of the trajectory near the ladder does, on rare occasions, pose a problem, as depicted
in Figure 7.30. The authors claim that it is vital to the function of the filter that the online
calibration of the IMU biases converge, which in [60] happens in a matter of seconds, but
as shown in Figure 7.31 and Figure 7.32, is less stable in this case. This might indicate
that there is still room for improvement in the calibration of the visual-inertial system of
the UScanner, which was also noted in the discussion section of Chapter 4. Interestingly,
the turn devoid of visual features presented in Figure 4.34 has not been observed to pose
a problem.
The sub-optimal calibrations and the suspected lack of convergence for the distance pa-
rameter for landmarks the illustrated lack of convergence for the IMU-biases, might also
explain why the algorithm has a tendency to drift an extreme amount when initialized, as
seen in Figure 7.13, which is rectified only after the filter resets itself.
Based on the observations from running ROVIO on the data obtained at ASTA, a potential
improvement could be adding loop-closure and consequent smoothing of the trajectory
into the algorithm, effectively transforming it into a SLAM alorithm. Additionally, as the
estimated position on the z-axis is a consistent source of error, as seen in Figure 7.25,
correctly integrating the barometer of the UScanner barometer in the algorithm could be
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
193
==PAGE:222==
a valuable improvement. However, based on the experiments downsampling the IMU and
camera, there is evidence to suggest that the IMU could be downsampled to 40Hz, with
no effect on the performance.
Running ROVIO on the data obtained in Sydhavnen from the primary data acquisition
campaign has proven largely infeasible due to the low light conditions of the recorded data,
with the exception of the segment of the data highlighted in the results.
Several unsuccessful attempts have been made to improve the visual data, enhancing the
clarity of the structures and surface of the wall featured in the recordings.
However,
while increasing brightness and contrast to the image does enhance feature visibility, it
introduces additional excessive amounts of noise in the parts of the image looking into
the ”open waters” of the canal, disrupting the FAST-feature detector. Recalling the for-
mulation of this feature detection as described in Section 7.1.4, it is reasonable that the
detector identifies possible false feature candidates in the noisy regions of the image. This
occurs as the required semicircle of n = 12 pixels, all brighter or darker than the candi-
date can appear randomly within the noise, leading to incorrect detections. Additionally,
the use of multilevel patches as landmark descriptors allows for tracking the position of
landmarks with non-corner features such as lines; it has been observed in some cases that
only line features are tracked along the wall. This, combined with rapidly moving features
detected in the noisy part of the image, causes the line-features to drift quickly in the
vertical direction, further adding to the error in the estimation.
The segment with successful tracking of features in Sydhavnen has optimal conditions,
with the ROV facing the wall directly, removing the aforementioned source of noise from
the ”open water” of the canal, as now the vertical structures in the wall stand out in
contrast to the background, allowing for robust tracking. Indeed, any object with high
contrast to the background, such as the bright yellow tether, is identified as a good source
of landmarks and tracked successfully by ROVIO.
Therefore, it is believed that given better lighting conditions, the algorithm could work at
the same level of success in Sydhavnen as in the controlled environment. However, when
considering deployment in the Ocean, where low light at depths is constant, the algo-
rithm could be improved by considering deep learning-based image enhancement methods;
however, this could potentially hinder real-time operation of the ROV due to increased
computational overhead. Furthermore, other methods could be considered for the visual
part of the algorithm, such as direct sparse optimization, taking the entire image into
account when calculating the photometric error, and not just selected patches. Finally,
the addition of lights could be considered.
7.6
Chapter Summary
• The ROVIO VIO algorithm has been implemented, and tested in a controlled en-
vironment with data from the UScanner, leading to a set of optimal parameters.
The performance has been evaluated by considering the Euclidean distance between
the estimated trajectory and the baseline trajectory obtained from the OptiTrack
system.
• In addition to determining the optimal set of parameters, an explorative analysis
considering 25 combinations of parameter values has been performed to investigate
what effect the maximum number of tracked landmarks, as well as patch size, has
on the performance of ROVIO.
• The ROVIO algorithm has been evaluated on the data obtained in the primary data
194
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:223==
acquisition campaign in Sydhavnen, comparing a small usable segment to a USBL
baseline.
• Experiments with the hardware have been made to evaluate the performance of
ROVIO when the amount of data available is decreased.
• A complete pre-processing pipeline has been obtained, describing how the data from
the USCanner should be processed to work with the ROVIO algorithm.
• For the low light data obtained in the primary data acquisition campaign in Syd-
havnen, two image enhancement techniques have been presented: A custom image
processing pipeline and Retinex, with the latter proving unusable.
• The alignment of the inertial world coordinate frame W with that of either the
OptiTrack coordinate system or the real-world ENU coordinate system has been de-
scribed. For OptiTrack by using the captured orientation at the beginning of the
trajectory, and for the real-world coordinate system by using the built-in magne-
tometer of the BlueROV2.

This chapter presents two distinct approaches for estimating
depth from images, as shown in Figure 8.2. One objective
is to enhance the seabed mapping described in Section 5.1
by providing a denser representation and improved semantic
accuracy using multiview RGB images. To achieve this, a
pipeline for 2D object detection followed by object masking
is introduced.
The resulting mask allows the exclusion of
the background and other objects, enabling a more focused
analysis of the 3D reconstruction.
8.1
Object detection in 2D
As previously mentioned Carion et al.[88] proposed the first
end-to-end object detector based on the transformer architec-
ture. By using bipartite matching between object candidates
and directly predicting the one-to-one object set it eliminated
the need for hand-crafted anchors and non-maximum suppression components. This work
inspired the generalization for object detection in 3D and the 3DETR model explored in
the Section 6.2.3. However, the weakness’ of the previously discussed 3DETR also hold
true for the DETR architecture in the 2D case, namely high computational cost, slow
training convergence and queries being hard to optimize. Many variants of 3DETR been
proposed like the Group-DETR[199] that introduces a one-to-many assignment, Eﬀicient
DETR[200] that reduced the encoder and decoder layers for less computational cost or
Lite DETR[201] that reduced the update frequency of low-level features in the encoder.
Stereo
images
Undistortion
Rectification
Stereo
Matching
Depth
Anything v2
Depth
Estimation
RGBD
Image
Figure 8.2: Depth estimation pipeline from stereo images, involving undistortion, rectifica-
tion, and stereo matching, or an alternative approach using Depth Anything v2, resulting
in an RGB-D image.
DINO[202] introduces a mixed query selection that does not solely rely on a fixed set of
learned or randomly selected queries. The idea is to select more dynamic queries based
on high-confidence features from the encoder and thereby the queries are more likely to
be closer to the actual object location.
8.1.1
RT-DETR
From many of these learnings the Real-Time Detection Transformer(RT-DETR)[203] arose
designed to perform real-time objection detection in videos with comparable performance
to the popular You Only Look Once (YOLO) architecture[204].
RT-DETR builds its
eﬀiciency on an architecture that first leverages a convolutional backbone, like ResNet[205],
to extract rich multi-scale features. Here, features are obtained from the last three layers
of the backbone, denoted as {S3, S4, S5}
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
197
==PAGE:226==
Figure 8.3: Overview of RT‑DETR. Multi-scale features from the backbone’s last three
stages are fed into an eﬀicient hybrid encoder, which converts them into a sequential rep-
resentation using Attention‑based Intra‑scale Feature Interaction (AIFI) and CNN‑based
Cross‑scale Feature Fusion (CCFF). Next, the uncertainty‑minimal query selection mecha-
nism chooses a fixed set of encoder features as initial object queries. Finally, a transformer
decoder with auxiliary prediction heads iteratively refines these queries to produce the fi-
nal category labels and bounding boxes. Figure is from [203].
Following the feature extraction, the model employs a hybrid encoder that consists of
the Attention based Intra-scale Feature Interation (AIFI) and the CNN-based Cross-scale
Feature Fusion (CCFF).
Figure 8.4:
The fusion block in
CCFF. Figure is from [203].
The AIFI optimizes computational eﬀiciency by re-
stricting the self-attention operation, as presented in
section 6.2.2, solely to the high-level features in S5,
based on the intuition that S5 contains richer seman-
tic information compared to the lower-level features
S3 and S4. By applying the self-attention mecha-
nism exclusively to S5, the AIFI-enabled single-scale
transformer encoder effectively captures relation-
ships between high-level conceptual entities, while
intra-scale interactions on lower level features, due
to their limited semantic content, are deemed unnecessary. This approach significantly
lowers computational overhead, given that standard self-attention formulation incurs a
quadratic cost with respect to the sequence length[203].
The CCFF module integrates features from adjacent scales. Each fusion block is designed
to fuse two adjacent scale features into a new enriched feature. Here the low resolution
image is upsampled using bilinear interpolation, before being concatenated along the fea-
ture dimension. Hereafter, two 1×1 convolutional layers are used to adjust the number of
channels, then applies N Repblocks composed of RepConv [206] which are used for feature
fusion. Finally, the two parallel outputs are merged via element-wise addition. Combin-
ing these elements results in the complete formulation of the hybrid encoder, where the
reshape operation restores the flattened features back to the original spatial dimensions
of S5.
198
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:227==
Q = K = V = Flatten (S5) ,
(8.1)
F5 = Reshape(AIFI(Q, K, V)),
(8.2)
O = CCFF({S3, S4, F5)}.
(8.3)
To alleviate the challenge of optimizing object queries, previous methods select the top K
encoder features based solely on their confidence scores[207]. However, since queries must
jointly model both object category and location, using only the confidence score introduces
uncertainty and leads to suboptimal query initialization, as no inherent localization infor-
mation is present. This is alleviated by the query selection not only being a result of the
confidence score, but also the IoU of between the ground truth and predicted bounding
box. Here, the uncertainty minimal query selection scheme that explicitly constructs and
minimizes epistemic uncertainty is proposed. This uncertainty U is defined as the discrep-
ancy between the predicted distribution of localization P and classification C as shown in
Equation (8.4). By integrating this into the loss function to minimize the uncertainty of
the queries Equation (8.5).
U(ˆX) = ||P(ˆX) −C(ˆX)||,
(8.4)
L(ˆX, ˆY, Y) = Lbox(ˆb, b) + Lcls(U(ˆX),ˆc, c).
(8.5)
Here ˆY and Y denotes the prediction. ^Y = {ˆc, ˆb} with ˆc and ˆb respectively represents the
category and bounding box while ^X represent the encoder feature.
8.1.2
Fine-tuning
The initial performance of the pre-trained model was limited, primarily because it had
been trained on the COCO 2017 dataset, which includes 121,408 annotated images across
80 diverse categories that do not encompass the specific classes observed in this study and,
therefore, the objects present in the pool environment were out-of-distribution compared
to those in the original training data.

To adapt RT-DETR to the specific detec-
tion task, the network was fine-tuned on
a custom annotated dataset. This dataset
consists of 1888 images, which represent
a subset of the experiment shown in Fig-
ure 4.34. Out of these, 1009 images were
allocated for training and validation using
a 90%/10% split.
These images capture
the path taken before reaching the sec-
ond milk crate, while the remaining 879
images, reserved for testing, illustrate the
return path.
The annotated object cat-
egories include milk crate, cylinder, cali-
bration board, pool ladder, and triangular
prism. A histogram over the counts can be
seen in Figure 8.5.
The fine-tuning process leveraged the pre-trained weights of RT-DETRv2-L[113] consisting
of 42 million parameters, which were then refined to capture task-specific features. In
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
199
==PAGE:228==
particular, the detection heads and transformer decoder were updated while keeping the
initial convolutional layers frozen to preserve the general image representations learned
during pre-training. The model was trained for 30 epoches with an initial low learning rate
of 5e-5 and 300 warmup steps. A weight decay of 0.01 was chosen to help prevent overfitting
and a gradient accumulation step of two was chosen. The model was optimized using a
multi-task loss that combined bounding box regression and classification losses, along with
the uncertainty-minimization term as defined in Equation (8.4) and Equation (8.5). The
training and validation loss can be found in Appendix G.2.
During fine-tuning, several data augmentation strategies were applied to enhance the
model’s robustness and generalization:
• Perspective (p=0.1): With a 10% chance, the image undergoes a perspective trans-
formation, simulating different viewing angles.
• HorizontalFlip (p=0.5): With a 50% probability, the image is flipped horizontally.
• RandomBrightnessContrast (p=0.5): With a 50% chance, the brightness and con-
trast are randomly adjusted to simulate various lighting conditions.
• HueSaturationValue (p=0.1): With a 10% probability, the hue, saturation, and value
of the image are modified, increasing color variability.
8.2
Segmentation
8.2.1
Segment Anything
Segment Anything (SAM) is work that is inspired by the advances and successes of foun-
dation models within the natural language processing domain that have in later years
proved strong zero and few-shot generalization. Something that is partially attributed to
the previously mentioned scaling-law for large language models[192].
SAM consists of three primary components: an image encoder, a prompt encoder, and
a mask decoder, as illustrated in Figure 8.6. The image encoder utilizes a Masked Au-
toencoder (MAE) pre-trained Vision Transformer (ViT) to generate image embeddings,
depicted in Figure 8.6. Subsequently, two distinct prompt categories are employed: sparse
prompts, such as points, bounding boxes, or text, and dense prompts, such as masks.
Points and bounding boxes are encoded through positional embeddings [180], whereas tex-
tual prompts are processed using the pre-trained CLIP text encoder [208]. Dense masks
undergo convolutional encoding and are combined via element-wise summation with the
corresponding image embeddings.
Figure 8.6: SAM employs a heavyweight image encoder to generate an embedding that
is eﬀiciently queried with various prompts, yielding object masks in near real-time. For
ambiguous prompts involving multiple objects, SAM produces several valid masks with
corresponding confidence scores. Figure is from: [209].
200
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:229==
The mask decoder employs the the same transformer decoder design as shown in Figure 6.8
which takes the prompt encoder as the input embedding and performs cross-attention
to the image embedding, followed by a dynamic mask prediction head. The decoder is
further modified by performing cross-attention in two directions, from prompt to image
embedding, and from image to prompt embedding to update all embeddings. Hereafter,
the image embedding is up sampled and an FNN maps the output token to a linear
classifier that predicts the mask probability at each pixel in the image. In the case that
a prompt have ambiguity it will lead to multiple valid masks. To alleviate this the model
is modified to predict 3 output masks for a single prompt, as this covers most cases for
ambiguity, namely the mask for the whole object, the part, and subpart as shown in in
valid masks part of Figure 8.6.
Segment Anything 2 (SAM2)[114] builds on the original SAM architecture while introduc-
ing key enhancements. Like SAM, SAM2 comprises of an image encoder, prompt encoder,
and mask decoder, but it incorporates several refinements:
• Improved Mask Decoder: SAM2 refines the cross-attention mechanism between
prompt and image embeddings, leading to higher-quality mask predictions even in
challenging, ambiguous scenarios.
• Enhanced Prompt Encoding: By incorporating advanced prompt encoding strate-
gies, SAM2 more effectively differentiates between similar objects and handles low-
quality or imprecise prompts.
• Eﬀiciency and Scalability: With further optimization, SAM2 achieves better com-
putational eﬀiciency, making it even more suitable for real-time and interactive
applications.
• Expanded Training Regime: Leveraging additional training data and enhanced train-
ing strategies, SAM2 demonstrates improved generalization across diverse image
distributions and segmentation tasks.
These improvements make SAM2 a robust successor, addressing some of the limitations
of the original model, particularly in handling segmentation ambiguities and small or
complex objects.
8.3
Depth Estimation
8.3.1
Rectification and disparity
Camera lenses deviate from the ideal pinhole model, resulting in geometric distortion that
warps captured images. Two primary types of distortion are generally considered: radial
distortion and tangential distortion. Radial distortion occurs due to the bending of light
rays more significantly towards the edges of the lens compared to its center. In contrast,
tangential distortion arises when the lens is not perfectly aligned parallel to the imaging
plane, causing the captured image to appear tilted or off-center. Typically, lens distortion
is modeled by extending the pinhole camera projection with distortion coeﬀicients based on
the Brown-Conrady model[210]. In this approach, distorted image coordinates (xd, yd) are
defined in terms of the undistorted normalized coordinates (x, y) and distortion parameters
(k1, k2, k3, . . . ) as follows:
xd = x(1 + k1r2 + k2r4 + k3r6 + . . . ),
yd = y(1 + k1r2 + k2r4 + k3r6 + . . . ),
(8.6)
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
201
==PAGE:230==
where r =
p
(xd −xc)2 + (yd −yc)2 is the Euclidean distance between the distorted image
point and the distortion center. Tangential distortion is modeled by coeﬀicients p1, p2 that
account for lens misalignment:
xd = x + (2p1xy + p2(r2 + 2x2)),
yd = y + (p1(r2 + 2y2)2p2xy).
(8.7)
Undistortion is the process of applying the inverse transformation of the lens distortion to
map image pixels back to their ideal undistorted locations. Here, the camera’s intrinsic
matrix K containing the focal length fx, fy and principal point cx, cy with the distortion
coeﬀicients to remap each pixel in the distorted image to a new corrected position. The in-
trinsic parameters and transformation between the two images planes are obtained during
the calibration process described in Section 3.4.3.
In practice, perfect alignment of two cameras is diﬀicult, so rectification is performed to
correct for the misaligned perspectives in the two images, such that a point in 3D does not
project to a different pixel coordinate in the right and the left image. Stereo rectification
is a transformation that simplifies the epipolar geometry such that the image planes are
aligned onto a common plane.
After rectification, corresponding points will have the
same y-coordinate in both images, meaning that the epipolar lines become horizontal and
aligned with the image rows[210]. By doing this the stereo matching is reduced to a 1D
search problem, since the disparity search can be done along a single image row rather
than across the whole image.
This transformation begins by converting the original pixel coordinates u, v in normalized
coordinates:
x = u −cx

such that the homogeneous coordinates X, Y, W is converted back to Cartesian x′, y′.

The Figure 8.7 illustrates this geometry.
In rectified stereo images, the difference in
horizontal position between a pair of corresponding points is known as the disparity. This
disparity is typically measured in pixels and defined as the offset d = xL −xR.
The
disparity encapsulates the parallax shift caused by the two camera viewpoints, and it is
inversely related to the scene depth. Namely that nearby objects yield a larger disparity,
while distant objects yield a smaller disparity. Under the pinhole camera model disparity
d is related to the actual depth Z of the 3D point by:
Z = fB
d ,
(8.14)
where B is the baseline between the two camera centers. This disparity-depth relationship
implies that once correspondences are found, the depth of each point can be recovered.
Figure 8.7: Figure of a rectified stereo image
setup. Figure is from: [210].
To
solve
this
correspondence
problem,
stereo vision traditionally relies on block
matching.
Here a small window around
a pixel in one image is compared to win-
dows in the other image along the same
scanlines.
A matching cost is computed
to quantify how well the block in one im-
age matches the block in the other im-
age shifted by d.
Common cost func-
tions include the sum of absolute differ-
ences (SAD) and sum of squared differ-
ences (SSD) that both compare the inten-
sity values I(x, y) of the two images:

(8.15)
A lower score indicates a closer match between the two blocks and thereby select the
disparity d the minimizes the cost.
Repeating this process for every pixel produces a
disparity map for the entire image that directly can be transformed into a depth map
using Equation (8.14).

8.3.2
Depth Anything
Pure block matching can be sensitive to image noise, repetitive textures, like flat contin-
uous surfaces, and occlusions[210]. Other methods like Structure-from-Motion (Sfm) and
Multi-view Stereo (MVS) for 3D reconstruction analyzes multiple images for keypoint cor-
respondences and camera pose estimation. These pipelines are, however, computational
expensive due to them involving bundle adjustment and dense matching.
Depth Anything[211] is a recently introduced foundation model for monocular depth esti-
mation (MDE) that aims to predict a dense depth map from a single image. Inspired by
Segment Anything, Depth Anything is designed as a general purpose depth predictor for
”any image under any circumstances”.
The idea of this approach is that two different datasets are acquired: Dl = (xi, di)M
i=1
and Du = (ui)N
i=1 that respectively denotes a dataset of 1̃.5M labeled images and 6̃2M
unlabeled images. The goal is to learn a teacher model T from Dl that are then used
to assign pseudo depth labels for Du. Hereafter, a smaller student model S is trained
on a combination of the labeled and pseudo labeled image set, as briefly illustrated in
Figure 8.8.
Figure 8.8: Solid lines indicate the flow of labeled images, whereas dotted lines denote the
flow of unlabeled images. The figure highlights the significance of large-scale unlabeled
datasets.
The label S signifies the application of strong perturbations.
To enrich the
depth estimation model with robust semantic priors, an auxiliary constraint is introduced
between the online student model and a frozen encoder, thereby preserving semantic ca-
pability. Figure is from: [211].
Since the labeled images comes in a wide range of environments and from different sources
an aﬀine-invariant loss Ll is created to ignore the unknown scale and shift of each sample:

where cos(., .) measures the cosine similarity between two high-dimensional and continuous
feature vectors. f is obtained from the student model S while f′ is from a frozen DINOv2
encoder. The tolerance margin α is introduced since semantic encoders (DINOv2) have a
tendency to produce similar features for different parts of an object. Which is desireable
in a pure segmentation context, but problematic for depth estimation. Therefore, if the
cosine similarity exceeds α, the corresponding pixel is excluded from Lfeat loss.
The

and student models employ a ViT encoder and a DPT[214] decoder for depth regression.
Depth Anything’s predictions are in the form of relative depth. The output is propor-
tional to actual scene depth, but not calibrated to exact units as training directly on the
metric depth across many different datasets can hinder generalization[211].
Moreover,
its pre-trained encoder serves as an effective initialization for downstream metric depth
estimation. During fine-tuning, the encoder (using a ViT-L backbone) is initialized with
pre-trained weights while the decoder is randomly initialized, and the model is trained
with metric depth supervision under both in-domain and zero-shot settings.

The stereo matching algorithm currently produces inconclusive results. In many instances,
it fails to generate valid disparity maps of the environment, resulting in no usable depth
information. On occasion, as illustrated in Figure 8.10, the algorithm produces a disparity
representation, but its quality is suboptimal. Consequently, further analysis using this
implementation is not warranted.
Figure 8.10: Example of two resulting disparity images, from the stereo matching algo-
rithm.
Investigating the depth images from Depth Anything, as seen in Figure 8.11, initially
results appears promising. A clear distinction between the foreground and background is
visible, and the fine details of the objects are well-defined. However, further investigation
reveals that the depth estimates do not scale properly as the observed surfaces and objects
recede into the distance. At these larger distances the model severely underestimates the
actual depth to the surface.
Figure 8.11: Example metric depth images from the Depth Anything v2. Depth is given
in meters.
Using these metric depth estimations, the scene depicted in the image can be reconstructed
in 3D, as shown in Figure 8.12.
Again, it can be qualitatively observed that the 3D
reconstruction captures the overall geometry of the scene and its objects. Indicating that
the model captures the relational depth for pixels in the images, but struggles to map it to
the captures the precise metric depth. As an example the milk crate in the reconstructed
3D point cloud (Figure 8.12) have dimensions 0.311 × 0.227 × 0.240 meters while the true
dimensions are 0.405 × 0.305 × 0.265 meters.
