8.5
Discussion
The fine-tuned RT-DETR model demonstrated strong detection performance for specific
object classes, achieving high average precision scores. However, the results also high-
lighted variability across classes, notably with weaker performance in detecting calibration
boards and pool ladders. This discrepancy could be a result of different viewing angles
between the train and test set.
Interestingly, the model does not detect the objects observed as reflected on the water
surface. This could be attributed to that the model is already over-fitted despite the low
number of epochs during fine-tuning.
Segmentation using the SAM2 model produced high-quality masks that significantly iso-
lated objects from backgrounds, facilitating targeted analysis for reconstruction. However,
the quality of segmentation is inherently linked to the quality of detected bounding boxes
used a prompts, being a weak link of the semantic masking.
Depth estimation via stereo matching yielded inconsistent results, with significant chal-
lenges in generating accurate disparity maps, particularly in scenes dominated by homoge-
neous surfaces. This is attributed to both the known issue of sensitivity for homogeneous
textures and an inconsistent rectification of the stereo setup. Here non-parallel epipolar
lines were at times observed, removing the desired correspondence during the 1D search
for stereo matching. This issue could be attributed to many things, like not accurately
enough estimating the intrinsic and extrinsic parameters during camera calibration. How-
ever, the challenges encountered in stereo disparity estimation highlight the importance
of robust feature matching methods capable of handling repetitive textures.
Depth Anything v2 demonstrated promising qualitative results in terms of relative depth
estimation from mono images, but struggled with precise metric scale and quantitative
accuracy, especially at surfaces far from the camera.
This can be explained from the
perspective that the foundation model is trained to estimate the relative depth in a given
scene. To extract metric depth capabilities the pre-trained encoder first has to be fine-
tuned on a given provided depth enabled dataset. Here the problem arises that the dataset
for metric depth fine-tuning does not match the environment with the additional light
distortions and reflections that an underwater environment adds. Here obtaining a per-
domain dataset for fine tuning. The depth analysis provided was qualitative primary due
to the lack of a ground truth. However, it was still deemed not feasible with the current
implementation to reach a suﬀicient depth precision that would enable future map fusion
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
209
==PAGE:238==
with the existing seabed mapping obtained in Chapter 5.
Performing object detection and semantic masking in 2D leverages more advanced and
computationally eﬀicient methods compared to 3D object detection. Having accurate pixel
coordinates for each object simplifies maintaining correspondence when transforming into
point cloud representations.
The field of both monocular and stereo depth estimation remains highly active, with con-
tinuous advancements improving the effectiveness of these methods. Emerging methods,
such as NVIDIA’s Foundation Stereo[190], claim significant improvements in zero-shot
stereo matching and address previously observed challenges related to long-range context
reasoning.
Investigating these recent developments could provide valuable insights for
future research in underwater depth estimation.
Finally, this work has focused primarily on the initial stages of 3D reconstruction and has
not addressed subsequent steps such as the fusion of multiple reconstructions or integrating
multiple views of objects into unified 3D maps. Methods for fusion could include Coherent
Point Drift[215] for point set registration, which would be critical to explore in future
studies.
8.6
Chapter Summary
The main areas covered in this chapter:
• The RT-DETR model exhibited robust object detection capabilities but faced per-
formance inconsistencies across object classes due to potential dataset limitations or
class imbalances.
• SAM2 produced high-quality segmentation masks, yet its effectiveness was directly
tied to the accuracy of bounding box predictions.
• Stereo matching faced significant challenges in environments characterized by ho-
mogeneous textures and calibration inaccuracies, limiting its practical utility.
• Depth Anything v2 showed promising qualitative relative depth estimation but strug-
gled with accurate metric scaling, particularly in underwater conditions, due to
training primarily on relative depth data.
210
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:239==
9
Multi-agent Marine System
Collaborative
SLAM and
Database
Figure 9.1:
This
chapter addresses
the
Collabo-
rative
SLAM
integration
of
the
Multi-agent
system.
This section will consolidate the previously described technologies into
a multi-agent marine system by proposing a collaborative SLAM ap-
proach. The collaborative part of the system is intended to solve the
problem of aligning the observations of the multiple robots in the sys-
tem into the same space, which can be done in a centralized or decen-
tralized manner as presented in the literature review (Section 1.1.5).
The choice of method highly relies on the properties of the system and
the definition of the system’s operational assumptions and properties.
An analysis of the system can be conducted by following The taxon-
omy of swarm robots proposed in [216]. A more in-depth description
of the taxonomy and the different terms and categories used can be
found in the original paper.
Swarm Size: The size of the swarm can be quantified as a LIM-GROUP meaning that it
consists of n number of AUVs and ASVs where n is small relative to the size of the
environment. The system must consist of at least one ASV and one AUV, as the
ASV dictates the positioning of the AUV.
Communication Range: The vehicles are limited by their communication medium and
method. Ideally, the ASV and AUV will communicate the accumulated information
to a server when a communication link to the server is established. The ASVs are
able to communicate with limited wireless bandwidth to a cloud service, given that
it is near the employed network. The AUVs are either able to communicate at a high
rate through tether if such configuration is employed. If the AUVs are tetherless,
several constraints apply as ultimately this will potentially require the vehicle to
resurface to share information with the swarm.
Communication topology: The proposed system allows for a broadcast approach or TOP-
BROAD, which means that every robot can communicate with all of the other robots
and are not placed in a hierarchy.
Communication bandwidth: The communication is BAND-LOW as the cost of establish-
ing communication in the tetherless scenario is very high and the communication
rate is inconsistent, which suggests that the robots are very independent.
Swarm reconfigurabillity: The robots are under coordinated rearrangement or ARR-COMM,
as the AUVs positions are dictated by the mission provided by the ASV, but is should
be able to avoid obstacles by dynamically adjusting to the environment.
Swarm Unit Processing ability: The proposed method intends to work in real-time on
each vehicle. Addressing this would require a more in-depth analysis and tests of
the deployed system.
Swarm Composition: The swarm consists of robots of two types, which makes it a het-
erogeneous system in terms of sensor modalities, behavior, and general physical
structure and capabilities.
Given the operational and communication constraints, a distributed, decentralized ap-
proach is most suitable. Such a system will optimize the operational range of the system,
enforce resiliency to communication failures, reduce the load of information shared, and
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
211
==PAGE:240==
comply with the constraints on communication and general robustness. The proposed
system is mainly inspired by [116] and [106]. The decentralized distributed approach sug-
gests that each robot solves both the local and global optimization problems individually,
sharing a common representation of the observed data in a message pool.
Based on the work done in this project, and the initial results obtained, an extension of
the setup originally proposed in Section 1.2 is proposed in Figure 9.2.

Figure 9.2: The proposed system that has been further expanded from Figure 1.3. The
decentralized approach adds a global optimization aspect to each robot, marked as C-
SLAM in the figure, which estimates the relative transforms between the local SLAM
graphs by sharing a common sparse message representation through the shared message
pool when communication is established. The USV dictates the mission plan of the AUV
by using automatic object detection of the seabed map. The mission objective is then sent
to the AUV, which conducts path planning and performs an Inspection of a given ROI.
The full maps are shared with an external database.
This approach builds on the individual parts of the project, integrating them into a fully
212
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:241==
distributed collaborative SLAM system.
USV SLAM
The implementation of LIO-SAM and the fusing of the acoustic- and LiDAR point cloud
into a seabed-to-sky map pave much of the way for integrating the USV into a collaborative
SLAM setup.
Indeed, the proposed collaborative SLAM method uses LIO-SAM as a
local SLAM method on the robots[106], which suggests using LiDAR features for robust
collaborative mapping above sea with other USVs. However, collaboration with AUVs
must be enabled through the shared underwater domain by sharing a representation of
the acoustic data with the AUVs and vice versa.
Extending Object Detection
In essence, the underlying system design for object detection has not changed from the
initially presented system architecture. However, a proposal of the 3DETR architecture
or an extension thereof, as a backbone for the object detection in 3D, have been analyzed
in Chapter 6. By incorporating a learning-based approach, the system becomes dynami-
cally adaptable to a wide range of environmental conditions, thereby enhancing both the
robustness and precision of detection. In addition, as the model simultaneously predicts
the semantic class of each ROI, it becomes possible to prioritize objects based on their
relevance. This prioritization enables AUVs to autonomously focus on important ROIs
while discarding less critical ones. The determined ROI positions and object classifica-
tions are then communicated to a shared message pool among system agents, triggering
one or multiple mission starts for the AUVs and initiating preliminary path planning.
From ROV to AUV
The work done on the ROV within this project has been that of first calibrating the sensors
and then implementing and evaluating the ROVIO VIO algorithm, which is capable of
estimating the trajectory in an underwater setting. These are now combined with the
efforts regarding photogrammetry, forming a comprehensive visual-inertial SLAM solution
that, in addition to providing robust localization, produces a highly detailed seabed map.
Additional sensor modalities must be considered to improve the robustness of the SLAM
solution in environments with a complete lack of discernible visual features. Furthermore,
with the addition of path planning to ROIs returned by the object detection module,
the underwater vehicle now gains autonomous capabilities, leading to the renaming of
this block of the diagram to Autonomous Underwater Vehicle (AUV) SLAM. The USBL
is still used as a reference localization method. The produced seabed map is added to
a database. At the same time, the local visual-inertial SLAM algorithm on the vehicle
communicates with the other vehicles via the shared message pool, thereby integrating
their maps with its own, enabling the collaborative SLAM aspect, where both local and
global map is updated locally.
Communication
To differentiate between communication that is required during deployment and data that
is offloaded after deployment, a message pool is introduced in addition to the database.
The global optimization (C-SLAM) blocks utilize the sparse representations of what each
robot has perceived by accessing it through the message pool. The database contains the
full local graphs from each vehicle, and additionally, collected reference data should be
employed.
The following sections will describe collaborative SLAM and a description of how it could
be employed in the proposed system, based on the individual parts described above.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

==PAGE:242==
9.1
From SLAM to Collaborative SLAM
Recall from Section 5.2 that the aim is to find the optimal set of states X∗by maximizing
the posterior distribution P(X|Z) which is equivalent to
X∗.= argmax

Given that all initial states are known in a multi-robot system, the extension from a single
robot SLAM MAP to multiple now includes all robots’ states, measurements, and inter-
robot measurements linking different local maps[19]. In the proposed setup with a ASV
and a AUV the two robots (α, β), will independently obtain two sets of measurements
Zα, Zβ and the inter-robot measurements linking the relative pose estimates. Given this
the estimation of the relative state variables (X∗

If the relative starting positions and orientations of the robots are unknown, no mean-
ingful joint prior p(Xα, Xβ) is available because there are infinitely many possible initial
alignments. In such cases, the problem reduces to maximizing the likelihood of the mea-
surements. That is, C-SLAM is reformulated as a Maximum Likelihood Estimation (MLE)

As more robots are added to the system, the same estimation framework can be extended
to include all robots’ states as well as the inter-robot measurements linking their local
maps. However, with each additional robot the optimization problem becomes increas-
ingly complex due to the higher-dimensional state space. This increased complexity neces-
sitates scalable, distributed, and decentralized methods, which can eﬀiciently handle the
communication and computational load without overwhelming centralized computation
resources.
While the uniformed SLAM formulation gives a strong framework for joint state-estimation
for multiple robots, the implementation details must account for the specific operational
domains and chosen sensor modalities of the different platforms.
Here especially the
intermittent communication between the ASV and the AUV is a key limiting factor. To
address these challenges, two specialized SLAM methods are used as inspiration for the
final proposal.
9.2
DiSCo-SLAM
DiSCo-SLAM (Distributed Scan Context-Enabled) [106] addresses the challenges of com-
bining robust loop closure detection between multiple collaborating robots. The method
leverages a sparse LiDAR feature descriptor called scan context[217] for eﬀicient sharing
of observations among robots. This information is utilized in a two-stage global and local
optimization framework where the global step solves the transformation among robots by
identifying and using inter-loop closures1 to estimate the set of transformations by mini-
mizing the total transformation between local robot frames. The inter-robot constraints
1Loop closures made between robots as common representations match.

the KD-Tree search takes a 3D-scan as in-
put from the global message pool, such that
scans are exchanged between all robots.
Scan Context
As a scene descriptor Scan Context[217] is
utilized which is a global LiDAR scan de-
scriptor in the form of a projection of a 3D
point cloud onto a polar grid, that are di-
vided into a number of sector Ns and rings
Nr. Each cell contains a representative value I which is the maximum intensity observed
in that cell. To ensure fast matching scan context uses a ring key, a feature vector k of
dimension Nr, by doing an occupancy count for each ring:
k =
 ψ(r1), ψ(r2), . . . , ψ(rNr)

,
where ψ : ri →R.
(9.5)
Here the ring encoding uses the occupancy ratio of a ring using the L0 norm ψ(ri) = ||ri||0
Ns .
This gives a rotation invariant descriptor, since the view does not change how many points
fall into a given ring, and thereby the ring key captures the coarse radial distribution of
structures, independent of heading. All ring keys vectors from the past scans, and from
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
215
==PAGE:244==
other robots, are stored in a KD-tree for eﬀicient nearest-neighbor lookup[218]. A KD-tree
is a binary space-partitioning data structure that recursively splits the data along different
ring key indices to organize points in a k-dimensional space. This allows nearest-neighbor
queries to be answer on average in O(log M) time, with M being the number of stored
keys. Given a new scan’s ring key, the KD-tree is queried for the closest matches. This
results in a short list of top-K loop closure candidates, i.e. indices of scans with similar
ring distribution. These top number of similar keys scan context is retrieved and compared
against the query scan context. To achieve rotation invariance the scan context matching
algorithm performs a circular shift alignment along the sector (column) axis. This means
that the candidate scans contexts are cyclically shifted to find the best similarity score.
The shift that produces the highest similarity is taken to be the correct alignment. The
estimated sector shift angle n∗is used for the initial yaw alignment guess for the point
cloud registration using ICP.
If an inter-robot loop closure candidate for robot αi at timestep i and robot βj at timestep
j is detected through the feature matching algorithm from the scans, a corresponding sep-
arator pose pair ⟨αi, βj⟩is returned to a filtering module based on Pairwise Consistent
Measurement (PCM). This module ensures that only valid loop closures are accepted, re-
jecting potential wrong candidates that may result from structural similarities in different
regions of the map.
This is done by considering both the inter- and intra-robot con-
straints for both robots. The PCM returns local transformations from robot α to robot
β in the global coordinate frame to both the local message pool and the transformation
optimization module.
Transformation Optimization
The transformation optimization module performs the two-stage global and local opti-
mization after obtaining a valid separator pose pair and the transformations between the
robots. For the global step, all transformations between the robots are considered mea-
surements in the global frame, optimizing only the transformation from the local robot
frame to the global coordinate frame using a Levenberg-Marquardt-based optimization
scheme. Following the global optimization step, all the separator poses are transformed
to the local coordinate frame, and an Euclidean distance-based radius search is performed
to find the nearest inter-robot constraint (separator pose), after which the intra-robot
loop closures (αzβjk) are calculated using the found nearest inter-robot separator pose
(αxk) and the present separator pose (αxβj). From the local message pool, this intra-robot
loop-closure is passed to the local SLAM algorithm such that the local pose graph can be
updated and optimized, as well as the global message pool. Also from the global message
pool, any potential intra-robot loop closures from the perspective of other robots (γzαmαn)
are taken into account.
Importantly, DiSCo-SLAM demonstrates that utilizing the Scan Context scene descriptor
yields a much more compact representation with a mean size of 4.08 kB size of the scan
context features and the local pose using a VLP-16 LiDAR[106].
DRACo-SLAM
Another approach for collaborative SLAM would be DRACo-SLAM (Distributed Robust
Acoustic Communication-eﬀicient SLAM)[116]. DRACo-SLAM is specifically designed for
underwater robots using sonar-based perception. Again, the idea here is not to contin-
uously communicate raw data between units but having a compact feature descriptor
instead such that the bandwidth requirements of inter-robot communication is low. They
do this by having a simplified robot pose xn,t = (xr, yr, θr)⊤, such that the robot pose is
defined with a fixed depth.
From this a scene descriptor is created using a range-based histogram based on the sonar
216
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:245==
data. The histogram will encode a given scene into different range bins of the histogram,
which is selected a priori, making it rotational invariant. One issue is with their given
approach is that the scene descriptor treats everything as a (x, y)-plane. This is feasible
as long as the environment consists of surfaces along the z dimension, like inside a harbor
area, but it would be problematic for open-sea SLAM.
Figure 9.5: System diagram for DRACo-SLAM for a
single AUV. The message pool consists received scene
descriptors. If regisition yields a inter-robot lopp clo-
sure it is added to the pose graph and shared with
team members. Image from [116].
The key takeaway from these two
approaches is their emphasis on
decentralized and compact repre-
sentation to overcome communi-
cation constraints. While DiSCo-
SLAM’s approach is more ro-
bust for varied terrain due to its
more information rich context de-
scriptors, DRACo-SLAM presents
a more streamlined but limited
method that performs optimally
in structured or shallow-depth en-
vironments.
In essence both DiSCo-SLAM and
DRACo-SLAM shares similarities
in the algorithmic design and both
utilizes a shared message pool
that would allow for collabora-
tion between the respective sur-
face and underwater modalities.
9.3
Discussion
The collaborative SLAM framework must remain eﬀicient as the number of agents grow.
A key challenge is the communication overhead, as underwater acoustic channels have
limited bandwidth (in the range of few kB per second), which limits how much mapping
data robots can share in real time. As previously mentioned many approaches often only
exchange lightweight descriptors due to bandwidth limitations. Here, robots might not
fully share their maps until after missions, avoiding network saturation. In the proposed
setting, this corresponds to AUVs transmitting the results from their local SLAM when
resurfacing and acting independently or with limited communication capabilities using
acoustic modems during submersed missions. Another concern is that the computational
load also scales with the team and environment size. In a global SLAM back-end setting,
this could become a bottleneck as the map sizes grow. A decentralized approach migrates
some of these strains by distributing processing across vehicles.
The proposed system architecture, as presented in Figure 9.2, relies on different sensor
modalities for the AUV and ASV, and an open problem is how to design reliant scene
descriptors that can work in very sparse environments. This comes from the consideration
that many methods are developed for feature-rich environments that do not have to deal
with largely dynamic or uniformly shaped surfaces that lack distinctive features. Another
complication arises when having to compare scene descriptors from different modalities,
as these modalities introduce different levels of FoV, precision, and operational ranges. In
addition, the LiDAR-specific scene descriptor Scan Context and the descriptor developed
for the DRACo-SLAM framework might not give suﬀicient accuracy, as the underlying
assumption does not hold true for an open-water environment. Here, an avenue for ad-
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
217
==PAGE:246==
vancement is the development of scene descriptors for heterogeneous environments that
can be shared between underwater and surface vehicles.
Even though this thesis has mainly focused on IMU and camera-based odometry estimation
using VIO for the ROV, additional sensor modalities would be required before it could
act autonomously in diverse environments. This is due to the prolonged periods during
submersion and ascension that no distinguishable visual features would be available for
the video algorithm, and it would have to rely solely on the IMU. Here suitable sensors
would be the Doppler Velocity Logger (DVL) for measuring the velocity and direction of
the AUV and SONARs for dynamic range estimation and feature extraction. The next
step would be to develop a suitable sensor fusion pipeline that would be able to adaptively
include different modalities based on an internally detected viability of the data source.
Here, a major diﬀiculty lies in data association across modalities, with multiple gaps
identified[219], such as the lack of eﬀicient real-time fusion in dynamic, turbid conditions
and the computational diﬀiculty of integrating many sensor types simultaneously.
Given the potential large survey area for this decentralized approach, an indirect robot
loop closure would be more suitable. Indirect robot loop closures are the process of looking
back into maps to find partial overlaps and identify locations that have been visited by
both robots, such that the relative transformations between these maps can be estimated.
Significant contributions need to be made to the ROV from the current iteration before it
can act autonomously. Firstly, the tether and manual control would have to be replaced
with onboard dynamic path planning, and obstacle avoidance would have to be imple-
mented and validated. From here, further work also has to be done for the deployment
process when ROIs are identified by the ASV. Currently, the deployment process is very
manual with the ROV having a limited battery capacity.
This is not a big challenge
for shore-based deployment compared to open-sea deployment. Here, multiple interest-
ing projects are being developed currently, like a modular underwater mothership solu-
tion[220]. This would allow multiple AUVs to operate in a larger area, with re-docking
possibilities for recharging. Such an approach would also allow for direct inter-robot loop
closures.
A decentralized architecture inherently improves fault tolerance because there is no single
point of failure. Consequently, if one vehicle loses connectivity, the others can continue
their respective missions. Each AUV/ASV maintains its own local SLAM process and can
operate independently for prolonged periods of time. Similarly, intermittent communica-
tion links are managed such that vehicles do not require a constant connection to a base
station or every teammate. Having multiple ASVs further opens up the possibility of creat-
ing a distributed network that increases resiliency to attacks or downtime on single nodes.
This setup would enable the creation of a mesh network that allows units to communi-
cate through multi-hop connectivity[19]. However, adversarial attacks remain a significant
concern for inter-robot communication. In particular, byzantine data manipulation could
lead to decision degradation when achieving consensus in decentralized networks. Many of
these issues, including the quantification of information loss due to dynamic environmental
conditions, remain open problems for further research and development.
218
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:247==
10
Conclusion
A payload solution for marine data collection was successfully developed, facilitating mod-
ularity of hardware and software components. The system was demonstrated fully opera-
tional, with multiple SONARs, an IMU, and a IMU, with a major shortcoming being that
the LiDAR operated on its own ROS domain and was therefore isolated from the rest of
the system.
Multiple real-world marine datasets were collected, with the primary using the final it-
eration of the developed sensor fusion payload box deployed on the otter. Within this
dataset, dual SONAR data from an orthogonal stereo configuration is recorded, in addi-
tion to inertial and optical data from the Ouster LiDAR. This dataset is supplemented
in the underwater domain with visual and inertial data recorded by the UScanner using
a stereo-camera configuration. GNSS, acoustic localization, and magnetometer data were
collected and enabled localization.
Seabed-to-sky maps were successfully constructed using the acquired data at a cm-level
accuracy. The addition of a second SONAR proved useful for assessing the accuracy, but
did prove to provide a significant contribution to the general mapping of the seabed.
A semantic classification of the objects was implemented and validated for simulated
environments; however, it remains to be successfully verified with real-world data. With
VIO successfully implemented and tested on the ROV in a controlled setting and with
an initial proposal for semantically aware photogrammetry, the elements to create a fully
integrated VI-SLAM solution are present but yet to be combined. In the current state of
the project, the ROV does, therefore, not contribute to the seabed-to-sky map.
Finally, an extensive multi-agent system design has been presented, integrating the agents
into a decentralized collaborative SLAM approach.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
219
==PAGE:248==
11
Future Work
This section highlights key insights from the preceding chapters and discusses how these
technological advancements can influence the development of future systems.
To succeed in implementing a collaborative SLAM system, further development is holisti-
cally needed to alleviate the computational and communication constraints a multi-agent
system would impose, operating both at surface level and underwater. Here, the proposed
modular system can act as a test bench for future hardware and software iterations.
Many domains within various branches of algorithmic design depend on realistic and rep-
resentative data sources from real-world environments during the development and vali-
dation phase. Thus, on the path toward autonomy, acquiring data that represents a wide
array of sensor modalities, environmental conditions, and scene representations is essential
for robust future research.
A future research topic would be to quantify the difference between object detection capa-
bilities between representations utilizing 2D sonar scans and 3D reconstructions. As object
detection and seabed mapping are tightly coupled, questions are raised about whether a
dense seabed map representation constructed by the USV is optimal. This also applies to
the shared feature descriptor in the collaborative SLAM, which ideally has to be sparse
and effective. Thereby, developing one or more light-weight multi-modal scene descriptor
that allows for comparisons between different scene representations would further enable
intra-robot loop closures in a collaborative SLAM system.
Considering the multi-agent mapping perspective of the project, the algorithm could ei-
ther be replaced by a fully-fledged visual-inertial SLAM solution, such as VINS-Fusion
or ORB-SLAM3. However, the underwater performance of these methods remains to be
assessed. Building on the implementation of ROVIO, the method could be combined with
the proposed photogrammetry solution, and be incorporated into a factor-graph, similar
to the one in LIO-SAM, shown in Figure 5.14, with the LiDAR odometry factor replaced
by visual-inertial odometry, updating the map with photogrammetry for each keyframe,
in the same fashion as is done for LiDAR scans.

// whether the string is complete

12

14

23

24

25

28

31

// Function to generate a GPGLL string
37
String getGPGLLString(float latitude , float longitude , String time) {
38
// Format latitude and longitude into the required NMEA format
39
String lat = String(abs(latitude), 8); // Absolute value and format to 6
decimals
40
String lon = String(abs(longitude), 8); // Absolute value and format to 6
decimals
41
42
if (lon.indexOf('.') == 4) {
43
lon = "0" + lon; // Add leading zero if only two digits before the decimal
44
}
45
if (lon.indexOf('.') == 3) {
46
lon = "00" + lon; // Add leading zero if only two digits before the
decimal
47
}
48
49
// Determine N/S and E/W based on latitude and longitude
50
String latDirection = (latitude >= 0) ? "N" : "S";
234
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:263==
51
String lonDirection = (longitude >= 0) ? "E" : "W";
52
53
// Calculate checksum
54
String gpgll = "$GPGLL ," + lat + "," + latDirection + ","
55
+ lon + "," + lonDirection + "," + time + ",A,A";
56
String checksum = calculateChecksum(gpgll);
57
58
// Build the final GPGLL sentence
59
gpgll = gpgll + checksum;
60
61
return gpgll;
62
}
63
64
// Function to calculate the NMEA checksum
65
String calculateChecksum(String sentence) {
66
byte checksum = 0;
67
for (unsigned int i = 1; i < sentence.length(); i++) {
68
checksum ^= sentence[i]; // XOR each byte in the sentence
69
}
70
String hexChecksum = "*" + String(checksum , HEX);
71
if (hexChecksum.length() < 2) hexChecksum = "0" + hexChecksum;
72
hexChecksum.toUpperCase();
73
return hexChecksum;
74
}
75
76
// Convert TimeLib time to a string in HHMMSS.s format
77
String getTimeString() {
78
// Get hours, minutes , seconds , and milliseconds
79
int h = hour();
// Current hour
80
int m = minute();
// Current minute
81
int s = second();
// Current second
82
int ms = millis() % 1000; // Milliseconds (0-999)
83
84
// Format the time into a string as HHMMSS.s
85
String timeString = String(h, DEC) + String(m, DEC) + String(s, DEC) + "." +
String(ms / 100);
// Only 1 decimal precision for ms
86
return timeString;
87
}

==PAGE:271==
Mission Description 
Set up: 
1. Prepare the BlueROV2 by following the SOP for the BlueROV2 
Experiment sequence: 
1. Deploy the BlueROV2 without the payload and address its current buoyancy 
2. Take the robot out of the water 
3. Mount the payload where there is already (plenty) of foam on it 
4. Slowly lower the robot into the water with your arms. If it is about to sink, pull it out again 
and add foam. If it [loats with the propellers above water, take it out and remove some foam. 
5. Iterate step 3 and 4 until we are satis[ied with the result 
6. Try controlling the BlueROV2 using the QGroundControl  
7. Deploy object of interest 
8. Capture footage using the UVisualiser software  
9. Pull out the ROV of the water and let it drip 
10. Follow the SOP for drying off and detaching the ROV.  
Data collection: 
Data from the UVision UScanner recorded on laptop.  
Videos showing how well the BlueROV2 has been calibrated with regards to buoyancy. 
Miscellaneous: 
NIL 
After Action Review 
Weather conditions: 
Controlled in ASTA. Bit cold. 
Successes: 
We successfully found a good amount of foam to use on the sensor payload, and where to 
place it on the payload. Furthermore, we mounted the cable securely to the payload, making 
sure we protect the plug to the sensor. We also connected the sensor, so we could address how 
far forward the sensor should be place, before any payload or foam would obstruct some of 
the view of the sensor. 
Complications: 
Due to constraints only being one person, we changed our approach to simply adjust the 
amount of foam needed for the payload alone by simply redeploying it over and over again 
with small changes. 
We have still not tried mounting it on the ROV to see how well it [loats together. We will rerun 
the experiment again to complete it.

E.5
Experimental Plan for Initial Deployment
The following document shows the experimental setup, necessary revisions, outcomes,
and reflections for the first deployment in Sydhavnen, utilizing a dual sonar configuration
mounted on a pole, as well as the ROV.
244
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:273==
Project: 
Collaborative SLAM for Maritime Robotics 
Aim: 
To collect data from Sydhavnen using a pole and BlueROV2 with 
different sensor payloads. 
Brief Description: 
This is our main data campaign. In this experiment, we will deploy two 
different setups – one pole and a ROV referred to as “BlueROV2”. On 
the pole, multiple sensors are mounted along with a payload box 
(Pelicase) that runs the sensors. The BlueROV2 will apart from its on-
board sensor be deployed with a UVision UScanner stereo camera and 
an USBL transceiver for localization tracking.  
Point B will be our main station.

requirements: 
See the document: Field Experiment Checklist

==PAGE:274==
General Setup 
Before department of Lyngby Campus, make sure that you have everything on the Field 
Experiment Checklist. Drive the car to point A on the map. 
1. Get everything out of the car 
2. Everything is transported from the van to point B.  
3. Mount the sonars on the dual sonar mount by doing the following 
a. Find the mount and make sure the two sensor-mounts are forward-facing the 
same direction like in the below picture 
 
b. First, we will mount the oculus. Take the oculus cable and push it through the 
hole of the black oculus mount like in the picture: 
 
c. Connect the cable to the Oculus sonar. Make sure it is finger-tight  
 
d. Make sure the screws on the side of the black mount a loose. Push the Oculus 
sensor into the black mount. Make sure it is pushed as far in as possible. From 
the back of the mount, you should be able to see the screw from the zink object 
(marked on the second picture). 
246
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:275==
 
e. Tighten the screws on each side of the black mount using an Allen Wrench size 3.  
 
f. 
Mount the Teledyne Blueview sensor. 
 
g. Make sure the sensor is rotated correctly. We wish to orient it perpendicular to 
the Oculus, i.e. we rotate it 90 degrees clockwise. The logo indicates the top of 
the sensor. Therefore, the logo should be on the right side of the mount. You can 
align the black square whole with the gap of the mount. Look at image below for 
example: 
 
h. Tight all 8 screws, 4 on each side 
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
247
==PAGE:276==
 
i. 
Mount the Blueview connector: 
 
4. Mount the red transponder on the aluminum pole using the 3D printed mount. Make 
sure the security wire from the transponder is connected to the pole as well. 
5. Push in the red button on the transponder. This keeps the pressure and is essential to 
keep the transponder watertight. 
6. Assemble the pole setup 
a. Mount the sonars by screwing the pole together with the dual senor mount.  
b. Mount the IMU on the pole using the provided mount. Note down where it is 
placed relatively to the USBL transponder. 
7. Connect all the sensors to the sensor fusion box 
a. Drag the Blueview Ethernet cable through the box hole and connect it in the 
lower connector, shown at in the top right of the image.  
b. Drag the Oculus Ethernet cable through the box hole and connect it in the higher 
connector, shown at in the top right of the image. 
c. Drag the USB cable from the IMU through the hole and connect it in the into the 
available USB 3.0 (Blue) USB A port of the Raspberry Pi labelled “Listener” 
d. Zip tie the different cables together from the payload box.  
248
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:277==
 
8. Power the sensor payload via the AC cable from the box to an AC power bank. (TODO: 
insert updated image of payload box)

Mission Description: Sonars on Pole 
Set up  
1. Follow the SOP for the Sensor Fusion Payload under the section “Data Collection” 
Experiment sequence: 
1. Deploy sensor pole into the water.  
2. Using an OOI to perform a batch test to find suitable parameters for both sensors 
(gain, range etc.).  
3. Survey the area around B to capture the geometry of the surrounding canal.  
o Might need to move it around at different depth levels, such that most of the 
seabed is captured. 
4. Sequentially deploy OOI’s close to the shoreline such that the pole setup can capture 
the geometry from multiple sides.  
Data collection: 
We wish to collect data in Sydhavnen which is surrounded by many buildings and is an 
area where the accuracy of GNSS data varies a lot. The pole will have the following 
sensor units attached: 
- 
9-axis IMU data 
- 
Orthogonal dual FLS setup.  
- 
USBL transceiver. 
The surveys will result in three different continuous data streams, each collected in a MCAP 
ROS bag.  
Post experiment 
1. If possible, let the OOI stay in the water. You will need it for the BlueROV survey. 
2. Power off the system by disconnecting the power bank.  
3. Pull up pole and dry of sensor with a towel.  
4. Dismount Oculus sonar, since Yu Fan needs it for his deployment at point “b”.  
5. Dismount sensor and pack everything in is respective boxes 
Miscellaneous: 
NIL 
 
Mission Description: BlueROV Survey 
Set up  
1. Go to point B on the map 
2. Jonas and Daniel will follow the hardware guide (BlueROV2 Heavy Blue2 Guide) for 
the BlueROV and mount the USensor payload (if not premounted).  Chrisitan will 
power on the laptop and start QGroundControl and UController. 
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
253
==PAGE:282==
3. Make sure the laptop is connected to AC as it has a poor battery  
4. Make sure you can control the ROV and can read data through the UController 
5. Check the ROS topics of the BlueROV by echoing into each one of them 
6. Start recording using “INSERT COMMAND HERE” 
7. Deploy the BlueROV by lowering it into the water. Be careful with the short tether 
(limited by the UVision sensor) 
Controlling the BlueROV 
For reference, here is the controller manual:  
 
Note: Forward-Reversal and Ascend-descend are switched around for some reason 
Experiment sequence: 
8. Make sure the two OOI’s are still deployed 
9. Deploy the ROV at point B 
10. Investigate the OOI “b” 
11. Investigate the OOI “a”  
12. Afterwards, pull the two OOI’s out of the water.  
13. Survey the area of where OOI “b” was, followed by OOI “a”. 
14. Return to deployment point “B” 
15. Follow the “Recovery” steps of the BlueROV2 Heavy Blue2 Guide. 
a. Manoeuvre the ROV to the recovery point. 
b. Disarm the motors. 
c. Shutdown the ROV software. 
d. Pull the tether until the frame is exposed. Grab the frame. 
e. With two people, lift the ROV onto the deck. Make sure to not pull on the cables 
penetrating the ROV. Try not to bump the multibeam enclosure against the hull / 
walls. 
16.   Follow the “Powering off” steps of the BlueROV2 Heavy Blue2 Guide. 
a. Dry off the battery enclosure cap. 
254
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:283==
b. Open the battery enclosure as in the powering on section. 
c. Remove the battery (it can be hot to the touch). 
d. Disconnect the battery by pulling on the yellow plugs only. Do not pull on the 
wires. 
e. Replace the battery cap and ventilation cap. 
Data collection: 
We wish to collect data of the harbor area, the seabed and the objects of interest. The 
BlueROV has a pressure sensor and inertial measurement unit onboard that will be used for 
data collection along with the UVision stereo camera. There is also an onboard camera which 
we will primarily use for orientation purposes. 
Post experiment

After Action Review 
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
255
==PAGE:284==
Weather conditions: 
Mostly sunny with few periods of clouds. A bit breezy. 
 
 
 
From: meteostat.net  
Successes: 
- 
The sensors on pole setup was successfully assembled, with all the relevant data being 
logged.  
- 
The ROV was successfully deployed, surveying the opposite wall and OOIs.  
- 
The OOIs were captured with both the sonars and the ROV/UVision setups.  
- 
Disassembling was successful and all equipment was postprocessed and stored securely.  
 
Complications: 
- 
The cable for the IMU was too short for this setup, since the payload box needed to be 
carried directly besides the pole. This was troublesome and not idle having the box carried 
so close to the waterfront. If another deployment is needed with this setup, a USB extender 
will be used.  
- 
Due to the improvised nature the of the pole setup, assembling took longer than anticipated.  
- 
Cables from the payload was not secured against sudden drags. 
- 
We were missing a m3 key in our toolbox, lucky a colleague had brought one along.  
- 
We were missing an angle-measurement tool to properly set the mount for the dual FLS. 
Instead, the angle was determined drawing triangles using a measuring tape.  
256
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:285==
- 
The distance measurement between the FLS mount and the IMU on the pole, might be a 
few centimetres of, since the disassembling began before the measurements were taken. 
However, due to markings the uncertainty should be low.  
- 
The 3D printed threads inside the pole didn’t stop rotation of the FLS mount, again 
introducing uncertainty with the relative position wrt the IMU.  
- 
The Uvision camera lenses seemed to be misted during the first part of the ROV 
deployment. For future use might need to put it in the water some time before mission start. 
- 
 An additional power socket would have eased the setup.  
- 
The data was saved in different computers. An optimal design would save everything into a 
single rosbag.  
- 
We lacked to define CRP and reference of direction beforehand on the USBL, making the 
relative distances of tranceiver and GNSS receiver inaccurate.  
- 
The mount of the USBL was very hacky and not very stable. 
- 
Logging of USBL data through the commsviewer is not stable, nor optimal. We think 
there’s a risk that it closed before end of BlueROV deployment. 
- 
Compass and gyroscope recalibrated automatically now and then, resulting in interruption 
of data collection.  
Reflections: 
Due to one of the motors on the Otter being broken, last-minute changes to the sonar 
experiment setup were needed. This resulted in a bit of a “hacky” solution with placing the 
dual FLS setup at the end of a long pole. Therefore, some of the solutions bore the mark of 
being improved during the deployment. However, combining the teachings that this 
deployment gave + plus having the pole equipment fastened on the Otter should alleviate 
these concerns.

==PAGE:290==
Master Thesis 
Experiment Plan 
12/01/2025 
Page 5 of 5 
Experiment sequence: 
1. Adjust the sonars to look straight forward 
2. Start recording on the optitrack system. 
3. Make sure the rosbags are being recorded of the sonars. 
4. Slowly rotate the sonars 45 degrees to the left from center 
5. Slowly rotate the sonars back to center and let it settle for a few seconds 
6. Repeat 3 and 4 to the right. 
7. Record another 30 seconds while letting the sonars stay centered. 
8. Stop recording on both sonars and optitrack. Validate the data 
9. Turn of the Teledyne BlueView by unplugging the cable. Record a rosbag consisting of 
the Oculus data for at least 30 seconds. 
10. Turn off the Oculus. Plug in the Blueview to the PC with proviewer software and make 
a 30 second recording. 
11. Save the recording. Plug the BlueView back into the payload system and make a 
separate recording of the Blueview using ROS for at least 30 seconds. Save the bag. 
12. Repeat step 2-11 having the sonar at a 45 degree tilt angle 
13. At last, make a “free hand” run where you walk around with the pole and use the 
optitrack to track it. Remember to use a colleague to manage the wires while walking. 
After Action Review 
Weather conditions: 
Controlled in ASTA. Cold. 
Successes: 
- 
The needed data was collected 
- 
No thermometer available for pool temperature. Ended up using Oculus’ calculated VoS 
under water. ~1436 m/s 
- 
What we thought earlier was interference between sonars seems to be too high gain on the 
BlueView 
Complications: 
- 
Experiment took longer than expected, totalling about 6 hours due to on/off follow up on 
collected data 
- 
We had to disassemble the setup when changing to 0 deg angle as the gimble was not 
mounted correctly adjust it to this position (arm in the way). Markers was placed before 
disassembling in order to mount it the same place again as precise as possible.

1 OVERVIEW 
Experiment Title: 
Otter and BlueROV Sydhavn Deployment 
Date/Time: 
Monday the 2nd of December 2024 at 8 AM on DTU Lyngby Campus 
Location: 
Sydhavnen, Belvederekanalen. 
https://maps.app.goo.gl/XCeHqyG62S9ZhCi27  
Project: 
Collaborative SLAM for Maritime Robotics 
Aim: 
To collect data from Sydhavnen using the Otter USV and BlueROV2 with 
different sensor payloads. 
Brief Description: 
This is the second data campaign, incorporating learnings from the first, 
conducted October 15th. In this experiment, we will deploy two different 
robots – one USV called the “Otter” and a ROV referred to as 
“BlueROV2”. On the Otter, multiple sensors are mounted along with a 
payload box “Sensor Fusion Recording Interface” constructed in a  
pelicase that runs the sensors. The BlueROV2 will, apart from its on-board 
sensor, be deployed with a UVision UScanner stereo camera and an USBL 
transceiver for localization tracking.  
The Otter will be deployed into the water at a kayak/canoe dock at point A. 
We will drag it to point B, where we will start the data collection 
campaign. The ROV will be deployed from this point as well, making 
Point B will be our main station.

3.2 ASSEMBLING THE ALUMINIUM POLE 
1. Mount the red transponder on the aluminum pole using the 3D printed mount. Make 
sure the security wire from the transponder is connected to the pole as well.

picture

4 MISSION DESCRIPTION: OTTER SURVEY 
1. Follow the SOP for the Sensor Fusion Payload under the section “Data Collection” 
2. Start data collection of the Otter.  
3. Survey the area between point B and C and back to point B. Make sure to pass the 
bridge before returning. 
4. Deploy the two objects of interest marked with green on the map 
a. A rope will be attached to the OOI and must be fastened to a suitable point 
on the bridge. 
b. “Yu Fan’s OOI” is deployed at the quay. 
5. Survey the area between point B and point C. Make sure to get some good readings 
from the sonar data on both OOI’s. 
6. Continue controlling the Otter down the channel, following the purple points in the 
channel in ascending order, starting from 1 and ending at 9. 
4.1 DATA COLLECTION 
We wish to collect data in Sydhavnen which is surrounded by many buildings and is an 
area where the accuracy of GNSS data varies a lot. The Otter will have sensor units 
abroad which are able to capture: 
- 
2D and 3D below water sonar data 
- 
3D lidar point clouds above water 
- 
GNSS 
- 
9-axis IMU data 
- 
Camera for reference 
- 
RTK high precession localization for reference  
We wish to collect as much continuous data as possible within all these modalities, 
following the route described above. Furthermore, we wish to collect data on the OOI. 
 
To start collecting data from the sensors mounted on the Otter USV, perform the 
following steps: 
a. Ensure that the HP laptop is connected to the payload box over the otter radio, 
being routed through the Dell Rugged laptop. 
b. Enter into the “otter-usv/ansible_scripts” folder. The “otter-usv” is located in the 
home directory. 
c. To start data collection on all connected sensors, run the ansible playbook 
“main_launch_playbook.yml” by running command: “ansible-playbook 
main_launch_playbook.yml”. 
d. When data collection is finished, close the data collection process gracefully by 
running the “main_close_playbook.yml”. 
e. To shutdown the entire system, run the “shutdown_all_pi.yml” playbook, located 
in the “shutdown” folder.  
278
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:307==
Otter and BlueROV Sydhavn Deployment                      Christian W., Daniel H. and Jonas P. 
Autonomous Systems Msc. Thesis 
 
28/11/2024 
 
To start collecting data from the ROV, perform the following steps: 
a. Ensure an active connection to the ROV is made through the interface. 
b. Enter the “bluerov_listener” folder in the home directory on the HP laptop. 
c. Run “docker compose up”. Data collection from the ROV and the Uvision 
Uscanner will commence immediately. 
a. To stop this, run the ROV for one minute longer than needed (to ensure 
that all important data is bagged and saved before the container is shut 
down) and run docker compose down. 
4.2 POST EXPERIMENT 
1. If possible, let the OOI stay in the water. You will need it for the BlueROV survey. 
2. Return the Otter to point B 
3. Make sure it is disarmed and tow it two the mole. We can let it be in the water 
until after BlueROV Experiment.

5 MISSION DESCRIPTION: BLUEROV SURVEY 
5.1 SETUP 
1. Go to point B on the map 
2. Jonas and Daniel will follow the hardware guide (BlueROV2 Heavy Blue2 Guide) 
for the BlueROV and mount the USensor payload (if not premounted).  Chrisitan 
will power on the laptop and start QGroundControl and the bluerov_listener 
Docker image. 
3. Make sure the laptop is connected to AC as it has a poor battery  
4. Make sure you can control the ROV and can read data through the UController 
5. Check the ROS topics of the BlueROV by echoing into each one of them 
6. Start recording using “INSERT COMMAND HERE” 
7. Deploy the BlueROV by lowering it into the water. Be careful with the short 
tether (limited by the UVision sensor) 
5.2 CONTROLS 
For reference, here is the controller manual: 
 
5.3 EXPERIMENT SEQUENCE 
1. Make sure the two OOI’s are still deployed 
2. Deploy the ROV at point B 
3. Investigate the OOI “b” 
4. Investigate the OOI “a” 
5. Continue controlling the ROV down the channel in the following order marked 
on the map: 1 → 2 → 5 → 6 → 7 → 8. When scanning seabed, try to stay 
perpendicular to the surface with the camera 
6. When you have arrived at point 8, pull the two OOI’s out of the water.  
280
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
==PAGE:309==
Otter and BlueROV Sydhavn Deployment                      Christian W., Daniel H. and Jonas P. 
Autonomous Systems Msc. Thesis 
 
28/11/2024 
7. Survey the area of where OOI “b” was, followed by OOI “a”. 
8. Return to deployment point “B” 
9. Follow the “Recovery” steps of the BlueROV2 Heavy Blue2 Guide. 
a. Manoeuvre the ROV to the recovery point. 
b. Disarm the motors. 
c. Shutdown the ROV software. 
d. Pull the tether until the frame is exposed. Grab the frame. 
e. With two people, lift the ROV onto the deck. Make sure to not pull on the 
cables penetrating the ROV. Try not to bump the multibeam enclosure against 
the hull / walls. 
10. Follow the “Powering off” steps of the BlueROV2 Heavy Blue2 Guide. 
a. Dry off the battery enclosure cap. 
b. Open the battery enclosure as in the powering on section. 
c. Remove the battery (it can be hot to the touch). 
d. Disconnect the battery by pulling on the yellow plugs only. Do not pull on 
the wires. 
e. Replace the battery cap and ventilation cap. 
5.4 DATA COLLECTION 
We wish to collect data of the harbor area, the seabed and the objects of interest. The 
BlueROV has a pressure sensor and inertial measurement unit onboard that will be used 
for data collection along with the UVision stereo camera. There is also an onboard 
camera which we will primarily use for orientation purposes.

==PAGE:323==
F
Appendices for 5: USV SLAM
F.1
Morphological Operations on Horizontal SONAR images
Figure F.1: Opening and erosion filters with different kernels applied on the same image.
Using a simple erode operation tends to thin out the features of the surface, risking that
the edge ”bleeds” into the surface. However, it does not seem to be a huge issue when the
kernel is small (3 × 1). Using a too-large filter, for instance, the 5 × 1 removes a lot of
noise but is simply too aggressive resulting in bad results for edge detection.
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
295
==PAGE:324==
Figure F.2: Opening and erosion filters with different kernels applied on the same image.

F.2
Strided version of rolling MAD
Algorithm 3 Rolling MAD on Profile (Strided Version)
Require: x is a 1D array
Require: W integer, size of the window. Must be uneven.
Require: threshold. Default value is 2.5
Require: b, distribution constant. Default 1.4826
Ensure: v for valid indices, u for upper bound, l for lower bound.
1: n ←length of x
2: Whalf ←⌊W/2⌋
3: ”Edge” pad x with Whalf values on each side of x
4: Construct rolling window matrix Xwindow
5: ˜xrolling ←median(Xwindow, axis = 1)
6: madrolling ←b · median(|Xwindow −˜xrolling|, axis = 1)
7: u ←˜xrolling + (madrolling × threshold)
8: l ←˜xrolling −(madrolling × threshold)
9: v ←(x ≥l) ∧(x ≤u)
10: return v, u, l
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

G
Appendices for 7: Object Detection
G.1
Canal Generation
Algorithm 4 3D Canal Generation with Random Objects (Part I)
1: function EulerToRotationMatrix(α, β, γ)
2:
Compute Rx, Ry, Rz
3:
return R ←Rz · Ry · Rx
4: end function
5: function GenerateCanal(npoints)
6:
Randomize L, W, H
7:
Divide points: nbottom, nside
8:
// Generate bottom surface
9:
Sample x, y; compute z ←Seabed(x, y)
10:
// Generate walls and add noise
11:
return Canal point cloud
12: end function
13: function SampleCoordinate
14:
Sample x, y within canal limits
15:
Compute zmin ←Seabed(x, y)
16:
Sample z ∈[zmin, H]
17:
return (x, y, z)
18: end function
19: function InsertObject(nobj_points)
20:
Choose random object type and parameters
21:
center ←SampleCoordinate
22:
Generate object points and add noise
23:
Rotate object using EulerToRotationMatrix(...)
24:
Filter object points within canal bounds
25:
if object not empty then
26:
Create bounding boxes
27:
Add object points to canal point cloud
28:
end if
29: end function
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure
307
==PAGE:336==
Algorithm 4 3D Canal Generation with Random Objects (Part II)
32: function GenerateScenes(nscenes, nmin, nmax, npt_min, npt_max)
33:
GenerateCanal
34:
for i = 1 to nscenes do
35:
Choose nobjects ∈[nmin, nmax]
36:
Choose nobj_points ∈[npt_min, npt_max]
37:
for each object in nobjects do
38:
InsertObject(nobj_points)
39:
end for
40:
if Total points > maximum then
41:
Downsample canal point cloud
42:
end if
43:
Save point cloud and bounding boxes
44:
Reset canal state
45:
end for
46: end function

H.1
Retinex Implementation Code
Implementation taken from this GitHub page.
1 import cv2
2 import numpy as np
3
4 def singleScaleRetinex(img,variance):
5
retinex = np.log10(img) - np.log10(cv2.GaussianBlur(img, (0, 0), variance)
)
6
return retinex
7
8 def SSR(img, variance):
9
img = np.float64(img) + 1.0
10
img_retinex = singleScaleRetinex(img, variance)
11
for i in range(img_retinex.shape[2]):
12
unique, count = np.unique(np.int32(img_retinex[:, :, i] * 100),
return_counts=True)
13
for u, c in zip(unique, count):
14
if u == 0:
15
zero_count = c
16
break
17
low_val = unique[0] / 100.0
18
high_val = unique[-1] / 100.0
19
for u, c in zip(unique, count):
20
if u < 0 and c < zero_count * 0.1:
21
low_val = u / 100.0
22
if u > 0 and c < zero_count * 0.1:
23
high_val = u / 100.0
24
break
25
img_retinex[:, :, i] = np.maximum(np.minimum(img_retinex[:, :, i],
high_val), low_val)
26
27
img_retinex[:, :, i] = (img_retinex[:, :, i] - np.min(img_retinex[:,
:, i])) / \
28
(np.max(img_retinex[:, :, i]) - np.min(
img_retinex[:, :, i])) \
29
* 255
30
img_retinex = np.uint8(img_retinex)
31
return img_retinex
32
33 variance=500
34
35 img_ssr=SSR(cv_image , variance)

==PAGE:357==
I
RANSAC
The following section gives a brief explanation of the method behind the RAndom Sam-
pling Consensus (RANSAC) algorithm[159].
Firstly a plane in 3D space is defined as:
ax + by + cz + d = 0
(I.1)
Here (a, b, c) are the the components for the normal vector n while d is the offset from the
origin. For a given point the shortest distance to the plane is defined as:
dist(pi) = |axi + byi + czi + d|
√
a2 + b2 + c2
(I.2)
Here a point is consisted an inlier if the distance is below a chosen threshold dist(pi) ≤τ.
The plane parameters (a, b, c, d) are iteratively found from 3 randomly sampled non-
colinear points that are used to form the following vectors:
v1 = p2 −p1,
v2 = p3 −p1
(I.3)
Using the cross product between these vectors yields the normal vector for the plane that
v1 and v2 expands and thereby, the resulting plane parameters (a, b, c). Substituding any
one of the three sampled points (p1, p2, p3) into the plane equation (I.1) gives the final
plane parameters by:
d = −(ax + by + cz)
(I.4)
Now the question of how many iteration RANSAC need, before fitting a model to the
least amount of outlier points. Here ϵ is introduced that reflects the proportion of outlier
points, yielding the following formula:
N =
log(1 −p)
log ((1 −(1 −ϵ)n)
(I.5)
This can be expanded to an adaptive approach, where upper bound of ϵ is iteratively
updated. If s is the number of inliers in the best model found so far, and m is the total
number of data points, the estimated proportion is found by ˆϵ = 1 −
s
m.
Using this
RANSAC returns the approximated best model after having done ˆN iterations.
ˆN =
log(1 −p)
log (1 −(1 −ˆϵ)n) > N
(I.6)
Multi-agent Marine System for Autonomous Mapping of Critical Infrastructure

==PAGE:361==
Experiment Plan for  
Autonomous Marine Systems 
 
Person(s)-in-charge: 
Jonas Poulsen & Daniel Holmelund 
 
Overview 
Experiment Title: 
Deploying the BlueROV 2 into the pool in ASTA 
Date/Time: 
2nd October 2024, 2:30 PM 
Location: 
ASTA 
Project: 
Collaborative SLAM for Maritime Robotics 
Aim: 
To calibrate the buoyancy of the BlueROV2 with UVision payload along 
with gaining experience in deploying and using the QGroundControl 
for controlling the BlueROV2. 
Brief Description: 
We will be using the BlueROV2 with a UVision UScanner as payload, 
which potentially will change how the center of mass of the combined 
setup along with its total mass, reducing buoyancy. We need to add 
foam for adjustment. Furthermore, we wish to gather experience in 
controlling and deploying the robot before harbor experiment.  
 
Resources 
Hardware 
requirements: 
• 
BlueROV2 and its box with contents 
o Charged battery. 
• 
Waterproof laptop  
• 
Payload 
o UVision UScanner mounted. 
o Foam mounted to payload. 
• 
Payload mount brackets 
• 
Extra foam 
• 
Xbox Controller 
• 
Arla box as object of interest 
Software 
requirements: 
• 
Installed UVision UScanner software on waterproof laptop. 
• 
QGroundControl is installed 
 
K
Deploying the BlueROV 2 into the pool in
ASTA
The following document shows the experiment plan for the experiment that aimed to cali-
brate the buoyancy of the BlueROV2 with UVision payload, as well as gaining experience
in deploying and using the QGroundControl for controlling the BlueROV2.
